with it.
Gluing It Together with RSS
The hardware (mics and mixer) and the software (such as Audition or Audacity) 
are necessary to record audio and create a podcast media file. That’s the fun and 
creative part. But to make your recording a podcast, you need to get your hands 
dirty on the tedious and technical parts and add one more three-letter acronym to 
your vocabulary: RSS.
Since the first edition in 2005, we’ve helped a huge number of podcasters get 
started  — and in nearly every case, the RSS step is the biggest source of 
confusion.
RSS (Really Simple Syndication) is a file format built on XML (eXtensible Markup 
Language), which is a markup language like HTML (the building blocks of the 
internet). XML enables systems such as computers, network devices, and other 
gadgets to exchange information in a structured format. The RSS file is a bit of 
information that the podcaster publishes so others can use their podcatching 
software to check for new content automatically.CHAPTER 3 Building Your Podcast’s Digital Workstation 53
If that somewhat technical explanation doesn’t do it for you, try this old-school 
analogy about deliverable content. Consider journalists for publications such as 
The Washington Post, The New York Times, Vanity Fair, or Wired Magazine as authori￾ties on what’s happening in life. Whether the subject matter covers Apple com￾puters, the Boston Red Sox, or Taylor Swift, you know that these journalists are 
trusted experts in their field or maybe fellow fans.
Good journalists create new and unique content with every issue or publication. 
And to get that content, you can check the newsstand or subscribe and have the 
content delivered. In this example, media created by the podcaster replaces the 
magazine or newspaper, and RSS is the delivery mechanism for those that 
subscribe.
As a podcaster, your job is to make sure you keep the RSS updated and current 
each time you post a new podcast media file. Lucky for you (and us too), plenty of 
software solutions make this step a breeze.
If you’re looking to spend the least amount of time hand-coding an RSS feed, a 
blog is a great way to include information about each episode and the audio file 
itself. Easy to set up and often free, blogs make the process of generating and 
updating RSS feeds insanely easy by doing it automatically.
You can choose from dozens of blog software packages (also called engines), each 
with a variety of bells and whistles designed to make your updates (including your 
RSS feed) as easy and customizable as possible. For a crash course in how blogs do 
SPLITTING HAIRS: WHAT DEFINES 
A PODCAST
Some people think that if they post media on a blog or a website and add a podcast
graphic, they’re podcasting media. Others think that if they record a snappy show about 
a favorite topic and post it on YouTube, they have a podcast. Then there are the old￾timer podcasters who shake their fists at a passing cloud, screaming “If it doesn’t have 
RSS, it’s not a podcast!”
The tricky thing about this ongoing debate is that podcasting has evolved. Streaming 
content can be consumed on mobile devices, laptops, and desktop computers and then 
circulated through blogs, communication apps such as Discord, and various social 
media platforms. The lines that define a podcast are blurring. For the sake of this book, 
we define a podcast as a delivery of media from your studio to a consumer by using 
RSS. A podcast happens when you have these three elements in place: a website or 
blog, some form of media, and an accompanying RSS feed to deliver said media.54 PART 1 Podcasting on a Worldwide Frequency
what they do, check out Blogging For Dummies, 7th Edition, by Amy Lupold Bair. 
Her book can help you choose which blog engine might be right for you. Mean￾while, here are a few options we prefer:
» WordPress (https://pfdbook.link/wordpress): Perhaps the most popular 
blogging solution, WordPress has many advantages over other free blogs. Not 
only is it easy to install and get running, but it also supports podcasting out of 
the box. When you incorporate the PowerPress plug-in (https://pfdbook.
link/powerpress), you’re provided with a new level of user-friendly options. In 
addition, you can use thousands of WordPress and user-developed templates 
as-is or customize them to fit the look and feel for your podcast. And it’s free.

(https://pfdbook.link/godaddy) offer packages with WordPress preinstalled 
or installed with a one-click option. Figure 3-4 shows the WordPress signup page.
If you choose to go the WordPress route, pay particular attention to the 
web address. There’s Wordpress.org and Wordpress.com. The .org flavor 
IT’S ALL ABOUT THE <ENCLOSURE>, BABY
If you have a blog, you’re already generating an RSS feed. Although podcast apps can 
read your RSS feed, the feed needs to include the <enclosure> tag for podcasting 
to work.
Dave Winer invented the <enclosure> tag in early 2001 to embed in an RSS feed links 
to large audio, video, or other rich media elements. At the time, Dave and Adam Curry 
were trying to solve the click-and-wait problems inherent in big files such as audio and 
video. Back then, if a user clicked a link to a 30MB file, several minutes would drag by 
before the file was downloaded to the user’s hard drive and made available. Not a good 
user experience, regardless of what’s in the file.
With the advent of the <enclosure> RSS element, users could subscribe to places 
where they expected large files regularly and move the file downloading to the early 
hours, when they were snug in bed and a 10-minute download was no big deal.
However, users back then had to be technically savvy to take advantage of this new RSS 
element. It wasn’t until the summer of 2004 that Adam Curry wrote what most consider 
the first podcatching client — a simple, user-friendly desktop program that extracted 
enclosed media files from RSS 2.0 feeds. And behold! Podcasting was born.CHAPTER 3 Building Your Podcast’s Digital Workstation 55
is where you can download the software and install it on your server of 
choice — this option involves a bit of technical work. The .com site has the 
software already installed and you just configure it; however, the configura￾tion options are more limited.
You have two basic options regarding where to put your blog, and prices and 
products vary from vendor to vendor. In a nonhosted model blog, you get to 
choose the blog engine, a great option if you want to use a specific engine. 
However, setting up and maintaining the blog involves more work. The other 
option, the hosted model, keeps things simple and chooses the blog engine for 
you. The trade-off for flexibility is ease-of-use.
» LibSyn (https://pfdbook.link/libsyn): Don’t be surprised if LibSyn 
(Liberated Syndication) turns up a lot in this book. Still going strong after 
launching in 2004, LibSyn is a combined blog/hosting company designed for 
podcasting. Although it may not address all your needs, its ease of use and 
all-in-one nature should not be dismissed. Some podcasters use LibSyn as 
their hosted solution; others use it to store their podcast files while their blog 
is on a non-hosted solution somewhere else. Either way, we think LibSyn’s 
pricing is quite reasonable, starting at $5 per month.

When you have software and hardware in place, test your setup to make sure 
everything works. Take a look at your application’s preferences so that your sound 
input and output are going through your mixer, bring up the volume on the chan￾nel on the mixing board that your microphone is connected to, and then listen to 
yourself through your headphones. Just make sure everything is running as 
expected so you can jump right in and get recording.
Podcast Management 101
Unless you already have hosting taken care of, you’re going to need a place on the 
web to put your podcast media files, RSS feed, and show notes. You also need a 
way to get them there.
Finding hosting providers is a breeze, with hundreds of companies all vying for 
your precious, hard-earned money. The good news is that this competition has 
brought down the cost of hosting packages significantly. The bad news is that you 
have to go through a lot of clutter to reach the right selection.
This section covers the basic needs for most beginning podcasts and mentions 
a few pitfalls. In Chapter  10, we get into the process of moving your files to 
your host.
Don’t rush into a hosting agreement just yet. We suggest reading the rest of this 
chapter as well as Chapter 10 before forking over your credit card. We cover lots of 
good information that can help you narrow your choices.
When you’re comparing hosting plans, try not to get bogged down in the number 
of email addresses, MySQL databases, subdomains, and the like. As a podcaster, 
you have only two worries: how many podcasts the site can hold and how much 
bandwidth you get.CHAPTER 3 Building Your Podcast’s Digital Workstation 57
Size does matter
Podcast media files are big. Unlike bloggers, podcasters eat up server space. Where 
simple text files and a few images take up a relatively small space, podcast media 
files tend to be in the 5MB to 50MB range. And that’s just for audio.
Here some suggestions for zeroing in on what you need for storage space:
» Think about how many podcasts you want to keep online and plan accord￾ingly. However, if your plan has unlimited storage space, you don’t need to 
worry about it.
» Consider the amount of server space you’ll need to host your blogging 
software, databases, text, and image files. For example, if Tee wants to keep 
all episodes of The Shared Desk, The Ministry of Peculiar Occurrences, and Happy 
Hour from the Tower online, he will easily need several gigabytes of space, as 
one of these shows first went online in 2010.
Podcasters should look for hosting plans that include at least 3GB of storage space. 
As of this writing, several host providers charge less than $10 per month for at 
least that much space.
Bandwidth demystified
Of equal importance to storage space is bandwidth, an elusive and often￾misunderstood attribute of web hosting that is critical to podcasters. Bandwidth
refers to the online space needed to handle the amount of stuff you push out of 
your website every month. The bigger the files and the more requests for the files, 
the more bandwidth consumed.
For instance, the bandwidth for Chuck Tomasi’s Technorama can be over 100GB a 
month — that’s impressive. Why so huge? Chuck and his co-host Kreg just cele￾brated their 750th episode. They’ve been podcasting since the beginning. At their 
launch, the amount of information exchanged (read: downloads) and the demands 
on bandwidth were modest, but both have increased with the rise in the show’s 
popularity (thanks to top-shelf interviews with people in the science and enter￾tainment industries). In situations like this, web hosts must consider when it’s 
time to upgrade to a larger bandwidth package to handle a show — and that means 
more cash outlay.
And therein lies the double-edged sword of success. Most podcasters want more 
listeners, and that means more podcatching clients requesting the podcast media 
files. Bottom line: The more popular your show gets, the more bandwidth will be 
consumed every month.58 PART 1 Podcasting on a Worldwide Frequency
To simplify, pretend that you produce one show each week, and your show requires 
10MB of bandwidth. You publish the show on Monday, and your 100 subscribers 
receive your show that evening. You’ve just consumed 1000MB of bandwidth (100 ×
10MB) for that week. But next week, more people have found out about your amaz￾ing show, and now you have 200 subscribers. Next Monday, your bandwidth 
increases to 2000MB, which gets added to your previous week’s total to bring you 
up to 3000MB. The new listeners were so happy, they also download the previous 
week’s show, tacking on an extra 1000MB.  You’ve just consumed 4000MB (or 
4GB) of bandwidth for the month. You still have two weeks to go in the month, and 
if your numbers continue to climb like this, you’ll be burning through bandwidth 
(and your web host budget) quickly!
As a general rule, the longer your podcast episodes, the more bandwidth you’ll 
need. If you find a plan that offers unlimited bandwidth, the problem is solved. 
Otherwise, find a plan that’s high in the gigabytes. If you have a podcast that’s 
both long and popular, start looking at plans that offer 1TB (terabyte) or more.
You have ways to avoid the issue of bandwidth or at least make it less of a concern 
even if you have large ambitions. Chapter 10 talks about some podcast-specific 
and advanced hosting options. Even if you don’t think you’ll have to worry about 
bandwidth, pay close attention to this topic because you’ll likely be more popular 
and perhaps wordier than you think.CHAPTER 4 Go, Go, Power Podcasters! 59

Up to this point, you’ve been building your podcast studio with a home

When you work remotely, test your setup to make sure everything works. Look at 
your system settings to ensure that you’re working with the proper sound input 
and output sources, check that your levels are solid, and run a quick sound check 
through your headphones. Then, when you know that everything is running as 
expected, you can jump right in and start recording.

Podcasting with Your Laptop
In Chapter 2 we talk about creating a simple podcast rig by connecting a USB mic 
to your laptop. That’s a good start, but if you want to create a podcast with studio￾quality sound and you have more than one microphone and want some control over 
the levels, it’s time to invest in a preamplifier, or preamp for short. To understand 
what a preamp is, you need to learn a few technical details about microphones.
Microphones, be they condenser or dynamic, record their signals at mic level,
which is the signal created when the internal diaphragm moves back and forth 
against a magnet in a wire coil, generating an electrical signal. It’s a clean signal 
but very weak. The best audio is recorded not at mic level but at line level. Line￾level is the strength of an audio signal used to transmit analog audio between 
components such as CD and DVD players, television sets, audio amplifiers, and 
mixing consoles. To boost a weaker mic-level signal to the line-level signal, you 
need to give it a swift kick in the pants. The preamp, either a separate unit (that 
connects back to your laptop) or built into a mixer board or a microphone, pro￾vides that kick without adding noise to the original signal.
Now that you know what a preamp is, how about a few options to consider?
Shure MVi
Shure, a name we introduce in Chapter 2, is no stranger to setting the bar for 
audio engineering and recording. With podcasting now an established platform, 
Shure found it natural to create gear that captures quality sound. Their MVi (shown 
in Figure 4-1) is a compact, USB-powered preamp ready to power your micro￾phone (or microphones, if you employ a splitter) accordingly.

The MVi offers a podcaster-on-the-go the following:

volume, and more

The Shure MVi can serve as a preamp, powered by USB. With a compact design, 
the MVi makes your portable studio even more so.
If you want to record on location, you could use a studio condenser mic, which due 
to its sensitivity will pick up a lot of the background, setting a nice ambiance for 
your podcast. Depending on your environment, though, there might be too much 
ambiance, which is why dynamic mics are preferred for most on-location set￾tings. You’ll still get some background noise, but it won’t be as pronounced as 
when using a studio condenser mic.

Podcasting with Your Mobile Devices
With cloud computing becoming more prevalent, mobile devices — smartphones 
such as iPhone and Samsung Galaxy and tablets such as iPad and Surface — are 
quickly becoming the new alternative to portable computing. Mobile devices have 
reduced portability to a single pocket and a connection to the internet.
Now audio gear is being designed to provide the portability and connectivity you 
expect from smartphones and tablets. Why smartphones? Particularly when con￾ducting interviews where sitting isn’t an option (such as a press conference or a 
person-on-the-street interview), you need your recording rig to be even more 
compact than a tablet. This is where your smartphone transforms faster than an 
Autobot (or a Decepticon, depending on your Transformer of choice) to become 
your recording studio on the fly.
Most mobile phones include a recording app, such as Voice Memos for iOS or Voice 
Recorder for Android. These should provide you with the basics for capturing 
audio on-the-go. However, you might want to look in your mobile app store for 
other apps for features to improve quality and workflow and provide a better user 
experience.CHAPTER 4 Go, Go, Power Podcasters! 63
RØDE VideoMic Me
The RØDE VideoMic Me series, priced between $59 and $79, upgrades your on￾board smartphone microphone to higher-quality audio. The Video Mic Me-L is 
captured in action in Figure 4-3.
Instead of that tinny quality found in most phone conversations and smartphone 
recordings, the VideoMic Me picks up a far wider range and frequency, while 
focusing the direction of the mic, yielding audio of a much higher quality. The 
accessory also comes with an optional mount for better mic stability, an audio jack 
for real-time monitoring with headphones, and a windscreen to cut down on any 
weather elements you might encounter.
The various models are designed for connecting to the different devices as follows:
» VideoMic Me-L: Lightning connector for Apple devices
» VideoMic Me-C: UBS-C connector for mobile devices
» VideoMic Me: 3.5mm audio connector for mobile devices
In turning smartphones into portable recording devices, you may find your 
recording riddled with intermittent static called RFI, or radio frequency interference.
RFI occurs when a disturbance in frequency is generated by an external source — 
for example, other nearby electronic equipment — causing signal degradation.

smartLav+

The smartLav+ connects directly into a smartphone or tablet headset jack and

» High-quality omnidirectional condenser mic design
» Standard frequency range between 20 Hz and 20 kHz
Many modern smartphones are doing away with headphone jacks, so you might 
need an adapter to make accessories like the smartLav+ with its 1⁄8-inch (3.5mm) 
jack operate properly. Before purchasing any audio recording gear for your smart￾phone, check out how the accessory connects to your smartphone model.
Two for the Røde: The Wireless PRO
Lavalier, or lav, mics such as smartLav+ (described in the preceding section) are 
appealing because you don’t have the pressure of recording in front of a visible 
mic, you no longer need to project your voice, and your hands are free for presen￾tations (in case you add a video component to your podcast). The only downside 
to working with a lav mic are those annoying cables. Watch early episodes of CHAPTER 4 Go, Go, Power Podcasters! 65
Jack Osborne and Katrina Weidman’s Portals to Hell, and you’ll see producers feed￾ing mics and their respective cables underneath people’s clothing, which makes 
for awkward moments of closeness.
Wireless microphones have made serious advancements, and RØDE’s Wireless PRO, 
shown in Figure 4-4, is an upgrade that will level up your podcast production. 
Unlike other lavalier mics that are connected to a receiver base station or a record￾ing device, the Wireless PRO mic connects to a small receiver via Bluetooth. After 
pairing the transmitter(s) and receiver, you can set levels, capture audio (as well 
as a backup recording) on your desired mobile device or video camera, and use 
either the transmitters or included lavalier mic as your wireless microphone.
Let’s run through the setup of a pair of Wireless PROs, connecting them to a 
smartphone:
1. Unpack the two Wireless PRO cases. Find the case that has your 
transmitters and the receiver.
The case will have a USB-C plug in the back. This case doubles as a charger for 
the two transmitters and one receiver; the devices work up to seven hours 
when fully charged.
2. Remove the receiver from its far-right slot in the charging case, and then 
remove the first transmitter from its far-left slot in the charging case.
Once removed from the case, the transmitter and the receiver should pair up 
immediately, as shown in Figure 4-5.
FIGURE 4-4:

3. If you have a guest or a co-host and want to use a separate microphone 
for their audio, remove the second transmitter.
The second transmitter will sync with the receiver automatically.
4. Using a USB-C-to-USB-C cable, connect the receiver to your smartphone.
Make sure the USB-C cable is optimized for fast data transfer. The cables that 
come with the RØDE Wireless PRO are built for high-speed transfer, but you 
can find this type of cable wherever tech accessories are sold.
5. Clip your transmitter on your shirt, a comfortable distance from your 
mouth. Have your guest do the same if you’re using two transmitters.
6. Connect the receiver to your mobile device with the cables provided per 
RØDE’s instructions.
Make sure the receiver is secured to your device — not flopping around on the

RØDE Wireless PRO receiver to your mobile device (again, wherever tech 
accessories are sold).
7. Launch your phone’s recording app and start recording.
Alternatively, you can use the RØDE Capture app for recording.
And that’s it! You’re working wirelessly with your smartphone.

Working wirelessly with a podcast can open up options for you and your co-host, 
but what if your podcast is more of an interview type of production? Again, RØDE 
is here to help. If you would prefer not to get awkward with your interview subject 
or continuously pause your recording to record random subjects on location, pick 
up RØDE Interview Go, an adapter for your second Wireless PRO mic that turns it 
into a handheld microphone.
The setup for the Interview Go is easy:
1. Follow Steps 1 — 3 of the preceding exercise to begin the setup of your 
Wireless PRO mics.
2. Clip the first transmitter somewhere on your shirt, a comfortable 
distance from your mouth.
3. Plug the second transmitter into the end of RØDE Interview Go, and cover 
the transmitter with the windscreen.
4. Launch your phone’s recording app and start recording.
After a quick sound check, you are now ready for your interview. Good luck!

Portable recorders have established themselves as reliable, durable, and afford￾able options for field and on-location recording. Depending on what you need for 
your podcast, you no longer have to worry about remaining tethered to a studio. 
Portable recorders can make your podcast happen just about anywhere!
Before recording, always check that you have enough storage on your recording 
device. Most portable recorders have an indicator that shows how many hours or 
minutes of storage remain. For mobile phones, go to the system settings and 
ensure that the phone has approximately 1MB available for each minute of audio 
you want to record (much more for video). Chapter 9 gets into the nitty-gritty 
about settings and storage considerations.
As noted, RFI can be a downer when recording. Even with portable recorders, 
make sure that the smartphone is at least 10 feet (or 3 meters) from your record￾ing device. Radio, data, and cellular signals can leave a telltale buzzing or beeping 
noise and ruin your recording.70 PART 1 Podcasting on a Worldwide Frequency

Getting audio from your portable recorder
After you finish recording with your portable recorder, the audio is sitting in your 
recorder’s internal memory card. How do you get it from the recorder to the com￾puter where you’ll be editing the podcast?
You’ve already done the hard part: getting great audio for your podcast. Getting 
the audio to the computer? In the words of brave, brave, brave, brave Sir Robin at 
the Bridge of Death: “That’s easy!” Do the following for Zoom devices (the process 
for other devices will be similar):
1. Connect the portable recorder to an available USB port.
Recorders should have an included USB cable that physically attaches the 
device to your computer, as shown in Figure 4-7. If you lack such a connecting 
cable, your local electronics store should have what you need.
Recent computers are transitioning from USB to USB-C ports. Adapters can 
convert standard USB plugs to connect with USB-C ports, or provide additional 
ports for other devices such as HDMI and native USB-C devices.
FIGURE 4-7:
Portable 
recorders, when 
connected to 
your computer 
via USB, appear 
on your desktop 
as an external 
drive.72 PART 1 Podcasting on a Worldwide Frequency
2. Choose Storage ➪ SD Card Reader, or an option indicating the device’s 
storage device.
Typically, the device can be set to one of two modes when connected to 
USB. One mode gives you access to the device’s storage (for example, SD card) 
and other mode is for using your portable recorder as a USB microphone or 
preamp for your computer. In storage mode, the portable recorder (and the 
SD card inside it) mounts to your desktop as an external drive.
Your system will present the portable player as a new drive available to your 
computer.
3. Once your portable player is available, select your new drive and then 
select the folder where your recordings are stored (such as FOLDER01).
Depending on the model of portable recorder, you may see folders labeled 
based on the device’s recording modes. Use that as your guide to locating 
your audio files. Most recorders save the files with a sequential number 
(ZOOM0001.WAV, ZOOM0002.WAV, and so on) or with a date and time stamp 
(such as REC20240401_132546.WAV).
4. Drag your latest recording (the highest number or latest dated filename) 
to the location on your computer’s hard drive where you store your audio 
sessions.
Whether you’re working with an internal drive, an external drive, or cloud 
storage, you’ve copied your audio file source from the recorder to your 
workspace. You can now begin the post-production process.
It’s usually quicker to transfer files from your portable recorder to your computer 
directly rather than use USB. If your computer has an SD card reader, you can 
remove the SD card from your portable recorder and place it in the SD card reader 
for faster copying. Just make sure you properly eject the media before pulling the 
SD card out of the computer and returning it to your portable recorder!
Getting audio from your mobile device
The process of getting audio from a smartphone or tablet is a little different than 
getting audio from a portable recorder. Instead of a hard drive or an SD card, 
smartphones and tablets have internal flash memory that will quickly fill up if 
you’re saving audio or video files directly on the device. To work with audio 
recorded on a mobile device, you first need to transfer the audio to a cloud storage 
service.CHAPTER 4 Go, Go, Power Podcasters! 73
The workflow we have created here is built around the following services, soft￾ware, and hardware:
» Cloud Service: Dropbox
» Portable device: iPad
» Audio recorder: GarageBand for iOS
If you are using other devices or services, it should be easy to incorporate your 
own setup and adapt it for this easy-to-follow workflow:
1. Before transferring audio, check to make sure that your cloud service’s 
app is loaded, updated, and synced with your mobile device.
2. Launch GarageBand for iOS.
If you’ve been working on a project, GarageBand for iOS displays the last 
project you were working on or defaults to an audio recording interface.
3. If you’re in a project, tap the save file icon in the top-left corner (and 
shown in the margin).
4. Tap and hold down on the desired project that has your audio ready for 
export and then tap Share from the menu that appears.
GarageBand for iOS offers three ways to share a project: as a Song (the final 
mixdown), a Ringtone (to personalize your iPhone), or a Project (taking your 
work to the Cloud for further editing).
5. Select Song from the menu, and then edit any options or information for 
the export.
The information (including a cover image, which is artwork representing your 
podcast) you offer here is part of your podcast’s ID3 tags. We cover ID3 tags in 
Chapter 9 and provide tips for good cover images in Chapter 10.
6. Tap Share in the top-right corner of the window.
The Share function (shown in Figure 4-8) accesses apps that can share the 
media you are about to export. Swipe left across the offered apps to find your 
cloud storage service.
If you can’t find the app you want to share with, tap the More option (at the 
end of the connected apps) and then find your cloud service app.74 PART 1 Podcasting on a Worldwide Frequency
7. Tap your cloud service app.
8. After your podcast is exported, select its destination and then tap the 
Save To or Upload option.
The audio is now on a cloud service, and you can either fine-tune the audio directly 
from the cloud or repeat the preceding process to upload other audio files, making 
the cloud service a storage bin for resources needed in a current or future project. 
Adjust this workflow accordingly if you want to finish producing an episode on a 
laptop or desktop computer.
This workflow can make your production process more efficient if you want to do 
all your editing and production on mobile devices, allowing your podcast — either 
the production or the final format itself — to happen wherever you are.
Although you can record and export audio to MP3, don’t make an MP3 from 
another MP3. Doing so is the audio equivalent of making a compressed JPEG image 
from another JPEG image. Editing a compressed file and compressing it again in 
the saving and exporting process will deteriorate the audio quality. Always strive 
to record, edit, and export from raw, uncompressed formats such as AIFF or WAV 
files. It takes more space initially but produces better results.

Chapter 5
Before You Hit the 
Record Button
When you hear people talk about finding your voice in broadcasting, it 
means finding a connection with what the average audience desires 
and, to some degree, expects when arriving at a specific channel. A 
classical station (and when we refer to classical, we mean Beethoven and Haydn, 
not the Beatles and Hendrix) features on-air personalities speaking slowly and 
articulately, mellowed and relaxed by the melodic creations of greats such as 
Mozart, Wagner, and Joel. (Yes, Billy Joel has a classical album — a pretty good 
one, too!) Pivot now to the on-air personalities of wacky Morning Drive guys who 
sound as if they’re on their eighth cup of espresso. Both stations understand their 
audiences and their expectations.
This chapter helps you develop the voice and personality you want to convey when 
podcasting. After you discover your voice, you find out how to prepare for a smooth 
and easy podcast that will be glitch-free during the recording process. Show prep 
is essential in making a feed worth catching. Even the most spontaneous podcasts 
follow a logical progression and general direction, remaining focused on the pod￾cast’s intent.
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Finding your voice for your podcast
» Using an outline or script
» Determining the podcast’s length and 
schedule
» Posting your podcast
» Understanding legal issues78 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Choosing a Topic
Podcasting isn’t all about being number one in your chosen podcast genre. Instead 
of worrying about analytics and accolades, think about what will make your pod￾cast uniquely worth your effort and your listeners’ time. What do you have to say 
about your chosen topic?
According to Oberlo (http://pfdbook.link/oberlo), there are currently two 
million active podcasts as of January 2024. Once upon a time, launching a podcast 
was all about being original and unique. However, in the podisphere of the 2020s, 
you need to grab someone’s attention, even for a split-second, and then maintain 
interest. Sounds easy, but it’s not. Here are some suggestions:
» Identify your audience. Understand who your audience is and their interests 
or issues. Talk to like-minded people online and in real life. Consider partici￾pating in a Facebook group or other online community related to your topic. 
Remember, your podcast is all about the audience — your content and 
delivery is for them, not you. Always keep their interests in mind.
» Study other podcasts. Subscribe to a few podcasts that are related to yours 
and that pique your curiosity. Listen to them for a few weeks and jot down 
what you like and don’t like about them. Remember that listening to other 
podcasts should be educational and constructive, not fodder for your own 
show to criticize someone else’s work. Then ask yourself, “What would I like to 
hear? What can I add to this conversation?”
Don’t steal another podcast’s content, special effects, or unique segment (like 
Technorama’s “On This Day in History” or Tee’s “The Five Minute Paranormal”). 
It’s okay to be inspired, as long as you don’t make your podcast a carbon copy 
of your inspiration. Make your podcast your own! Stay on the pod-sitive side.
» Choose a topic you know. Whether the topic is, say, music, religion, or 
technology, the best way to make your podcast unique is to find an angle 
you’re comfortable with (such as Polka: The Misunderstood Music, Great 
Travesties of Sports History, or Forgotten Greats of Science Fiction). Who knows? 
Your initial show might inspire an additional angle so different from the 
original that you’ll have to start another podcast to address that audience.
» Speak confidently. Don’t apologize for being “yet another podcast on . . .” 
or point out what you’re doing “wrong” compared to others. What makes a 
podcast fun is the passion and confidence you exude when the mic is hot. 
Address your topic with authority and energy, and enjoy your time recording 
(see Figure 5-1). If you have a blast making a podcast, your audience will enjoy 
it along with you. That confidence might even inspire others to create a 
podcast.CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 79
Regardless of the genre, the content you bring to a podcast is unique because it’s 
your voice, your angle, and your approach. Provided you maintain a high confi￾dence level, focus on the intent of an episode’s topic, and genuinely enjoy what 
you’re doing, people will tune in as well as talk to other listeners about what 
you’re podcasting.
Finding Your Voice
The broadcasting industry might not want to admit to this, but podcasting and 
commercial radio have a lot in common. In the early days of what is now a major 
radio genre, talk shows were reserved for National Public Radio and news stations. 
In general, they were dry and lackluster, bringing their listeners the news, the 
weather, and daily topics that affected the world  — but nothing particularly 
unusual or exciting.
Then a guy named Howard Stern came along and changed everything in this 
once-tiny niche! You can love him, you can hate him — you can claim to hate him 
when secretly you love him — but Stern turned around what was considered AM￾only programming. Now talk radio is big business. Some personalities are out to 
entertain, other hosts deep dive into lifestyles and subjects of interest, and still 
others voice their political viewpoints.
FIGURE 5-1:
Following the 
successful 
podcasting 
of a novel in 
2005 — the first 
time it was ever 
done — Tee 
hosted The 
Survival Guide to 
Writing Fantasy, 
a podcast of 
marketing 
strategies for an 
author on a 
budget.80 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
A majority of podcasting is just that: talk radio. Each podcast has a different per￾sonality and appeals to a different market. Finding your voice is one of the most 
challenging obstacles to clear. Even if your podcast’s aim is entertainment, you 
have a message you want to convey. That message will influence the voice you 
adopt for your podcast. If you’re podcasting an audio blog about life, its chal￾lenges, and the ups and downs you encounter, maybe a soft tone — relaxed and 
somewhat pensive — would be appropriate. But if you decide to go political — say 
you’re the Angry Young Man who’s fed up with the current business on Capitol 
Hill —it’s time to fine-tune the edge in your voice.
After you discover the passion your podcast is centered on (see the preceding sec￾tion for tips), here are some ways to find your voice:
» Record your voice and then listen to what it sounds like. It astounds us 
how many people hate listening to their recorded voice. It’s a fear akin to 
getting up in front of people and speaking. When finding your voice, though, 
you need to hear what your current voice sounds like. Write a paragraph on 
your show’s subject. Then read it aloud a few times and find a rhythm in your 
words. Expect the following:
• Talking too fast
• Swallowing small, one-syllable words such as to and in
• Saying pause words or stop words like um, ah, y’know, and right
• Ignoring commas, thereby creating one long, run-on thought
• Lip-smacking, and heavy breathing.
You can edit out some of these problems (see Chapter 8), but you should 
grow accustomed to hearing your own voice because you’ll hear yourself 
again and again during the editing process. The more familiar you are with 
how your voice sounds, the easier time you’ll have editing your podcasts 
before publishing them online.
» Play around with the three Ps: pitch, power, and pace. You don’t have to 
be an actor to podcast, but you can apply some acting basics when you’re 
recording. Using the same monotone throughout your podcast is likely to 
drone your listener to sleep. Not what you want especially if they’re on their 
morning commute. Try varying the pitch. If you’re excited or passionate about 
a topic, raise the pitch above your normal speaking tone. You might also 
increase the volume (or power) a bit. And when you want to make a point, 
slow down the pace to make an impact. Experiment with pitch, power, and 
pace to add variety and to find the sweet spot between deadpan and overly 
melodramatic (unless your show is based on those characteristics, of course).CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 81
» Speak clearly. Another simple trick from the acting world is to open your 
mouth wider. Many people talk with their mouths mostly closed, but you gain 
clarity by opening your mouth more.
» Speak with confidence. Yes, we’re saying this again because it bears 
repeating: Speak confidently about your topic. (And this won’t be the last time 
we repeat ourselves.) No one is going to believe in what you say if you don’t 
believe in yourself. It may take a few podcasts to find a groove, or you might 
hit the ground running and have a podcast that immediately takes off. Just 
speak with conviction and allow yourself to shine.
» Develop your podcasting personality. After you know what you sound like 
when you record, it’s time to develop your podcasting personality. Will your 
persona be light, fun, and informal, or something a little edgy or jaded? Is your 
message potentially more controversial, discussing topics such as sports, 
politics, or religion? Or are you podcasting a love of music, science, or your 
Mac? Your persona should match the theme of your show. If you’re doing a 
show on classical music, a persona of a morning radio DJ isn’t going to work. If 
you’re taking a lighthearted look at politics, you want a little more levity in your 
tone and pace than a funeral director. Make sure your tone and delivery are 
consistent with your podcast’s theme.
Finding Your Collective Voice
What if your podcast is not a solo effort? One of our favorite ways to podcast is 
with co-hosts or in-studio guests, where more than one voice gets on mic. While 
there’s something to be said for in-depth monologues or one-on-one interviews, 
a show’s dynamic changes when several people are gathered over a favorite topic. 
For one thing, it’s a lot easier to carry on a conversation! And with the right 
dynamic between hosts, an energy is created that subscribers can see and hear in 
every episode.
Along with the guidelines described in this and other chapters, be aware of the 
following when doing a show with multiple guests.
» Use a mixer with enough channels. In Chapter 2, we talk about gear. 
Remember the mixer? The mixer becomes a crucial piece of equipment when 
co-hosts become part of the production. For the best sound and optimal 
control, each person needs their own microphone, so you need multiple XLR 
connections for microphones. Two hosts, and you’ll need two channels. Four 
hosts, four channels. And you may want a few extra inputs for music, sound 
effects, and more.82 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
» Make sure all contributors can hear. You wear headphones when you 
record, so each guest should have a set of headphones, especially if drop-in’s 
are included in your recording. Before you run out and get a cheap Y cable to 
split the signal, realize that the audio signal degrades with each split. To keep 
the investment economical, invest in a stereo headphone amplifier like the one 
in Figure 5-2 (about $70), which splits the headphone signal (while boosting) to 
four channels. Then pick up a five-pack of Audio-Technica headphones from 
online audio vendor BSW (https://pfdbook.link/ATheadphones) for you 
and your co-hosts or guests.
» Do your prep work. Even after all the years we’ve been podcasting, our audio 
systems still have gremlins. We can record on Saturday afternoon and come 
back Sunday morning to find that audio levels have been adjusted. Okay, it 
could be the cats playing with the mixer settings in the middle of the night, but 
it never hurts to check your audio and video settings before each recording.
» Assign one director. The director is the person in charge of your show’s flow 
and timing and, in some cases, coming up with clever segues from one topic 
to the next. It’s the director’s job to keep the energy up, the conversation 
going, and the episode on track. The director is usually the person at the 
mixer but might instead be someone off mic (or camera) giving hand signals. 
FIGURE 5-2:
With a stereo 
headphone 
amplifier, like the 
Rolls HA43PRO 
pictured here, 
your co-hosts can 
hear your 
recording in real 
time, keeping 
your team 
engaged and 
involved.CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 83
Sometimes, the role of director passes from person to person in the cast. Find 
what works best for your group.
» Give everyone air time. The director should also make sure that everyone 
has a chance to talk. Different people bring different things to your show. 
Some people may be passionate and outspoken (and an unstoppable train) 
while others don’t want to interrupt so they wait their turn. Encourage guests 
to play fair and give everyone a share of the air time. We recommend 
discussing this among regular co-hosts before it becomes a problem.
» Give guests most of the air time. Both Chuck and Tee have seen and heard 
their fair share of interviews gone bad. It can be something as horrific as hosts 
not knowing (or caring) to research anything about the guest. We are talking 
about basic research, such as not knowing how long a science fiction author 
has been writing or an AI engineer’s stance on artificial intelligence. When a 
guest is on the docket, remember that the episode is no longer yours. It’s 
theirs. Do the prep work. Research them. And when possible, share interview 
questions with your guests to see if there’s anything you might be missing. For 
more on interview techniques, look at Chapter 6.
» Orient your guests. Before recording, let guests know the format and flow of 
your show. You don’t need to go into all the details, but give them a general 
idea of when they can start talking, how long the show runs, and if you want 
them to hang around after recording for any post-show requests such as 
recording identification drops, quick audio or video bumpers where your guest 
reminds people what they’re listening to. (“Hi, this is Tee Morris of Podcasting 
for Dummies, and you are listening to Technorama.”)
» Make sure everyone has clear sight lines. As much as 93 percent of 
communication between co-hosts is nonverbal. Twirling fingers at your 
co-host (“Wrap it up”) or raising a hand (“I want to ask the next question”) 
makes an interview or an episode recording flow. (However, if video is 
involved, you might not have the luxury of non-verbal communication.) If 
you’re not in person, use Google Meet, Discord, Zoom, or another videocon￾ferencing program to see each other — yes, even for an audio podcast! The 
ability to read a co-host or guest will help you gauge how well the conversa￾tion is going.
To keep your show length consistent, include fewer topics than usual when your 
show includes guests. Part of Chuck’s Saturday ritual for building out the Sunday 
night episode is to find out how many people are coming over so he can add or 
remove topics accordingly. Tee coordinates with his co-hosts on who will take the 
lead on a specific topic. Breaks offer a moment for anyone to tell him “Mind if I 
take the lead in the second half?” If you don’t care about length, just realize that 
more guests will make for a longer show and plan accordingly.84 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Deciding Whether You Need 
an Outline or Script
Some writers prefer to use an outline when putting together a story; others merely 
take an idea, a few points, and a direction, and then let their fingers work across 
the keyboard. What method will work best for you when podcasting? A full script 
and hours of prep time, or a single notecard and two mouse clicks  — one for 
record and another for stop? Both approaches work, depending on the podcaster’s 
personality.
If you decide to work with a script, invest some time in preshow prep, a simple 
preparation for what you’ll say and how you’ll deliver it. Depending on your pod￾cast, prep time may vary. Here are a couple of examples of how dramatically dif￾ferent prep time can be for different podcasting situations:
» For his podcast Don’t Turn Around with Phil Rossi (see Figure 5-3), Phil does 
some basic research on the next guest joining him online or in the studio — 
usually just enough to get some basic facts about the paranormal aspect they 
want to cover. After minimal orientation with the guest, he’s ready to record.
» On the other side of the spectrum is one of the original podcasts, The Radio 
Adventures of Doctor Floyd, a 10-minute show in the style of old-time radio with 
a modern, educational, comedic spin. From 2004–2010, Grant Baciocco and 
Doug Price carefully scripted every show, spending one to three hours on 
preshow prep. That thorough scripting came in handy when Doctor Floyd
featured celebrity actors playing a part in the show.
Preshow prep can range from jotting a few notes on a napkin to writing a com￾plete script with full sound effects — regardless of show length. So how far should 
your prep go? That depends on what your podcast needs. Outlines and scripts can 
serve as roadmaps that move the conversation smoothly from topic to topic.
Whether you’re a napkin scribbler, a script writer, or someone in between, the 
secret to efficient preshow prep can be boiled down to three disciplines:
» Habit: Many podcasters, especially those emerging from corporate offices, 
prepare for podcasts as they do for business presentations. They jot down 
the essential points to keep the podcast on track. You can easily apply your 
organizational skills from the workplace to the podosphere. Whatever you 
do for your preshow prep, make it a habit.CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 85
» Talent: Some podcasters are truly the Evel Knievels of podcasting, firing up 
their mics and recording in one take. These podcasters tend to have back￾grounds in live entertainment, deciding in a moment when a change of 
delivery is required. For most, a talent for quick thinking must be developed 
with time.
» Passion: With a majority of podcasters, passion is a driving force that keeps 
their podcasts spur-of-the-moment. With enough drive, inspiration, and 
confidence in their message, they keep their prep time to a minimum because 
podcasting isn’t a chore but a form of recreation.
Determining a Length for Your Show
If you’ve been using this chapter to develop your podcast, you’ve made serious 
progress at this point in completing your preshow prep. Now you’re ready to pod￾cast, right?
FIGURE 5-3:
Phil Rossi 
doesn’t rely on 
meticulous show 
preparation for 
his podcast but 
does conduct 
basic research to 
keep interviews 
on track with the 
podcast’s 
paranormal 
theme.86 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Well, no. Have you thought about the length of your show’s episode? Podcast 
length is the subject of a much-heated debate. Some say that your podcast 
shouldn’t be longer than 30 minutes because that’s the time of an average 
commute. (Chuck has received listener feedback asking “Can you make your show 
the length of my commute?”) Tee’s response hasn’t changed since 2005: 
“Obviously, you don’t live in the Washington, D.C. Metro area, do you?”
Even if the average commute is 30 minutes, that shouldn’t be a set-in-stone tem￾plate for podcast length. Some shows are 60 seconds, others go on for hours 
(thank you to the person who invented the Pause button!), and still others vary in 
length. When you decide the length of your podcast, remember that you always 
have the option for super-sized episodes if a juicy conversation might happen. For 
this discussion, though, we consider a sweet spot for a podcast to be around 
30 minutes.
The value of the short podcast
On average — and this is more like an understood average, not a scientific, detailed 
study of all the podcasts out there — podcasts runs 20 to 30 minutes per episode. 
However, many podcasts run 10 minutes or less. Hosts deliver their message and 
sign off moments after you thought they signed on. So, what about these quick￾and-simple productions? Does size matter? Does time matter? (Woah. Deep.) Is 
there such a thing as too short a podcast?
Here are some advantages to a brief podcast:
» Shorter production time: Production time may be reduced from a weeklong 
project to a single afternoon of planning, talking, editing, and mixing. With a 
quick and simplified production schedule, delivering a podcast on a regular 
basis is easier.
» Faster downloads: Your podcast subscribers will always have fast and 
efficient downloads — no matter how you compress your audio.
» Easier to stay on target: If you limit yourself to a running time of 10 minutes 
or less, it’s easier to stick to the intent (and the immediate message) of your 
podcast. There’s no room for in-depth chat, spontaneous banter, or tangents. 
Shakespeare said, “Brevity is the soul of wit.” Ol’ Bill would host a short 
podcast if he were alive today.
Nothing’s wrong with keeping a podcast short and sweet. In fact, you might gain 
more subscribers who appreciate your efficiency.CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 87
A little length won’t kill you
Now with that quote from the Bard about brevity, you might think, “Shakespeare 
said that?! Before or after he wrote Hamlet?” That’s a good point because a number 
of his characters did say, “My lord, I will be brief” and then launched into a mul￾tipage monologue.
So what if Shakespeare’s podcast ran over 10 minutes? Would he lose sub￾scribers? What if he broke the 60-minute ceiling? Would the Podcast Police shut 
down his show?
Some podcasts can easily fill two or even three hours. You’ll know if you have a 
beefy podcast on your hands after you record a few episodes. Huge productions 
have the following advantages:
» If the show is an interview, you’ll have anywhere from two to three 
hours with an authority. Your podcast becomes something like having a 
one-on-one consultation. When a really good interview takes place, you can 
rest assured your podcast will go a little longer. Allow the interview to run its 
course, and then you can judge how many episodes you have from an 
unexpectedly long recording session.
Shows and interviews that ramble run the risk of losing the audience’s 
attention. We talk more about good interview practices in Chapter 6.
» You have verbal breathing room. A discussion stretching past the 30-minute 
mark allows you and your co-hosts or guests to break off into loosely related 
banter, widening your podcast’s focus and sparking a conversation that can 
lead in other directions.
Podcasts longer than 30 minutes have additional bandwidth and file storage costs. 
See Chapter 3 for more on bandwidth demands on your server and the associated 
financial expense.
Finding a happy medium
Is there such a thing as a middle ground in the length of a podcast? How can you 
find a happy medium if podcasters can’t agree on a standard running time?
The happy medium for a podcast comes down to expectancy and consistency. For 
example, in Tee’s award-winning podcast Tales from the Archives, the running time 
for each episode is across the board  — the shortest clocking in at just over 88 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
30 minutes and the longest weighing in at over 60 minutes. His audience, how￾ever, understood this was a podiobook, an audiobook presented in a serialized for￾mat. Readers expect that chapters and short stories vary in length, so it was not a 
surprise when Tales from the Archives followed suit and had various running times 
for episodes.
In the case of Technorama, Chuck and Kreg have a lot to geek about, but they are 
able to focus their passions and keep the show length close to an hour. Sure, they 
may have a special episode that runs long (a visit to the Hoover Dam operations 
center or backstage at The Star Trek Experience in Las Vegas), but Technorama
remains consistent for the majority of episodes.
Give yourself time to develop your show, your voice, and your direction. If you 
build consistency and expectation for your audience, it’s easier to introduce a little 
variation or even a happy medium into your running time.
Mark Your Calendar: Posting Schedule
You have a format for your show. You have an idea about its running time. Now 
you have to figure out when episodes will go live. What is the best posting pace to 
set for your podcast? What are the advantages to posting frequently versus post￾ing on occasion?
You can post whenever you have an episode ready, but setting a consistent podcast 
schedule will help you establish trust with your audience. Subscribers like to know 
when they can expect your next episode. There are several options for posting 
schedules: daily, weekly, Mondays and Fridays, biweekly, monthly, and so forth.
To figure out the posting schedule that works best for you, sit down and brain￾storm show topics. If you rattle off several ideas, rapid-fire, you might have 
enough content for a more frequent schedule. If your content relies on current 
events, you’ll want to have a workflow that is best suited for quick episode drops. 
If you’re struggling to get ideas on paper, you’ll want more time between 
episodes.
Posting daily
The demands of podcasting — recording, editing, and posting — can be daunting, 
but daily podcasts are still out in the podisphere and prospering. Some podcasters CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 89
achieve a daily schedule by minimizing production with raw and unedited record￾ings. In rare cases where budget includes a post-production crew, a host will 
record and then rely on the staff to take on the editorial workflow. An advantage 
with daily podcasts is that these productions do not necessarily follow a linear 
path. Listeners don’t have to go back to the first episode to understand the flow 
and atmosphere of later episodes. Just jump in and enjoy!
Regardless of your intended schedule, life sometimes deals an unexpected hand. 
Just let your audience know. They’ll understand. Most podcasters have a day job, 
family that needs attention, and a host of other responsibilities, scheduled or oth￾erwise. You don’t have to reveal personal details if it’s not appropriate, but be 
polite and send a quick message to your audience.
Similar to the preceding tip, you might also consider having seasons for your pod￾cast, similar again to television schedules, to prevent burnout or to better fit your 
content. For example, in Tee’s steampunk podcast, Tales from the Archives, seasons 
are defined by 10 to 12 short stories. Again, if a break is coming up in your produc￾tion, make sure to let your audience know.
Posting weekly
The most common schedule we have seen for podcasts is weekly posting, much 
like popular television shows. Making a commitment to producing new content 
once a week might not seem like much of a demand, but it may surprise you how 
quickly “posting day” comes around when running a weekly schedule.
Make sure the content is recorded, polished, and ready to schedule before you 
launch a podcast. Record a few episodes and work with them to develop a work￾flow that makes your production and posting schedule more efficient. For exam￾ple, gaming podcasts such as ADHD D&D (https://pfdbook.link/adhddnd), 
shown in Figure 5-4, and So Many Levels (https://pfdbook.link/somanylevels) 
record gaming sessions that can last for an hour (or longer). The editors of these 
podcasts create several episodes from these gaming sessions, and then present 
them in a serialized format.
With a buffer of previous gameplay, you’re assured of enough content to meet the 
demands of a weekly podcast. Be consistent with the day of the week for your pod￾cast because listeners or viewers will be expecting your next episode. However, as 
with many things, there are exceptions. If you’re creating a podcast about news or 
other current events, it’s not possible to record in advance.90 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Posting biweekly (or fortnightly for our 
friends in the Commonwealth)
Say you have a reasonable amount of content, but not a lot of free time. Or maybe 
you want to podcast frequently but are concerned that you’ll burn through the 
content before you can come up with new ideas and directions for your show. A 
biweekly posting schedule is a comfortable alternative to the regular demands of a 
weekly production. A biweekly podcast such as The Topic Is Trek (https://
pfdbook.link/thetopicistrek) finds a good life-work-podcast balance. How￾ever, a biweekly podcast may have less audience engagement and won’t be as 
timely compared to a more frequent posting schedule.
Posting monthly
One of the biggest benefits of a monthly podcast is a more relaxed production 
schedule. Another appealing aspect of a monthly podcast schedule is that you can 
have longer-than-usual running times on topics inspired by the recent weeks’ 
headlines. Such is the case with shows like Reset with Liz Tran (https://pfdbook.
link/reset) and The Art Monthly Talk Show (https://pfdbook.link/artmonthly), 
where the hosts enjoy the conversation, tangents and all, recording episodes of an 
hour or more.
And because you have so much time to prep for a monthly podcast, you can gather 
plenty of resources and backstories as well as your thoughts. So if you have a 
FIGURE 5-4:

has an audio

adventures, so 
they can post

subject that needs time for research and discussion, posting monthly may be a 
better option for you.
However, building and nurturing a community with content that occurs only once 
a month can be difficult. Additionally, creating a podcast with a timely topic is 
impossible when commenting on news or events that occurred weeks before the 
recording session.
If you find yourself with an intermittent schedule, don’t apologize for the absence. 
Over the years, we’ve listened to a number of podcasts with random schedules. 
When we finally do hear from these wayward podcasters, we’re excited. It would 
be a letdown if their first words were “Sorry for not producing the show as fre￾quently as I would like.” Instead, consider telling us how glad you are to be back. 
Or don’t mention it at all; get right in and start your delivery as if nothing hap￾pened. We forgive you!
I Hear Music (and It Sounds 
Like Police Sirens!)
To give your show an extra punch or a shot of personality, add music. Although 
our tastes range from classical to jazz to rock ’n’ roll, and our skills in music com￾position are far from the level of composer, we both appreciate and understand 
the power of music and what it can bring to a podcast. A soundtrack can bring a 
lot to a production, so we encourage you to find a solid score for your podcast.
We also understand and appreciate the law, so read on and find out more about 
what not to do with music in your podcast. Although you may think it’s cool to 
thumb your nose at corporate America, there are serious rules to follow when 
featuring your favorite song as your podcast theme.
We are podcasters, not lawyers. We can tell you about the law as it pertains to 
podcasting and can provide a few simple definitions, but we aren’t giving legal 
advice. If you need a legal call on a matter concerning your podcast — whether it 
concerns the First Amendment, copyright issues, or slander  — please consult 
a lawyer.
The powers that be
The government still regards the internet as a digital Wild West, an unknown ter￾ritory that’s avoided regulation for many years, granting those who use it a true, 92 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
self-governed entity where ideas, cultures, and concepts can be expressed without 
filtering or editing, unless it comes from the users themselves.
Does this mean we podcasters are free to do as we please? No, not by a long shot. 
Even podcasters must follow rules and regulations as well as pay attention to cer￾tain organizations and their influence on the podisphere. It will be to your benefit 
to understand how their legislation, activities, and actions can affect you.
Federal Communications Commission (FCC)
The Federal Communications Commission, or FCC (https://pfdbook.link/fcc), is 
the watchdog of anything and everything that gets out to the public through mass 
communications. The FCC keeps an eye on technology development, monopolies 
in the telecommunications industry, and regulating standards for telecommuni￾cations in the US and its territories. It is most commonly known for enforcing 
decency laws on television and AM/FM radio.
For podcasters, the FCC can’t regulate what is said (yet) because it doesn’t con￾sider the internet a broadcasting medium. However, given existing legislation to 
reduce spam (junk email) and the ever-growing popularity of podcasting among 
mainstream broadcasters (such as iHeartMedia, NPR, and ESPN), it may not be 
long before the law catches up with the technology.
Recording Industry Association of America (RIAA)
Shawn Fanning. Does that name ring a bell? Fanning lost his battle against the 
Recording Industry Association of America, or RIAA (https://pfdbook.link/riaa), 
when he contested that his file-sharing application, Napster, in no way infringed 
on copyright laws and was not promoting music piracy. The RIAA led the charge 
in shutting down the original Napster and continues to protect the property rights 
of its members — as well as review new and pending laws, regulations, and poli￾cies at the state and federal level.
The RIAA will have a definite say as to why you can’t use a selected piece of music 
for your podcast. Simply put, it’s not your music. Sure, you downloaded that 
album from Apple Music, but you listen to that music with the condition that you 
use it for listening purposes only. (Didn’t realize there were conditions involved, 
did you?) You can’t use it as a personal introduction that people will associate with 
you. And, no matter how appropriate your favorite song, you can’t use it as back￾ground music. Unless you are granted a license and pay a fee to the record label 
and artist, you’re in copyright violation when playing music without permission.CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 93
One way of getting music for themes, bed music (low volume background music), 
and segues is to check out podsafe music, which is professionally produced music 
from independent artists offering their works for podcasting use. The demand for 
podsafe music has been so high that several sites have been launched, offering a 
wide array of genres, artists, and musical works:
» Free Music Archive (see Figure 5-5): https://pfdbook.link/fma
» Neosounds: https://pfdbook.link/neosounds
» Sound Stripe: https://pfdbook.link/soundstripe
» Sync or Stream: https://pfdbook.link/syncorstream
Visit these sites to find out more about podsafe music, the conditions of using it, 
and how it can benefit your podcast.
Electronic Frontier Foundation (EFF)
In addition to the big dogs who pass laws and legislations to restrain your pod￾casting capabilities, a group is looking out for you, the podcaster, with science 
fiction author and tech activist Cory Doctorow stepping forward as one of its more 
outspoken members. The Electronic Frontier Foundation, or EFF (https://pfdbook.
FIGURE 5-5:
The Free Music 
Archive is an 
interactive library 
of high-quality,

directed by 
WFMU-FM, the 
longest running 
freeform radio 
station in the 
United States.94 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
link/eff), is a donor-supported organization working to protect the digital rights 
of the individual; to educate the media, lawmakers, and the public on how tech￾nology affects their civil liberties; and to uphold said civil liberties if they’re 
threatened.
A good example of EFF’s mission is its involvement in various legal cases con￾cerning URL domain registration and cybersquatters (individuals who buy desired 
domains and do nothing but hold onto these web addresses, waiting to sell them 
to the highest bidder). The EFF stands for the rights of legitimate website owners 
who own a domain that a larger corporation wants to use.
Provided you have a strong case to contradict the findings of the RIAA and the 
FCC, the EFF will stand up for you and give your voice a bit of power against 
the corporate legal machine. Find out how by visiting their site and looking at the 
information and services they provide.
Creative Commons (CC)
Copyright protection is a double-edged sword for many. On the positive side, your 
work is protected so that no one can steal it for personal profit. Also, if someone 
claims that you’re ripping off their work, your copyright is proof that your egg 
came before their chicken. The downside of this protection is that people must go 
through channels to feature your work. Although you’re given credit for the fea￾tured property, there’s still a matter of approvals, fees for usage, and conditions 
that must be met. Also, many contributors just want to share their work with oth￾ers on no other terms but to contribute and share with the world. Copyrights com￾plicate this.
Enter Creative Commons, or CC (https://pfdbook.link/creativecommons), a 
nonprofit corporation founded in 2001 and dedicated to helping artists, their 
copyrighted material, and the individuals who want to use copyrighted material in 
a constructive manner but may not have the resources to buy rights from groups 
like the RIAA. CC, shown in Figure 5-6, is dedicated to drafting and implementing 
internet licenses granting fair use of copyrighted material.
In the case of the podcaster, if you want to offer your audio content to everyone, 
CC can provide you with licenses that aid you in letting people know your podcast 
is up for grabs as long as others give credit where credit is due. CC provides these 
same licenses to artists and musicians who don’t mind if you use their music for 
your podcast.CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 95
CC licenses are made up of the following permissions:
» Attribution: Grants permission to copy, distribute, display, and perform the 
original work and derivative works inspired from it, provided credit to the 
artist(s) is given.
» Noncommercial: Grants permission to copy, distribute, display, and perform 
the original work and derivative works inspired from it for noncommercial 
purposes only.
» No Derivative Works: Grants permission to copy, distribute, display, and 
perform the original work only. No derivative works are covered in this license 
category.
» Share Alike: Grants reproduction of the original work and also allows 
derivative works if they’re also released under a similar Creative Commons 
license.
These permissions can be used as standalone licenses or can be mixed and matched 
to fit the needs of the podcaster or the artist offering content for the podcast.
The CC and its website give details, examples, and an FAQ page that answers 
questions concerning the granting of licenses for the use of protected content. On 
the off-chance you don’t find your answer on the website, check out the contact 
information for CC representatives. CC is a good group to know and can open 
opportunities to present new and innovative ideas and works in your podcast.
FIGURE 5-6:
Creative 
Commons offers 
free licenses for 
use of original 
content in 
podcasting.96 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
I can name that tune . . . I wrote it!
Using anyone else’s music for your podcast can be an open invitation for the RIAA 
to shut it down, primarily to protect the artist’s rights. Think about it — how 
would you feel if you were producing a popular podcast and receiving praise from 
all over the world, and while you’re thinking about ways of taking the podcast to 
the next level, you turn on your radio and hear your podcast being broadcast on a 
top-rated radio station. Soon, your podcast is all the rage on the broadcasting 
airwaves — and you haven’t made dollar one.
The same can be said for artists and their music. They work hard to produce their 
work, and now podcasters are using their music to brand their shows, not bother￾ing to compensate the artists for their efforts.
So how can you use a piece of music without suffering the wrath of the RIAA or 
FCC? Ask permission of the artist? Only if the artist owns all rights to the music 
and the recordings. Otherwise, you need to get written permission from vocal art￾ists, musicians, record labels, producers, and more.
The best way to avoid the legal hassles is to avoid copyrighted material that is not 
your own.
If you want to use published pieces that aren’t royalty-free, ask the artist directly 
(if you can) for permission to use that music on a regular basis. Compensation to 
the artist may come in the form of a promotion at the beginning of the podcast, 
the end, or both. As long as you have written permission from the artist and the 
artist has the power to grant it (that is, they haven’t signed the power over to their 
label or publisher), you should be able to use their work to brand your show or 
feature them on your podcast. (If you’re not sure whether you have the appropri￾ate permission, you may want to consult an attorney.) Acknowledgement of the 
music artist is usually acceptable with independent artists because, in many cases, 
they also own the record label. Confirm these details with the artists. Otherwise, 
you run into the same legal issues if you were to use music recorded by Queen, Dua 
Lipa, or U2.
You can always offer your podcast as a venue for the musician to sell his or her 
work. Such is the case for George Hrab (shown in Figure  5-7) of The Geologic 
Podcast (http://pfdbook.link/geologic). George made an impression on pod￾casters and listeners attending Dragon Con, and his irreverent humor, critical 
thinking, and love of music made The Geologic Podcast an instant hit. George is also 
a mega-talented musician, and The Geologic Podcast serves as a viable platform for 
his music, which you can hear before it goes live on Spotify and Apple Music.CHAPTER 5 Before You Hit the Record Button 97
I’ll take the First: Free speech 
versus slander
Words can get you in as much trouble as music. The legal definition of slander is a 
verbal form of defamation or spoken words that falsely and negatively reflect on 
one’s reputation.
So, where does podcasting fit into this? Well, the internet is a public space. Before 
you begin a slam-fest on someone you don’t like in the media or at work, remem￾ber that your rant is reaching MP3 players around the world. Cite your sources and 
make certain those sources are not only reliable and authentic but also confirmed 
by other credible sources. By citing your sources, you tell your listener that you’re 
not making this stuff up and you drive traffic to the referenced website. When 
expressing opinions — jaded, constructive, or otherwise — have evidence to back 
up what you say.

Musician George 
Hrab doesn’t 
need to worry 
about the music 
he features on his 
podcast, The 
Geologic Podcast,
because he writes 
and performs it. 
Bruce Press Photography98 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Your freedom to express yourself comes with responsibility and consequences. 
Your words carry weight, and if you go after others in your professional circles or 
in your community, there is no guarantee your listeners will back you. You may 
picture your fans rallying around you and your show but find yourself working 
damage control instead. Before wrapping yourself in the First Amendment (which 
doesn’t apply unless the government is spamming your comments section), 
remember freedom of speech is a right, not a privilege.CHAPTER 6 Interview-Fu: Talk to Me, Grasshopper 99
Chapter 6
Interview-Fu: Talk to 
Me, Grasshopper
Podcasting is empowering. There’s something about a microphone in your 
hand that instills courage. Suddenly, you’re not afraid of anything. Oh yeah, 
you’re “running with the big dogs” now, and like Paula Zhan, Stephen 
Sackur, or Anderson Cooper, you’re finding out what makes your guest tick.
What sets you apart from those big dogs, though, is skill. The late, great Anthony 
Bourdain made it look easy on Parts Unknown, as people opened up to him over a 
local dish or an original culinary experience — but that was the magic of the per￾fect setting, the best dish, and the charm of Bourdain. He clicked with people. (His 
interview with Danny Trejo is a favorite of Tee’s.)
Make no mistake: Interviews are not easy. Hosting an interview is a skill, and 
hosting a great interview is an art. The good news is that these luminaries blessed 
with the gift of gab had to start somewhere. Podcasting is an excellent venue for 
developing and honing these skills, but you need a solid foundation on which to 
build your skill set.
In this chapter, we provide tips for making a successful interview request. After 
you land the interview, we’re here to help you get ready by showing you how to 
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Requesting an interview
» Getting prepared for interviews
» Welcoming guests
» Recording your interviews with Zoom 
and StreamYard
» Making sure your interviews are 
trouble-free100 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
both ask good questions to keep the conversation lively and engaging and avoid 
bad questions. You also discover some tried-and-true hardware and software for 
recording your interview. Finally, we impart those always-valuable behind-the￾scenes technical tips to make the interview go smoothly.
I’ll Have My People Call Your People: 
Interview Requests
The courage to submit an interview request comes simply from your interest in 
the interview subject. Compose an email to ask your favorite author, actor, sports 
celebrity, streamer, podcaster, or whomever you want for an interview. You may 
need to submit the request multiple times, and sometimes you might work 
through numerous people simply to get “no” as your final reply. But for every 
“no,” you’ll find others who will enthusiastically say “yes.”
Keep the following in mind when working on your interview request:
» Market yourself and your show. A successful podcast requires a lot of 
marketing. Your interview request should sell your services to the prospective 
interviewee. If you’re part of a podcasting network, be sure to mention that 
fact because large listenership numbers are always helpful. If you’ve con￾ducted other interviews, do some name dropping. If not, a good place to start 
might be to interview other podcasters, who also want to increase their 
listenership.
» Figure out what can you do for the interviewee. The person (or the 
person’s agent) is going ask, “What’s in it for me (or my client)?” Does the 
person have a new book coming out or are they about to launch a special 
product? In 2008, Chuck and his co-host Kreg went to the Hoover Dam for an 
on-site interview for their Technorama Podcast; they had a genuine interest in 
the science and the history behind the engineering achievement. That pitch 
granted them an incredible behind-the-scenes look at the famous dam (see 
Figure 6-1). Find an angle and work with it.
» Be flexible. Remember, you’re asking for their time. There may be restrictions 
in your schedule and theirs. You might be asked to record an interview right 
on the spot, or within 24 hours, or weeks or months later. You might have to 
take time off from your regular job, rearrange other plans in your week, or 
cancel previous arrangements, just like the interview subject who is taking 
time out of their day to chat with you.CHAPTER 6 Interview-Fu: Talk to Me, Grasshopper 101
Don’t assume that the person reading your interview request is going to know 
your podcast. Provide a brief overview of your show. Your tag line is a great place 
to start. Having a ready-made “elevator pitch” (a 20- to 30-second description) 
is particularly helpful.
FIGURE 6-1:
In 2008, the 
Technorama crew 
got an exclusive
