formal, sincere 
request for an 
interview.
THE INTERVIEW REQUEST
One of the easiest ways to make an interview request is via email. It’s time to put 
your marketing hat on top of your public relations hat and consider how you want to 
represent your show when sending queries. First, putting words like Interview request or 
Podcast interview request in the subject tells the reader what you want before they even 
open the message. Use a warm greeting: “Hello” followed by the recipient’s name is 
always a good start. Remember, you may not be sending the message to the inter￾viewee directly but rather have to work through their agent or handler.
In the body of the message, use the BLUF — bottom line up front — rule. The first line 
of your message body should include something like “I am requesting an interview with 
Brent Spiner for The Topic is Trek podcast.” There’s no doubt about what you want. We 
(continued)102 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
know it’s tempting to dive into the show details after you mention it by name, but don’t. 
This query is about the interview subject. Don’t go into great detail about your show yet.
Explain why you want to speak with that person. Do they have any upcoming or recent 
books, movies, or appearances they are interested in promoting? Also mention how 
much time you’re asking for: 2 hours is a much different consideration (and easier to 
say no to) than 15 minutes.
Next, describe your show but don’t drone on — a sentence or two and perhaps some 
notable names of people you have interviewed is all you need. This is where a well￾polished tag line is useful. Something like “The Topic is Trek is a show about Star Trek
news, episodes, and thought-provoking questions in a fun and energetic tone.” In some 
cases, you won’t need to explain your show if its title explains what the show is about. 
For example, “We would love to host an interview with Shohreh Aghdashloo in an 
upcoming episode of Happy Hour from the Tower: A Destiny Podcast.” You’ve just made 
the request, identified the show, and described what it’s about. If you have an audio 
file or a video file that describes your show, include a link to that as well.
Close with a respectful yet positive salutation, such as “I look forward to hearing back 
from you.” Remember, most people are busy and may not respond right away, so you 
might need to follow up periodically. It took Chuck two years of polite reminders to get 
an interview with Dr. Robert Ballard (the man who discovered the Titanic), but it was well 
worth the effort. Here’s an example of how that might look:
Hello agent name,
I would like to request an audio interview with guest full name for our podcast 
Technorama (link below). As fans, we’d love to talk to guest first name, and we’re 
sure our listeners would love to share in the experience. Our phone interviews 
are typically 30 to 45 minutes long.
Kreg and Chuck would like to talk about guest first name’s story and any other projects 
they have coming up. Chuck has just finished reading book name and absolutely loved it!
About our show: Technorama is a weekly podcast about tech, science, and sci-fi, with 
pop-culture references and other geek humor. Along with our standard weekly show, 
we also have special interviews. Some of our guests included:
• Dr. Robert Ballard, scientist and discoverer of the Titanic and Bismarck
• Adam Savage and Grant Imahara from the Discovery Channel’s MythBusters
• Astronomer David Levy, discoverer of comet Shoemaker-Levy 9
• Dean Haglund from The X-Files
(continued)CHAPTER 6 Interview-Fu: Talk to Me, Grasshopper 103
Don’t forget to exchange contact information with your guest after your interview 
is confirmed. Will you be calling their phone or will you be using Facebook Mes￾senger, Zoom, or Discord? The means of contact will have an effect on what you 
need to record the call (covered later, in the “Recording Interviews” section).
Preparing for Interviews
Have a plan. All great interviewers, be they Barbara Walters or Stephen Colbert, 
plan which questions garner the best responses.
Asking great questions
A podcast interview isn’t like a job interview. In podcasting, interviews are inter￾personal, casual chats that can become thought-provoking or downright contro￾versial, depending on the podcast’s subject matter. Whether you did all the 
pre-interview prep or are simply a mouthpiece for someone else’s questions, here 
are a few tips to take to heart so you can hold a good, engaging interview:
» Know who you’re talking to and what to talk about. When guests appear 
on your show, at the very least you should know the subject matter. For 
example, suppose you’re interviewing an author who has written a popular 
• Steve Wozniak, co-founder of Apple Computer
• Dr. Michio Kaku, theoretical physicist
Thank you for your time and consideration. I look forward to scheduling a time when 
we can speak to guest first name.
Technorama website: http://www.chuckchat.com/technorama
Technorama explained: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=WeyFK6SWvWo
Warmest regards!
--Chuck Tomasi
--Father, Husband, IT professional, Podcaster, Author, Martial Artist
--https://www.chucktomasi.com/104 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
series. Finding time to read the entire series would be difficult, so go online 
and research it instead. Also find websites relevant to the topic of the series. 
If the series is steampunk, dig up information about the Victorian era. If the 
series follows a snarky, sentient robot, look up artificial intelligence. You’ll be 
able to ask better questions. These same rules apply for nonfiction authors 
and really any guests. You don’t have to be an expert on their subject matter, 
but you should be familiar with it so you know in what direction to take the 
interview.
If your guest has a website and is on social media, check out those venues 
as well.
» Make sure your questions follow a logical progression. Say you’re 
interviewing a filmmaker who is working on a horror movie. A good 
progression for your interview would be something like this:
• How did you get started in filmmaking?
• Who inspired you in this genre?
• In your opinion, what is the most challenging film you ever made?
• I found your character Anabel particularly intriguing. Was she based on 
someone you know?
• Do you have any upcoming projects?
These questions are all based on filmmaking and focused on your guest’s 
work. We start with a broad question, then get more specific, and conclude 
with a potential promotion for their upcoming work. Most interviews should 
follow a progression from specific to broad or vice versa, where you start with 
a very broad question and slowly become more specific to the guest’s 
expertise.
» Ask open-ended questions. To understand open-ended questions, it’s 
simpler to explain closed-ended questions. Close-ended questions are the 
kind that give you one-word answers, for example, “How many years have you 
been studying plate tectonics?” Don’t be surprised if your interviewee comes 
back with “Seven.” And then . . . silence. Close-ended questions make the 
process harder than it needs to be. Instead, rephrase your question as “So 
what got you interested in plate tectonics?”
» Prepare twice the number of questions that you think you’ll need. Some 
interviews grind to a halt because the interviewer thought that the guest’s 
responses would be longer. You’re in for a bumpy ride if you ask a guest, “Tell 
the listener a little bit about your experience at WidgetCo,” and the guest 
replies, “It was a lot of hard work but rewarding.” (Yeah, this is going to be 
painful.) This is why you should always have far more questions than you 
think you’ll need.CHAPTER 6 Interview-Fu: Talk to Me, Grasshopper 105
If you figure that every question merits a 2-minute answer on average and the 
interview will last 20 minutes, have 15 to 20 questions ready to ask. However, 
your guest could go on for 10 minutes in response to the first question, so 
prioritize your questions so you ask the main ones you think your audience 
will love — and be prepared to do some editing!
Also be ready with a closing question to let your guest know it’s time to wind 
down. For example, “Thank you for your time today. Before we go, can you let 
the listener know how to contact you?”
» Don’t worry about asking an obvious question. When asking questions 
that may sound obvious or frequently asked, remember that your audience 
has probably never heard them answered before. Okay, maybe a writer has 
been asked time and again, “Where do your ideas come from?” or a steam￾punk, as shown in Figure 6-2, has often heard, “So, how long did it take you 
to make this prop?” When you have a guest present for a podcast, there’s no 
such thing as an obvious (or stupid) question; what’s really dumb is to not ask 
a question that you think isn’t worth the guest’s time. The person may be 
champing at the bit in hopes you’ll ask it.
FIGURE 6-2:
While interview￾ing steampunks 
at DragonCon, 
Chuck never 
worries about 
asking “How long 
did these props 
take you to 
make?” because 
the often-asked 
question is 
welcomed by 
makers, 
cosplayers, and 
other creatives.106 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Leave room for spontaneous questions. Listen to your guest’s answers and see if a 
new path has opened up. They may be tense while answering the same questions 
for the 50th time, but if you strike a chord and stumble on a piece they are pas￾sionate about, abandon the questions for a bit and follow the trail! Doing so can 
lead to interesting conversations and stories.
Avoiding inappropriate questions
Before you start dreaming up a few questions based on the preceding tips, stop 
and think about the interviews you’ve listened to where things suddenly went 
awry. Usually, the interviewer knows nothing about the guest and is expected to 
interview the person on the fly, or the host ambushes the guest with questions 
that dig into something out of the guest’s scope or are none of the interviewer’s 
business. In this section, we pile up the typical gaffes in a prime example of a good 
interview gone bad.
The following blunders will turn a pleasant conversation sour faster than the run 
of an Uwe Boll movie:
» Asking inappropriate questions. Keep in mind that your podcast is not 
60 Minutes, HardTalk, or even Judge Judy. If you want to fire off hard-hitting, 
tell-all, mudslinging questions, think about who you’re talking to and whether 
the person can answer honestly and openly. If not, an awkward moment may 
be the least of your worries. Inappropriate questions can also be irrelevant, 
wacky, off-the-wall, and far-too-personal questions for your guests. “Who 
was the rudest person you’ve ever worked with on a set?” could put a stunt 
performer’s career into jeopardy if answered earnestly. “What’s the worst 
book you’ve ever read?” could drop a writer into hot water with their col￾leagues. Asking athletes, “You’re in fantastic shape. Do you sleep naked?” 
could easily derail an interview. Maybe these wild card questions work for 
shock jocks, but when you have an opportunity to interview people you 
respect, do you really want to ask them something like, “Boxers, briefs, or 
none of the above?”
» Continuing to pursue answers to inappropriate questions. If a question 
has been deemed inappropriate by a guest, don’t continue to ask it. Move on 
to the next question. Podcasts are not an arena for browbeating guests into 
submission until they break down in tears and cough up the ugly, sordid 
details of their lives.
And news flash: They’re not going to.
Are there exceptions? Yes, depending on the content of your podcast. Say 
after reading — and enjoying — Podcasting For Dummies, you decide to take 
on the risk of asking inappropriate questions and become the next Zach CHAPTER 6 Interview-Fu: Talk to Me, Grasshopper 107
Galifianakis from Between Two Ferns, complete with foliage for a backdrop. We 
warned you and wish you all the best. (And yes, we had this format in the 
early years of podcasting with Kent Nichols and Douglas Sarine’s Ask a Ninja,
archived at https://pfdbook.link/askaninja.) Of course, if you’re after 
irreverent material for your show and push that envelope as far as you can, 
your guests may not want to play along — especially if they don’t get the joke. 
If that’s the case, expect your guests to get up and walk away. Even in the 
most idyllic situations, guests can (and do) reserve the right to do that.
» Turning the interview into the Me show. Please remember that the 
spotlight belongs to your guest. Tee recalls a podcast where — no kidding — 
the three-person crew invited a guest on their writing podcast to talk about 
their books and their writing methodology, only to launch into a 15-minute 
discussion between themselves on an unrelated topic, leaving the guest on 
the other side of their mic. Silent. For 15 minutes.
Yes, it’s your podcast, but when a guest is introduced into the mix, you’re 
surrendering control of the show to that person. Let guests enjoy the 
spotlight; your audience will appreciate them for being there, which adds a 
new dimension to your feed. One way to avoid the “me factor” is to think of 
yourself as a liaison for the listener. Ask yourself, “As a listener, what ques￾tions would I ask or what information would I be looking for from the guest?”
» Being disrespectful to your guests. It has happened to Tee, both as an 
interview subject and as an interviewer. He’s answered some questions that 
made him uncomfortable, and then requested the podcasters to please edit 
out the question and related awkward response. In most cases, his request 
was respected. The others who didn’t edit their podcasts? Well, he no longer 
fields queries from them. Tee extends the same courtesy to his guests. 
Interview subjects talk to their friends and you want them to speak positively of 
you and your podcast. Show them respect, and your guests will do the same.
Consider sharing your list of questions with the interviewee before the recording 
session. This gives your guest time to prepare their thoughts and might save you 
a lot of editing later.
Feelin’ the synergy
One final note on preparing for interviews: We’ve heard some guests say, “I’m 
doing these interviewers a favor by going on their show.” And we’ve been told by 
other show hosts, “We’re doing you a great favor with this chance to showcase 
your work on our show.” Both opinions are subjective.
The host and the guest are working together to create a synergy. The interviewer 
has a chance to earn a wider audience and display mastery of journalistic 108 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
techniques. The guest has a chance to get in the public eye, stay in the public eye, 
and talk about the next big thing coming in sight of said public eye. The host and 
the guest form a seamless promotional machine for one another. And if you’re 
lucky, the interview subject might take complete control of your show, which is 
what actor Lani Tupu did with Tee in a recording session captured in Figure 6-3. 
This interplay reflected the trust as well as the fun that the Farscape actor was 
having alongside Tee.
If your format allows it, ask your guest for an ID that you can drop in from time to 
time. You’ve probably heard these before on radio stations “Hi, this is Rex Kramer, 
danger seeker. You may remember me from such films as Airplane and Kentucky 
Fried Movie. You’re listening to the Shameless Self-Promoting Podcast.” If the 
interview guests want to be creative, let them. These IDs are a great self-promotion 
tool, a lot of fun, and a way to remind your listener of previous accomplishments. 
Remember to ask politely, and be aware that not everyone will (or can) comply.
Recording Interviews
Unless you’re conducting an interview in person, your podcast just got more com￾plicated technically. You need to have the appropriate hardware and software to 
record your interview. Software solutions typically cost less and are not as 
FIGURE 6-3:

created between 
interview subject 
and host, you can 
ramp up the fun 
and set aside the 
prepared 
questions while 
you riff.CHAPTER 6 Interview-Fu: Talk to Me, Grasshopper 109
complex to set up and use as hardware solutions. However, a software solution 
can put more load on the computer’s CPU, and if the CPU is too busy, it can affect 
the quality of the recording. We cover both software and hardware options in this 
section.
There are legal restrictions concerning the recording of telephone calls and video￾conferencing calls (such as Zoom), and these restrictions vary from country to 
country, state to state, and region to region. A quick web search for terms such as 
regulations for recording conversations should turn up several useful results. 
Compliance with these laws is the responsibility of the podcaster. Before record￾ing a call, ask for permission or, better yet, get it in writing (search for recording 
consent form).
Recording using Zoom
One of the most popular options for making calls and recording interviews is Zoom
(https://pfdbook.link/zoom), which is available for Windows, Mac, iOS, 
Android, and Linux. Zoom can be run as a downloadable client or from their web￾site. However, if you want to record your interview, you need to install the client 
on Windows or Mac.
Check your recording settings on the Zoom website under Settings ➪ Recording 
before starting Zoom. If you’re using a corporate Zoom account, you be able to 
record in the cloud but not locally.
Recording a Zoom call is simple:
1. Download and install the Zoom client at https://pfdbook.link/
zoomdownload.
2. Launch Zoom on your device. Create an account or sign in if you haven’t 
already.
Zoom offers free accounts but limits the meeting length to 40 minutes. If you 
need more time, want to record to the cloud, or need other advanced features, 
investigate the paid tiers.
3. Click the Schedule button and enter the details of your meeting (see 
Figure 6-4):
a. In the Topic field, give your meeting a meaningful name.
b. In the Date & Time fields, enter the start and end date/time for your meeting. 
Additionally, set the time zone to avoid confusion.
c. In the Attendees field, enter your guest’s email. For now, you can skip over the 
options in the sections labeled Meeting ID, Security, and Encryption.110 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
d. In the Video section, choose the appropriate options to determine whether you 
want the video on or off for you (Host) and your guest (Participant). Scroll down 
for more options.
e. Leave the Audio section as-is with the setting Telephone and Computer Audio.
f. In the Calendar section, choose which calendar type you want to send the 
invite to.
4. Click Send.
Congratulations, you’ve scheduled the meeting and sent a calendar invite to 
your attendee(s). Now sit back and wait for the momentous occasion to arrive.
5. When the meeting time arrives, click Start on the main Zoom screen.
6. Click the Record button, located on the bottom toolbar.
Your guests will be greeted by a message indicating that the session is being 
recorded.

7. At the end of your session, click the red End button in the lower-right 
corner and then click End Meeting for All.
If you don’t see the toolbar at the bottom, move your cursor over the Zoom 
window and it should appear.
After you’ve ended the session, Zoom’s servers may take a few minutes to save 
your recording (if you have recorded to the cloud). You’ll get an email when 
your recording is available.
8. To retrieve your recording, follow these steps:
a. Log in to the Zoom home page (https://pfdbook.link/zoom).
b. From the menu on the left, click Recordings.
c. In the list of recordings, click the meeting name.
d. Hover your cursor over the Audio Only list entry.
e. Locate and click the download icon (downward-pointing arrow), which appears 
between Audio Only and the file size. Optionally, if you want the video, 
download the Speaker view or Gallery view files.
9. Open the recording and verify it worked.
The recording is available in your computer’s Downloads directory. The video 
file is saved in the MP4 format and the audio file is saved in the M4A format.
Having each meeting attendee recorded on their own track enables you to adjust 
volume levels independently and mute unwanted background noises (such as your 
dog barking while your guest is talking). Zoom’s paid subscribers have an option 
under Settings ➪ Recordings to record each attendee to their own track. This can 
be configured only by logging into the Zoom website and is not available in the 
desktop client. Just be aware that the files are M4A files and may need to be con￾verted to work with your software.

Recording using hardware
If recording using software has your head spinning, using hardware, such as a 
physical mixer, may simplify things. The input source can be a microphone, a 114 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
computer, an MP3 player, an electronic keyboard, or just about anything with an 
audio output to feed into the mixer.
Some mixers, such as the RØDECaster Pro II and RØDECaster Pro Duo (see 
Chapter 2) even have Bluetooth so you can pair your phone without a wired con￾nection! If your mixer doesn’t include a Bluetooth option, you’ll need to use a 
technique known as mix-minus. Mix-minus enables you to use your audio mixer 
to create a second audio-output mix containing all sources except the connected 
computer providing the call to your guest(s). That second audio-output mix is 
then sent back to your guest(s). The mix is everything minus one source  — 
theirs — hence the name. With this approach your guests don’t hear an echo of 
their voice. For a short video tutorial, go to https://pfdbook.link/mixminus.
As shown in Figure 6-6, your input as the interview host comes from the micro￾phone while your guests’ audio comes from a computer running meeting software 
such as Zoom. The output needs a destination, such as a recording device like the 
Zoom H4n.
Figure 6-6 looks a bit challenging, but it’s not that complex. Whether you have a 
basic four-channel mixer or something the size of a cruise ship, most mixers have 
the same basic layout and features. Plugging in a microphone to a single audio 
channel is straightforward. Adding the stereo output of a destination device (a 
second computer, a digital recorder, and so on) is also simple, provided you have 
the right connecting cables.
FIGURE 6-6:
To prevent your 
guests from 
hearing an echo 
of their voice, you 
need a mix-minus 
setup.CHAPTER 6 Interview-Fu: Talk to Me, Grasshopper 115
The real fun begins when you need to get your output back to the call-in computer 
and the recording device. To do so, you need to take advantage of your mixer’s Aux 
Send (sometimes called Effects End) port, which is an output port that you will feed 
back to your call-in machine. So the question now becomes, how do you get your 
microphone input sent out the Aux Send?
Most mixers have the capability to create multiple mixes. The main mix is what 
you typically record, but often hidden mixers, called buses, let you create an alter￾native mix. How cool is that? You thought you were just buying one mixer, and 
you get another one (or more) for free! To make use of this additional output mix, 
locate the row of (often red) knobs on your mixer labeled Aux. (If your mixer has 
more than one row of Aux knobs, each row corresponds to a separate Aux Send 
channel.) These knobs are the volume controls for your Aux Send. If you turn up 
the volume on your microphone, whatever is connected to listen to the Aux Send 
output will hear it. By turning the input for the call-in computer on Aux all the 
way down, your guest will hear you clearly but won’t hear themselves.
Prepping Your Green Room for Guests
A podcast guest could be your dad, your mother-in-law, your best friend, or a 
person on the street. It could be the friend of a friend who can get you on the 
phone with your favorite author, actor, or athlete. Regardless of who the guest is, 
you have a second party to worry about when you conduct an interview.
Removing the technical difficulties element means taking the show to the guests 
or bringing the guests to the show. This kind of interview not only is the most fun 
to do but also gives you direct contact with the subject so you can observe body 
language, facial expressions, and reactions to questions and answers.
Welcoming in-studio guests
When guests visit your facility — which is probably a spare room in your house — 
make them feel at home. Offer them something to drink. Take them on a tour of 
your humble abode. Introduce them to your family. The point is to be polite. You 
don’t have to cook dinner for them, but offering a hint of hospitality, be it a glass 
of water (or a beer, if you’ve ever worked with Chuck or Tee), is a nice touch.
It’s also a good idea to get your home and yourself ready to receive guests. Sure, 
Tee has recorded quite a few podcasts in his pajamas, but because he’s working 
with his wife or recording short stories for his podcast, he’s allowed. If fantasy 
and science fiction authors Elizabeth Bear and Scott Lynch ever come over to his 116 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
house for an interview, he wouldn’t be greeting them in his Avenger jammies and 
Stone Brewery slippers.
Okay, maybe he would greet them wearing the Stone slippers, but he would be 
bathed and dressed and have his teeth brushed and hair combed. The key word 
here is guest. Treat them as such. Be cool, be pleasant, be nice. And if you’re a 
guest on someone else’s podcast, the same rules apply. Don’t prop your feet on the 
furniture, don’t demand hospitality, and don’t be a jerk.
The in-studio visit is an audition for both guest and host. If the guest is abrasive, 
abusive, and just plain rude, chances are good that the guest will never be invited 
back. If a host asks unapproved questions, continues to pry into personal matters 
that have nothing to do with the interview, or seems determined to take over the 
interview spotlight as if trying to impress the guest, said guest may never return.
Meeting guests on their own turf
Be cool, be pleasant, be nice. These same rules apply when you take your podcast 
on the road. You may find yourself at a person’s home or place of business, or 
some neutral place. If your meeting was not prearranged, you’re now practicing 
guerrilla journalism. Ambushing unsuspecting people with questions that may not 
strike you as hard and probing but could be to people who don’t expect them. 
Respect your guests, wherever you are when the interview takes place.
One approach for getting good interviews is to ask for permission to interview 
them, whether they’re passersby or experts at their place of business. Shoving a 
microphone in someone’s face and blurting out a question is hardly a great way to 
introduce yourself and your podcast to the world. If the guest you want to inter￾view has a handler or liaison, it’s good protocol to follow the suggestions and 
advice of the guest’s staff.
If you start out with a warm, welcoming smile and explain what you’re doing and 
why, most people open up and are happy to talk.
When interviewing people on the street or in the moment, it’s helpful to identify 
yourself. Michael Butler of The Rock and Roll Geek Show (https://pfdbook.link/
rnrgeek) uses a mic cube around his microphone, also called a mic flag (see 
Figure 6-7). The classic cube usually has a logo identifying the network, show, or 
organization affiliated with the interviewer. You can find mic cubes online 
(unprinted) starting around $25.CHAPTER 6 Interview-Fu: Talk to Me, Grasshopper 117
Whatever your setup, test your equipment before recording an interview. You’re 
out of the controlled environment of your home studio and have to deal with 
ambient noise and uncontrollable background variables. Set up your equipment; 
power up your laptop, mixing board, and mics; and record a few words. Then play 
back your tests and set your levels accordingly.
Ensuring Trouble-Free Recordings
When it comes to recording conversations, here are a few points to keep in mind 
before asking the first question:
» Get permission to record conversations, even if the interview is prear￾ranged. Laws (both federal and state) prohibit the recording of conversations 
without permission, and further restrictions limit broadcasting these conver￾sations. If you plan to record and publish a conversation, get the subject’s 
consent for both beforehand, both verbally and in written communication 
(even email) to make sure your legal issues are covered.
» Test the calling equipment. If you’ve arranged an interview with someone 
for your podcast — say, a favorite musician or politician — prepare for the 
interview. Run a sound check and make sure the recording setup works and 
sounds good.
The bandwidth demand increases the more people you conference through 
your computer. Reception will be affected, so if you know more than one 
person will be involved in the interview, test how many people you can 
FIGURE 6-7:
A mic flag is a 
great way to 
show your brand, 
either on camera 
or on the street, 
when interview￾ing people.118 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
effectively conference. Off-loading the recording to a cloud service such as 
StreamYard can be beneficial.
» Check your batteries. If you’re using a portable recorder (such as the Zoom 
H4n Pro), make sure the batteries are charged and you have spares. (Check 
the spares, too.) Similarly, when recording at home, if you live in an environ￾ment with periodic electrical problems, consider picking up an uninterrupt￾able power supply (or UPS) in case your main power cuts out.
» Check your storage space. Hard drives and solid state devices are getting 
bigger and cheaper, but they don’t have infinite storage space. Audio files can 
be big — especially if you’re recording to a raw format such as WAV or AIFF. If 
you run out of space in the middle of recording an interview, you waste time, 
interrupt the pace, and lose face with your interviewee. If you’re recording 
to a portable recording device, it’s basically the same idea. Know how much 
storage you have, in megabytes or gigabytes, and how long you can record 
at your current bit rate. (We talk more about bit rates in Chapter 9.)
This may sound pessimistic, but be ready for things to go wrong. A guest might 
not show up for an interview. Your new high-tech toy or other technology, if not 
given a proper pre-interview shakedown, may not come through. Prepare to have 
plenty of topics to discuss on your own. Have another recorder running as a sim￾ple backup. Plan for the worst, and you can celebrate like a pro when that incred￾ible interview goes off without a hitch.
After you’ve finished your recording, and perhaps completed your editing, let your 
guest preview the episode before it goes public. This courtesy prevents issues 
later, if a guest wants you to delete some bits. Offer guests a few days or weeks to 
say “Can you make one more change?” or “I love it!”CHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 119
Chapter 7
So What Are You Waiting 
For? Record, Already!
Okay, you’ve most likely checked out the hardware and software gadgets 
mentioned in Chapters  2,  3, and  4. In this chapter, we make the bold 
assumption that you’ve purchased your hardware, hooked it up according 
to the supplied documentation (you did read the documentation, right?), down￾loaded your software, and have everything ready to record. This is it! A micro￾phone is pointing in the direction of your mouth, and you are eagerly waiting to 
begin podcasting.
Okay then. What’s stopping you?
Perhaps you have no clue how you sound on your recording equipment, or maybe 
you’re trying to understand why your smooth and sultry voice sounds like you just 
finished a dozen espressos. The problem could be in your audio application’s 
sound settings. Too much pep in the voice, and you sound like Rob Zombie croon￾ing a goth’s delight. Too little amplification, and you’d be better off going to your 
front porch and shouting your podcast’s content.
Before the podcast gets underway with a click or touch of the record button, you 
must set levels. That’s a fancy-schmancy way to say “fiddling with knobs and slid￾ers” on your mixing board or your audio-editing software. Setting levels ensures 
that the signal you’re sending through the microphone is loud and clear. Once the 
technical side is running smoothly, you need to give some thought to timing and 
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Setting levels and parameters
» Capturing ambient sound
» Pacing and clock management
» Going off on tangents
» Recording your first podcast120 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
enunciation because how you say your message affects the listeners’ interest in 
what you’re saying.
In this chapter, you learn how to set audio levels, modify your environment 
to capture the best sound. You also find out about pacing, clock management, 
and more.
Did Your Sound Check Clear the Bank?
If you show up early enough for a rock concert, you’ll see roadies setting up 
microphones, playfully waving to the crowd as they speak quickly into a micro￾phone “Check one, check two, check-check-check!” It’s a staple for rock ‘n’ roll￾ers to do a mic check because the fans expect a good performance.
With podcasting, your own mic check should be more involved than saying the 
same word a few times. We recommend that you perform an audio diagnostic just 
to assure yourself (and, if applicable, your guests) that the equipment is working 
and sound is, in fact, reaching your computer. The goal is not only to confirm that 
your mic is picking up sound but also to check the volume of the voices — yours 
and that of whoever else is involved in the podcast.
To test your audio levels, say words that you plan to say in your episode as you 
would actually say them. Also play the sound effects or music that you will use. If 
you have guests, have them do a bit of their introduction. Don’t settle for “how’s 
this?” Get more! Be sure to take your equipment for a test drive before every 
podcast.
Understanding dB levels
Setting levels is easy, provided you know where the decibel (dB) input levels are 
displayed in the software. The decibel unit expresses the intensity of sound, begin￾ning at −9 dB for the least perceptible sound to approximately 130 for an intensely 
loud sound level. Your readout measures how hot you are (in this instance, that’s 
the power of your voice, not how good you look) on the microphone. Audio signal 
strength (measured in decibels) is the amount of power that goes into the signal, 
which affects how clearly it can be heard and how hard it hits the ears. “Loud and 
clear” is good; too much signal strength causes distortion, and that’s a pain to 
listen to.
In vintage radio and audio equipment, a dB signal was represented in a volume unit
(VU) meter — the little needle that bounced in response to your voice. Later, the 
needle was replaced by lights that react to your voice or to sounds around you, CHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 121
going from green to yellow to orange to red. What the lights represent is easy to 
translate:
» Green: Well, I can hear you, but wow, are you quiet!
» Yellow-orange: You’re coming in loud and clear.
» Red: You’re in danger of hitting the distortion level (clipping).
» Red with double bars: Your audio will sound distorted.
Volume meters appear differently in different hardware and software (Figure 7-1 
shows the volume meter display for Audacity), but they all serve the same 
purpose: to make sure your content is heard clearly. Your aim, as you speak and 
monitor the volume meters, is to have peak maximum dB levels in the high level 
(low red) without lighting up the double bars. When you attain that average, your 
voice is rising and falling within a good balanced dynamic range. The optimal 
upper range for your voice is in the yellow and orange range — with only the high 
points (when you raise your voice) going into the red occasionally. You can always 
bring up the low spots with some simple post-production tips (described later in 
this chapter).
Because microphones, audio-capture cards, and mixing boards all work at differ￾ent sensitivity levels, it’s best to test the level of your voice before you record. If 
your levels are in the green, you’re loud enough to be heard by your equipment but 
still so soft that people will have to crank up the volume on their computer or 
mobile device, consequently blowing out their eardrums when they listen to a dif￾ferent podcast or playlist.
FIGURE 7-1:
Audacity’s input 
level meters (in 
the upper-right 
corner) respond 
to your voice and 
enable you to 
monitor how loud 
you are when 
recording.122 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
If your recording is too loud (or hot), it tends to cause problems. Listeners have 
their players set for a pleasant, comfortable volume — and suddenly your show 
begins with guns blazing and bagpipes blaring. As your levels reach deep into the 
red, listeners fumble for their players and try to turn down the sound so the pro￾gram can be understandable through its own distortion. Sadly, the modulation is 
such a problem that your voice crackles and growls as it tramples the volume 
limits of your recording equipment, even at the lower volume. Even worse, some 
programs and microphones will cut the signal from the mic for a second, leaving 
you with chunks of missing audio — and you may not discover this until after 
your precious recording is over!
You want to avoid clipping to prevent that distorted sound. Clipping occurs when 
you’re feeding too much signal to your equipment. It can come from many sources, 
including incorrect settings on a microphone or other audio input, incorrect set￾tings on a mixer, or your software preferences. Once audio is clipped, you can’t get 
the original (or intended) quality back.
In achieving a balance in your audio, you could spend the day setting and resetting 
those levels in quest of dB nirvana. That’s time spent but not well spent. It makes 
more sense to practice till you get a pretty good sense of your best working level, 
and then get comfortable speaking into a mic at that level. Time to go mano-a￾mano with setting the levels.
Setting your levels
Your mission, should you choose to accept it, is to keep your levels in the neigh￾borhood of 0 dB, dipping and spiking when necessary. For podcasting, consistency 
is key. Make sure the mixer, if you have one, is turned on, the mic is plugged in 
and turned on, and the software is running. Now follow these steps to check levels:
CALLING IN THE CREW
It’d be great to set your levels so your lights remain at 0 dB, but recording is a real-world 
activity. Sometimes your meter may never reach yellow, and other times it might hit the 
double red bars. If your goal is to maintain perfect levels from the beginning to the end 
of a recording session, an audio crew can work with you in rehearsal and performance, 
adjusting levels when you go loud and when you go soft.
The downside of hiring a crew is the added expense to producing your podcast. Even 
if the crew is a collection of audio geek friends, you’ll still have to pay for all that pizza 
every time you record.CHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 123
1. Begin talking into the mic about your podcast topic or plans.
You can do a scripted test read or talk off the top of your head, but be sure to 
speak in the manner and mood of your podcast.
Instead of speaking to thin air in the vague direction of the mic (or the person 
next to you), speak in the exact direction of the mic (as shown in Figure 7-2). 
Even if the mic is omnidirectional (meaning it picks up sounds from all 
directions at once), it can pick up your voice better this way.
If needed, adjust your microphone stand or boom mic arm so you can address 
your mic at the optimal angle. Boom mic arms, like the one included with MXL’s 
Overstream bundle, tend to be the easiest to adjust.
If you speak directly into the microphone, you might hear some sounds — 
particularly ps and bs — causing an effect known as popping. This issue can be 
fixed with an investment of less than $20 in a windscreen or pop filter, a foam 
or nylon filter that stops the wind made by your mouth from hitting the 
microphone’s diaphragm and causing distortion.
2. While you’re talking (or if you’re monitoring by playing back your test 
takes), keep an eye on your dB levels on the computer screen and adjust 
them as necessary.
If the levels are spiking into the red or double-red area or remaining in the 
green, check your input volume settings on your mixing board or audio-editing 
software. This might require a bit of multitasking on your part — continue to 
adjust the input levels and the distance of your mouth to the microphone while 
talking and watching the input meters.
FIGURE 7-2:
Set your 
microphone at a 
comfortable 
distance, close 
enough to 
overpower 
ambient noise 
but far enough to 
avoid contact 
with the 
microphone.124 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
3. Re-record your voice at the new settings.
Try to speak in the same manner and inflection as you did on the first 
recording.
4. As you review the second take, watch the dB input levels and adjust 
accordingly.
When in doubt, err on the side of caution. Record your volume a little low. You 
can always correct it in post-production. One way to increase the volume later, 
using Audacity, is to select your audio and use the Effect ➪ Amplify feature to 
bring it up.
5. Repeat Steps 3 and 4 until you’re happy with the results.
After you set your levels, make a note of the settings somewhere, such as in a doc￾ument on your computer, in a notes app on your smartphone, or on its retro 
ancestor — a legal pad and pen. Having a backup copy is useful in case your pref￾erences are lost or fiddled out of whack by someone’s child or best friend playing 
spaceship in your studio. (Yes, even adults enjoy playing spaceship with 
super-cool-looking equipment!) That way, you’ll always have your last known 
settings to reference.
THERE’S A RIGHT WAY AND 
A WRONG WAY . . .
Time for a confession.
When Tee got his MXL990, it was his first studio condenser microphone. He was so 
excited about this microphone that he failed to check the small slip of paper included in 
the package that told him how to talk into it. Perhaps it was the excitement of venturing 
into new frontiers of creativity that made him hook up the equipment without reading 
the directions. Maybe it was a bold assumption that he knew what end to talk into with 
a mic. Whatever the reason, Tee was speaking into the microphone incorrectly (left 
image). His friends in the podosphere, on seeing Tee in action, didn’t have the heart 
to tell him so — or didn’t know any better themselves.
Fortunately, Evo Terra (https://pfdbook.link/evo) is a heartless evil mastermind. 
He told Tee he was doing it incorrectly as any heartless evil mastermind would — on a 
podcast, naturally.CHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 125
Noises Off: Capturing Ambient Noise
Part of the charm that is podcasting is just how varied the content is, as well as 
how spontaneous the shows tend to be when the record button is pressed. Some 
podcasters believe that in a true podcast (whatever that is!), the podcaster must 
record everything in one take and deliver the content unedited. This supposed 
mark of authenticity includes any background noise (also called ambient noise) 
you happen to capture while recording — from comforting sounds like rustling 
trees and birdcalls to the more grating ones like pounding car stereos and 
jackhammers.
Hey, if that’s the style of your show, great. However, if you’re trying to take 
listeners to a place in their imagination, read on to find out how to reduce or 
eliminate ambient noise.
All microphones do not behave the same, and condenser microphones work best when 
speaking into them properly, from the side (right image). Tee, reflecting on Evo’s “kind” 
advice, positioned the mic accordingly and continues to do so. When hooking up micro￾phones, refer to the enclosed documentation for additional and essential information 
on getting the best sound out of your microphone.
In short, understand whether your microphone is end addressable, where you talk into 
the end opposite the cable, or side addressable, where you speak into the side. Then you 
can avoid the embarrassment (caught forever) that published photographs may deliver 
upon you . . . regardless of how good your content is. And when in doubt, read any doc￾umentation included with your new gear. It might save you some aggravation and a lit￾tle embarrassment.126 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Identifying ambient noise
As we discuss in Chapter 8, how much you edit depends on what kind of content 
you’re presenting. For example, if you’re doing an off-the-cuff, off-the-wall 
podcast about your life and a typical day in it, you might just grab the VideoMic 
Me-C, plug it into your mobile phone, and head out the door, recording every step 
along the way. This kind of podcast can be (note we said can be) the easiest to 
record. You’re podcasting a slice of Americana, or Britanniana, if you’re in the 
United Kingdom, or Kiwiana if podcasting from the land of hobbits, championship 
rugby, and pavlova. Especially if your goal is to capture the look and feel of your 
culture, ambient noise is not only welcomed but desired — up to a point.
Some podcasters cringe at the mere mention of ambient noise — ambient noise 
like . . . well, what was in Tee’s first podcast. When he podcasted MOREVI: The 
Chronicles of Rafe & Askana back in 2005, he worked to create a magical setting with 
voice, story, music, special effects — a world that was shattered by real-world 
interference such as school buses, kids at recess, UPS trucks, the Virginia com￾muter rail system, and air traffic from two nearby airports. Even if you love the 
source of the ambient noise, sometimes it just doesn’t fit what the podcast is try￾ing to do.
The best noise reduction happens in pre-production. If your podcast could do 
without input from the outside world, strategically schedule recording sessions. 
Pay attention to your environment and record when it’s quiet. You might find that 
the best time is late at night, when traffic is lighter, the kids are in bed, and con￾struction crews aren’t working. For others, the best time is mid-day, when noisy 
neighbors are away at their day jobs. Being attentive and adapting your recording 
sessions can add up to less ambient noise and fewer takes on the mic.
Minimizing ambient noise
Some creativity is in order if you want to reduce the intrusion of the outside world, 
record anytime during the day, and still maintain a budget.
There isn’t an easy solution to podcasting in a noisy world. One not-so-cost￾effective answer is to rent a studio and record your podcast there. Unless you have 
a sponsor who bankrolls the costs, your hobby could easily max out credit cards 
and cast a hungry eye on your nest egg. (Let’s not even go there.)
A somewhat-less-expensive option is to soundproof your home-based recording 
room. This may sound simple, but it can involve a lot of home improvement before 
you have one room where you can be sure the only sound is yours. In 2021, Chuck 
converted a bedroom into a home studio for audio and video podcasting.CHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 127
First, he purchased blackout (room darkening) window shades to ensure that he 
could light the room and the people in it directly from the studio lights — even on 
cool winter mornings when the sun is trying to blast straight at the camera. Next, 
to control the ambient sound, he installed wall-to-wall carpeting. It’s amazing 
how much sound is absorbed simply by adding carpet to a room. If you love your 
hardwood floors, buy area rugs instead to at least dampen the echo.
However, the floor isn’t the only place that reflects sound. Chuck also covered the 
ceiling (see Figure 7-3) in soundproofing foam tiles. If he had been doing audio￾only podcasts, he would have covered all the walls as well. Instead, he treated the 
walls off-camera with a combination of soundproofing foam and cloth — don’t 
underestimate the value of simply hanging a duvet on the wall.
Sometimes the simplest option is the best. If you’re a solo audio podcaster, an 
easy way to dampen room echo is to put a blanket over your head and mic while 
you record. It’s a cheap DIY solution provided you can still keep an eye on things 
such as your audio levels.
Renovation, especially if you’re a fan of DIY projects, makes this kind of home￾built studio a possibility, but may not be a practical solution for all homeowners. 
And this kind of aggressive home improvement is seriously frowned upon if you’re 
renting an apartment.
You can keep this type of home renovation affordable and within your lease agree￾ment terms with the following:
» Stuff towels under the door. It decreases the amount of sound inside your 
house that can filter into your recording area.
FIGURE 7-3:
Chuck’s use of 
acoustic 
soundproofing 
foam tile on the 
ceiling helps 
control the sound 
in his studio.128 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
» Keep the microphone as far away from your computer as possible. If the 
computer’s fan is audible, it can be a noticeable distraction and take away 
from the ambiance you’re trying to create.
» Turn off any ceiling fans, floor heaters, air conditioners, or room 
ionizers. With fewer appliances running, you have less chance of additional 
ambient sound being created.
» If you encounter ambient noise that you don’t want in your podcast, 
wait a few moments. Pause until the noise subsides, and then pick up your 
podcast a few lines before the interruption. That’s for the sake of post-production: 
With a substantial gap in your podcast activity, you can easily narrow down 
where your edits are needed.
When noise interferes with your podcast, leaving gaps of silence so you know 
where to edit isn’t a foolproof method. Always set aside enough time to listen to 
your podcast — and really listen, not just play it back while you clean the office or 
call a friend. Make sure the levels are even, no segments are repeated, and the 
final product is ready for uploading and posting.
When recording a podcast on location, make sure the ambiance  — be it a 
particularly busy crosswalk at a street corner, a frequented bar, or backstage at a 
concert  — doesn’t overwhelm your voice. Background noise belongs in (well, 
yeah) the background. Its intent is to set a tone for the podcast, not to become the 
podcast itself. If possible, do a few test recordings in the space to see how much 
volume you’ll need and how close you should be to the mic.
Take Your Time and Hurry Up: Pacing 
and Clock Management
Podcasts, whether short and sweet or epic and ambitious, all share something in 
common: the need for pacing. As a rule, you don’t want to blurt out the aim or 
intent of your podcast in the opening 5 minutes and then pad the remaining 10 or 
15 with fluff. Nor do you want to drone on and on until suddenly you have to rush 
into why you’re podcasting on this particular day about this particular topic. Give 
yourself ample time to set the mood comfortably and competently and make it to 
the intent of the podcast without dawdling. Respect your listeners’ time and don’t 
overstay your welcome.
The trick in pacing is to understand how much time you have to get your message 
across. Where to start?CHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 129
Find a clock, watch, or stopwatch and for 1 minute (and only 1 minute) sit quietly 
and do nothing, say nothing, and remain perfectly still. That 1 minute will feel like 
a short eternity. Now imagine that 1-minute times 15. (No, no, no — do not repeat 
this exercise for 15 minutes. There’s a fine line between an exercise and a 
complete waste of time.) You can see that 15 minutes gives you ample time to get 
to the message of your podcast. The journey you take your listeners on doesn’t 
necessarily have to occupy all that time — be it 15, 20, or 30 minutes — but you 
have time to play. That’s the most important thing to remember as you set your 
best podcasting pace.
Take the potato out of your 
mouth and enunciate
It isn’t out of the ordinary to fire up the mic and launch into your podcast with 
enough energy and vitality to power a small shire in England. Nothing wrong with 
that — until you go back and listen to yourself. Your words, phrases, and thoughts 
will be running together and forming one turbulent, muddy stream-of-thought. 
You’re having a tough time understanding what the podcast is about and what the 
podcaster is saying — and you’re the podcaster!
One way to get a grip on pacing yourself through a podcast is by enunciating — 
pronouncing your words distinctly, explaining your topic clearly — and there’s no 
better way to do that than to slow your speech slightly and listen carefully to cer￾tain consonants (for example, the t, s, and d sounds). Proper enunciation can help 
you set a comfortable pace and keep you from rushing through your presentation. 
In fact, it isn’t a bad idea to over-enunciate. In the excitement of recording, over￾enunciation forces you to slow down, clean up your pronunciation, and make your 
voice easily understandable. If you think you’re speaking just a little too slowly, 
you’re probably going just the right speed. (However, if a rapid pace is part of your 
presentation, stick with it.)
PODCASTING GOES PRO
Broadcasting networks such as CNN, FOX News, BBC, and others often take to the 
podosphere as a way to market their shows and reach a worldwide audience. Along 
with talk-radio personalities, other major players in broadcasting such as NPR, ESPN, 
Disney, and ABC use podcasting as a way to reach audiences that may have missed 
their original broadcasts. One of Chuck’s favorites is a show from “the other ABC,” the 
Australian Broadcasting Corporation, called Dr. Karl on TripleJ. Tee enjoys NPR’s Note to 
Self and Mike Rowe’s The Way I Heard It, both of which were recommended to him by his 
podcasting app. Thanks to podcasting, all of us can learn more about bee stings, double 
rainbows, and tidal waves on our own schedules.130 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Speaking a few tongue-twisters before going on mic helps your enunciation and 
warms up your voice. Here are some easy ones that emphasize the problem con￾sonants that are lost when speaking too quickly:
» Toot the tin trumpet, Tommy, in time.
» Pop prickly pickles past the peck of parsley.
» See sand slip silently through the sunlit seals.
» The sixth sheik’s sheep is sick.
» Will Wendy remember wrecking the white rocker?
If you perform a search online for tongue twisters, you can find a wide variety of 
classic and original warm-ups for the voice. Or compose your own.
And now let’s take a break for 
station identification
Ever notice that certain podcasts suddenly break in with a show ID or take some 
other marked break and then play their sponsors’ spots (if they’re lucky enough 
to have sponsors)? They’re trying to emulate — and maybe make themselves eas￾ily syndicated by  — conventional radio. You know, the broadcasting industry. 
Clock management is the careful, standardized interruption of a typical radio 
broadcast — usually every 15 minutes in a 1-hour slot — for such necessities as 
station identification, show identification, public-service announcements (PSAs), 
and (of course) commercials.
Another nice bonus of following clock management is that you have several points 
in your show to give a quick show ID (something like “You’re listening to Tech￾norama with Chuck and Kreg") to let people know who you are. Chuck and Kreg 
like to collect audio clips from celebrities for their show IDs because, as seen in 
Figure 7-4, astronauts are cool! This show ID is a great way to catch the attention 
of people who may be tuning in to the middle of your podcast. Although show IDs 
are usually expected at the top of the hour and on the half-hour, you can drop in 
a show ID anytime you want.
The following is a good general timetable for clock management for a 30-minute 
show format:
» :00: Programming break, station identification, show ID, PSAs, advertisements
» :10: Programming break, PSAs, advertisements
» :20: Programming break, station identification, show ID, PSAs, advertisements
» :30: Programming break, PSAs, advertisementsCHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 131
These breaks vary in length but can last anywhere from 30 seconds to 5 minutes. No 
surprise that this is where the old-school podcasters get a bit restless. They find 
clock management and show ID redundant, if not frivolous. Why bother with a show 
ID or clock management? People have downloaded your feed specifically. They know 
what they’re listening to, so what purpose does clock management serve?
It all comes back to what you see for your podcast’s future. If you intend to remain 
in the podosphere, it comes down to your format, your way. No constraints from 
the FCC. It’s just you, the microphone, your listeners, and throwing caution to the 
wind. However, if you set your sights on taking your passion project to the air￾waves, — AM, FM, satellite radio, or online streaming — you have to format your 
show to fit the standards.
Think about the possibilities of your podcast and where you see yourself with it 
several months (or years) down the road. Maybe a bit of management and organi￾zation will aid your podcast in jumping to the airwaves.
Concerning Tangents and Their 
Val — Oh, Look, a Butterfly!
It’s hardly surprising that broadcasters who have no experience in broadcasting 
tend to stray from their topic once the microphone goes hot, and then lose them￾selves in the thickets and thorns of tangents. Defensive podcasters claim that’s 
FIGURE 7-4:
Always have your 
recorder ready! 
Video game 
developer and 
astronaut Richard 
Garriott (left) 
graciously gave 
Chuck an audio 
ID for his podcast 
at Dragon Con 
2012.132 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
part of the charm of podcasting, but that charm fades as the topic gets closer to 
serious. If (for example) you launch a podcast dedicated to The Witcher series but 
wander into a discussion of the growing concern of television violence, don’t be 
surprised if your audience wanders away.
It’s worth repeating: Stay focused on the intent of your podcast. Remaining true 
to your podcast’s subject matter isn’t just about staying within your running time; 
it also makes clear to your listeners that, yes, you have a message and will deliver 
it. Your listeners have expectations based on your show’s published description or 
the verbal intro at the top of the episode. When people listen, you want them to 
feel assured that what they hear is what you’ve described.
Read on to find out how you can make tangents work and how to smoothly get 
back on topic after you’ve taken your side trip.
“Say, that reminds me of something . . .”
A tangent can be a creative opportunity. You don’t necessarily treat tangents as 
strictly verboten in podcasting. If your tangent is related to, or benefits, your topic, 
it can be effective and engaging.
Say your commentary begins with “Anyone notice how smartphones are no longer 
just phones but tiny personal digital assistants that can be easily monitored and 
hacked into?” You cover that for a bit, and then break off on a tangent about phone 
etiquette and the lack of manners it brings out in people. This tangent will keep 
your listeners engaged, which can count as much as staying on topic.
What if your podcast isn’t so structured, though? Nothing’s wrong with hitting 
record and forging ahead into the great unknown of the next 15 or 20 minutes, as 
long as you have an idea of where you want to go. Tangents are terrific in moder￾ation, but keep them at least (well, yeah) tangentially related to the subject matter 
of your podcast. If, say, your podcast is about movies but your review of Doctor 
Strange suddenly goes into the decline of the comic book industry (regardless of 
the onslaught of comic-books-to-film productions), you still have a sense of 
focus. However, if your review of Doctor Strange wanders into how your cat is 
throwing up hairballs every time you podcast or your car’s sunroof chose to stop 
working, your audience may grow frustrated enough to stop listening.
“But getting back to what 
I was saying earlier . . .”
When you’re podcasting for 15 or 20 minutes (or longer), it’s okay to take the 
scenic route with your discussion, but make sure you return to the point you CHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 133
wanted to make in this installment. The listener’s delight in podcasts is often the 
revelation that individuals who (in many cases) have never set foot in a recording 
studio can produce entertaining and even informative shows. Sometimes mean￾dering back to the point is part of that delight.
For instance, a podcast might begin with something in the headlines — say, a new 
marketing strategy launched by Apple for the iPhone to reach a wider client base. 
This can lead to a variety of topics in the discussion, such as the following:
» Smartphones that begin extending into other functionality, such as playing 
games on the PlayStation Network or providing Wi-Fi hotspots
» Costs for iPhone services from AT&T, Sprint, and other carriers
» Ways that carriers could bring down the costs of services
» Continued shortcomings and disappointments from the smartphone industry
But in the last 5 to 10 minutes, a simple segue like “So, to recap our thoughts on 
this bold marketing strategy from Apple” can successfully steer you, or you and 
your co-hosts, back to the aim and intent of your podcast. When you return 
deliberately from a tangent, your listening audience returns too and knows the 
destination as well as the scenery they went through to get there.
When it comes to staying on track, a script has a distinct advantage over an out￾line. (We hit on the topic of using a script versus an outline in Chapter 5.) And 
during an interview (see Chapter 6), having a list of questions is important, but 
don’t be afraid to ask impromptu questions — you can always go back to your 
prepared list.
THE NAME SAYS IT ALL
Unless you’re an established brand such as Joe Rogan or Dave Ramsey, your show 
name is your calling card. For example, with the Astronomy Cast podcast (https://
pfdbook.link/astronomycast), the name tells you right away what the show covers: 
galaxies, stars, gravity, black holes, and extra-solar planets. Once you have a topic for 
your show, the name should convey what the show is about.
