Getting Started with Audacity
Now it’s time to put that planning and practicing into action. To show you how to 
get started recording, we use Audacity (https://pfdbook.link/audacity). This 
free popular software package, which we introduce in Chapter  1, is shown in 
Figure 7-5.
FIGURE 7-5:
Audacity is 
popular among 
podcasters 
because it’s free 
and it makes it 
easy to get start 
recording.
(continued)CHAPTER 7 So What Are You Waiting For? Record, Already! 135
To record a podcast in Audacity, follow these simple steps:
1. Make sure that your microphone is plugged in.
The microphone has to be plugged in if you want Audacity to recognize it.
2. Download and install Audacity for your computer’s operating system 
(Windows, macOS, or Linux) from https://pfdbook.link/audacity.
3. Start the Audacity app.
4. Set your output and input devices:
a. Click the Audio Setup button and choose Playback Device from the menu that 
appears, and then choose the device to use to listen to your audio. This device is 
commonly your built-in speakers or headphone jack.
b. Click the Audio Setup button again and choose Recording Device. Then choose 
the input device you will use to record. The input device is usually your USB 
microphone or audio mixer. Note that other devices might have micro￾phones, such as webcams.
If you don’t see your audio device under Playback Device or Recording Device, 
double-check your connections and power to make sure everything is con￾nected. After you’ve inspected connections, click the Audio Setup button and 
select Rescan Audio Devices to see if the devices appear where you expect.
5. When you’re ready to record, click the red record button in the upper left.
Audacity creates a track and begins recording. As you speak your words of 
wisdom to the world, the meters move and a waveform is created.
Audacity comes with a terrific noise reduction function that attempts to 
digitally identify offending noise and remove it from the recording. To do this 
effectively and efficiently, though, Audacity needs to have a clean sample of the 
room’s natural audio (or background noise), called the noise floor (or “room 
tone”) in professional settings. At the beginning or end of your recording, 
record 5 to 10 seconds of your recording environment, leaving the mics open 
but saying nothing. Later, when you’re doing post-production (discussed in 
Chapter 8) you can use this sample with Audacity’s Noise Reduction feature (in 
the Effects menu). This method can reduce (if not completely remove) your 
background noise.
6. When you’re finished, click the square stop button in the upper left.
And just like that, you’ve recording a podcast. Now you know why so many pod￾casters love Audacity.CHAPTER 8 Cleanup, Podcast Aisle 8! 137
Chapter 8
Cleanup, Podcast Aisle 8!
Although a high-tech activity like podcasting doesn’t qualify as quaint, the 
charm of podcasting — whether produced by a major studio or by friends 
with a microphone and a laptop — is its inherent, homespun quality. Steve 
Jobs once described podcasting as the Wayne’s World of radio. (No kidding, he said 
that on ABC in 2005; go to https://pfdbook.link/jobs-podcasting.) Podcasts, 
much like the classic Saturday Night Live sketch depicted, are often done on a 
shoestring budget and recorded in one take with no editing. All the trip-ups and 
tangents are captured for posterity and sent to MP3 players everywhere. These 
unpolished moments are part of the grassroots appeal that podcasting is not only 
known for but prides itself on nurturing as new and innovative podcasters enter 
the podosphere.
It’s been our experience that many top-rated podcasting hosts know nothing 
about the editing process. However, it’s worthwhile to understand the ins and 
outs of a podcast production. Taking time to review and polish your podcast can 
make the difference between a listenable podcast and an incoherent mess of audio. 
For example, you might want to eliminate the sound of a train going by or silence 
a cough that would otherwise distract your listeners from a brilliant riposte. This 
chapter shows you how to use editing to shape your podcast while retaining its 
natural atmosphere, how to add depth to that atmosphere with music, and how to 
give the final touch to your podcast’s format with an introduction and an exit.
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Pondering “To edit, or not to edit”
» Adding bed music for a comfortable 
podcast
» Introducing intros and outros138 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
A Few Reasons to Consider Editing
Take a serious look at the mood you want to convey with your podcast. From 
there, you can judge how intense your editing workload will be. The following list 
covers several cases where you should consider adding editing to your podcast 
production workflow:
» Elevate your production quality. You can’t always get everything right on 
the first take — and sometimes not even on the second or third. Editing 
makes it sound as though you got even hard-to-pronounce names and 
tricky tongue twisters right on the first try. What’s more, you can drop in 
pre-produced clips with just the right amount of space before and after 
to give your podcast that polished feel.
» Remove boring material. You’ve probably watched a live show or listened 
to live audio in which the content gets dull or everyone runs out of things to 
say. The only thing worse than aimless rambling are deafening seconds of 
dead air. Listeners can fast forward, change the channel, or just turn it off. 
Podcasters can avoid this situation with editing. However, while taking out 
the boring bits, make sure you maintain continuity.
» Correct ambient noise. As we explain in Chapter 7, ambient noise is the 
natural and spontaneously occurring noise picked up when recording a 
podcast. If you’re recording a story or setting the stage for a historic drama 
and a passing siren or the rumble of a garbage truck makes it into your 
recording, that noise can distract the audience. Therefore, you should 
control and minimize ambient noise.
» Adjust the running time. Suppose that you just wrapped up your latest 
podcast with a great interview, but you have 90 minutes of interview for 
a 30-minute podcast. You could run the whole thing, unedited, but that 
might test the patience of your audience (as well as your bandwidth and file 
storage). Or you could split the interview into two 45-minute interviews — or 
three 30-minute interviews. In this approach to editing, everyone wins. Editing 
can increase your productivity by rescuing discussions and content that would 
otherwise be hard to shoehorn into one podcast.
» Clean up scripted material. Some podcasters argue that the true podcast is 
done in one take, but as podcasting matures as a medium, audiences become 
more demanding. Expectations for a podcast change when it includes scripted 
material. Unlike the option of elevating your production quality in the first bullet, 
editing is a necessity in these situations. With dramatic readings and productions, 
moments of “ah” and “um” should be edited out to maintain the clarity of the story 
as well as maintain the mood or atmosphere established in your reading.
With the popularity of storytelling in the podcast universe, professionalism 
and performance are the keys to a good product — and usually that means CHAPTER 8 Cleanup, Podcast Aisle 8! 139
(yep!) editing. Lots of it. If you feel that editing would mar the spontaneity 
of your podcast — but still want to present literature or other scripted 
material — ask yourself how good you think Avengers: Endgame would had 
been if Josh Brolin and Robert Downey, Jr. did that moment in one take. 
Imagine their dialogue sounding like this:
• Thanos: I am . . . uuuhhhhhh . . . inevitable. Yeah.
• Tony Stark: Ummm . . . and I *sneezes suddenly — sniffle sniffle* . . . am Iron 
*ahem* Man.
Not what we would call riveting. With scripted material, editing is a must.
Do you want to throw the book across the room, screaming “These jerks just 
spoiled Avengers: Endgame”? Come on, it came out in 2019.
Editing with Audacity
Editing out breaks, stammers, and trip-ups may sound easy, but there’s a science 
to it. If you cut off too much from a clip, one word comes right on top of another, 
and you sound unnatural. If you don’t cut off enough, pauses last too long between 
thoughts.
When you’re editing audio, the key is to review, review, review. When you’ve 
completed your first pass of edits, play the episode again. Listen without distrac￾tion, and keep an ear out for any odd edits or spots that might need attention.
You can choose from many audio applications, each with its own way to edit 
stumbles, bumbles, and moments of silence to honor a lost thought. For all these 
applications, you should follow the same general steps:
1. Find the unwanted content.
2. Give your clip about a half second of silence between edits.
3. Review the edit, making sure it sounds smooth and natural. An effective edit 
doesn’t sound like an edit. Any change to the audio should be unnoticeable.
But instead of reading about it, how about doing it? In this section, we cover basic 
editing skills using Audacity (https://pfdbook.link/audacity). Audacity is a 
popular audio-editing software package in podcasting due to its availability on 
many platforms and (let’s be honest) its price (free). With the open-source com￾munity behind it, this digital audio workstation (DAW) offers you many incredible 
options, some of which we will cover here. If you’re using another audio-editing 
software package, the steps are similar enough for you to apply to your own project.140 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
At this point, we assume you have just finished recording something awesome 
with Audacity (see Chapter 7 for instructions) or have opened a saved project. If 
you have an audio file from another source, such as a portable recorder, you can 
import it by choosing File ➪ Import. Now follow these steps to make a basic edit:
1. Find the segment that you want to edit.
To view the entire timeline of your project, click the fit project in window icon 
(labeled in Figure 8-1).
You can easily navigate between the selected segment of your timeline and the 
project timeline as a whole with the fit project in window and fit selection in 
window icons.
2. Click the selection icon (labeled in Figure 8-1) and then click and drag 
across the unwanted segment.
The unwanted segment is highlighted, as shown in Figure 8-2.
This is a good time to click the fit selection in window icon and double-check 
that the selection border doesn’t go into the recorded content you want to keep.
FIGURE 8-1:
Frequently used 
tools at the top of 
the Audacity 
screen.
FIGURE 8-2:
With the selection 
tool, click and 
drag across the 
unwanted 
content in the 
timeline.CHAPTER 8 Cleanup, Podcast Aisle 8! 141
3. Click the segment between your two cuts and then press the Delete key 
(Mac) or the Backspace or Delete key (Windows).
To remove the unwanted segment, you can also choose Edit  ➪   Cut or you can 
press ⌘ +X (Mac) or Ctrl+X (Windows).
Do not use the Trim command. (Here’s the command not to use: Edit  ➪   
Remove Special  ➪   Trim Audio or ⌘ +T for Mac and Ctrl+T for Windows.) You 
may think you’re trimming unwanted content, but it does the opposite — 
leaving you with only the content you wanted to edit out. Ack!
4. Review the clip.
5. If the edit doesn’t sound natural to you, undo the changes by choosing 
Edit  ➪   Undo Change of Position Region or by pressing ⌘ +Z (Mac) or 
Ctrl+Z (Windows), and trying again.
You’re allowed multiple undos in Audacity, so you can go back to the beginning 
point of your editing in case you aren’t happy with the sound of the edit.
Making Your Musical Bed and Lying 
in It: Background Music
A few podcasters like to add atmosphere in their individual podcast with bed 
music — a background soundtrack. Sometimes the bed music lasts only a minute 
or so until the hosts return from a break. Other times, the soundtrack is looped 
and plays throughout the podcast, fading out if the hosts bring in another source 
of audio (voice mail, other podcast promos, ads, and so on).
HATE EDITING? GET REAL
Is it an absolute rule that you must edit your podcast? Certainly not! Some would argue 
that a performance is at its best when your actors are stammer-free, but back in 1938 
there was a radio drama chock-full of trip-ups, stutters, and stammers. With all this 
going against its production, the Mercury Theatre terrified a nation with its adaptation 
of H.G. Wells’s War of the Worlds, arguably the first reality-based entertainment (well, a 
realistic simulation of reality, anyway) unleashed on America. The ubiquitous mistakes 
intensified the illusion of reality. If your podcast is entertainment anchored in reality, 
you can probably avoid the arduous editing process. Again, it depends on the content 
you’re podcasting.142 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
Regardless of the length of the loop, you must obtain permission from the artists 
to use their music and also give them audible credit at the beginning or end of 
your podcast. If you’re using shareware music, such as that from Freesound 
(https://pfdbook.link/freesound), giving audible credit is one of the condi￾tions you must agree to. Other options include using music loops found in Garage￾Band, Audition, or Logic Pro.
Finding the right balance
As you add bed music, incorporate sound effects, and bring in prerecorded audio 
from other sources (such as H4n Pro recordings or a Zoom call), the sound of your 
podcast gets more complex. Balance becomes not only harder but more essential. 
(Note, for example, the various volume levels of the multiple tracks in 
Figure 8-3.)
Bed music can add atmosphere to your podcast, but you must find a proper bal￾ance between talking and the soundtrack.
If your looped music is too soft, it could be mistaken as unwanted ambient noise 
or some kind of technical difficulty such as a stray signal bleeding into the pod￾caster’s wireless microphone. Music too indistinct to hear can be a distraction, 
particularly during quiet moments or pauses in the conversation.
When bed music is too loud, your voice is lost in the melody. Music, especially 
classical music and selections that rise and fall in intensity (like any good Queen 
FIGURE 8-3:
Adding multiple 
tracks is easy, 
but balancing 
them can be 
challenging.CHAPTER 8 Cleanup, Podcast Aisle 8! 143
album), can be tricky to mix into a conversation. The background music shouldn’t 
be so loud that you have to pump up your voice track to be heard. Your bed music 
should remain in the background as a setting.
Applying bed music the right way
When you’re setting audio levels, you want to find the best blend of music and 
voice. Both tracks should work together and not struggle for dominance.
Always listen to the podcast using headphones and your computer speakers before 
uploading to the internet — review, review, review, (as we said earlier) and find 
that balance.
To avoid music that’s too soft or too loud, keep the following points in mind:
» Experiment with levels for the music before you record voice. Watch your 
decibel level meter and set your music between −11 and −16 dB, depending 
on the music or sound effects.
» When you’re comfortable with the bed’s level, lay down a voice track and 
see how your project’s overall levels look on the decibel level meter.
Remember, your aim is to keep your voice in the 0 or red area without 
over-modulating. With the music bed now behind your voice, it’s much 
easier to hit the red without effort.
» Avoid uneven music, or music that suddenly dips low or has moments 
of intensity. The best music-bed-friendly loops have an even sound (whether 
driving and dramatic or laid-back and relaxed) and an even level.
» Experiment with putting your music in strategic places. Rather than playing 
music throughout the podcast, consider using it as an intro or outro. You might 
even use a short version of your music as you transition to and from ads or 
promos to give listeners an auditory clue of the upcoming change in content.
Setting volume levels for bed music
Each audio application has its own way to change volume dynamically, but the 
basic process is the same. In this section, you use Audacity to bring music into 
your podcast at full volume and then balance it to be just audible enough in the 
background:
1. Click the selection tool (labeled in Figure 8-1) and then click at the 
10-second mark of your timeline, placing an edit line there.144 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
2. Move the cursor to the Track Name until it becomes a hand, as shown 
in Figure 8-4.
The hand icon is the time shift tool, which takes an entire track of content and 
places it elsewhere along the project’s timeline.
3. Click and drag your audio to the edit line you created in Step 1.
4. Import the music file you want to use as bed music:
a. Choose File  ➪   Import  ➪   Audio.
b. Browse for and select the music file you want to use.
c. Click Open to begin the import process.
Although you can import MP3 files directly into Audacity when you’re editing, 
you shouldn’t be working with compressed files. It’s just too easy to lose quality 
along the way.
5. Zoom in to the opening of your audio.
The envelope icon (labeled in Figure 8-1) allows you to dynamically control the 
audio levels over a timeline.
6. Click anywhere in the track of recently imported music.
The left side featuring the audio track name becomes highlighted in blue.
FIGURE 8-4:
Use the hand 
cursor to control 
when audio starts 
in your timeline.CHAPTER 8 Cleanup, Podcast Aisle 8! 145
7. Click the envelope icon on the toolbar.
Blue bars appear at the top and bottom of your audio timeline, indicating that 
the envelope tool is selected.
8. Click the blue bar at the top of your timeline at the beginning of the 
music and a second time farther in the timeline, for example, at the 
15-second point.
The two points indicate the transition from maximum audio to minimum audio 
(such as 15 seconds).
9. Click and drag the second set of points to the 0.5 dB mark, as shown in 
Figure 8-5.
You now have a fade-down of the music that maintains its subdued volume 
behind the voiceover.
10. Click the selection icon (labeled in Figure 8-1), click at the beginning of the 
timeline, and then click Play to review your podcast.
Now you can change the levels as needed to set the music at a level you think 
works best.

Making an Entrance: Intros
Now that your podcast has a solid lead-in and a cue to fade out — or you have a 
loop throughout your podcast that sets a tone — your podcast is beginning to 
solidify. Be proud of the way your podcast is maturing.
But when the microphone comes on, do you know the first words you’ll say? For 
some listeners, you’re about to make a first impression. What do you want that 
first impression to be? Are you looking for something spontaneous every time, or 
do you want to create a familiar greeting that makes listeners feel like old friends?
You can make a first impression with your listeners with an intro, which is the first 
thing the listener hears. Consider the ten simple words that became the signature 
introduction for George Lucas’s Star Wars saga:
A long time ago in a galaxy far, far away
Or from another science fiction franchise, this time with only four words:
Space. The Final Frontier.
Even if you’re not vying to be the next George Lucas or Gene Roddenberry, a good 
first impression is important. Something with an original flair and panache is 
going to make a far more lasting impression than “Yo, what’s up? It’s [insert web￾lebrity’s/influencer’s name here].” No matter who you are, this is a moment that 
can establish your podcast as something that people will enjoy and eagerly await 
from episode to episode. You want the first impression to be unique, lasting, and 
positive.
Consistent, iconic intros have a subtle flair of professionalism. You’re announcing 
to your audience that the show is on the launchpad, you’re ready, and the journey 
is about to begin.
Theme music
How about a catchy theme? Just as television and motion picture themes establish 
a thumbprint in pop culture, an opening theme — be it a favorite song (used with 
permission!) or an original composition — can be just the right intro for your 
podcast. Perhaps you have a friend in the wide world of podcasting who can help 
with audio production. Ask — you might be surprised how willing other podcast￾ers are to assist.CHAPTER 8 Cleanup, Podcast Aisle 8! 147
Software applications such as GarageBand, Audition, and Logic Pro offer royalty￾free loops that you can easily edit into your own podcast intro. Other great free 
music sources include Freesound (https://pfdbook.link/freesound) and the 
YouTube Audio Library (https://pfdbook.link/youtubemusic), which also 
includes sound effects. If you have a little cash in the coffers, check out a mem￾bership with Envato Elements (https://pfdbook.link/envanto) for an astound￾ing collection of royalty-free music, jingles, and sound effects.
Royalty-free means you’re free to use the audio clip without paying a license fee. 
You may still need to purchase the audio.
Intro greeting
Some podcasters use quick, snappy intro greetings for their podcasts. When Adam 
Christianson produced his long-running podcast, The MacCast, he opened every 
show with a heavy rock riff and the salutation “Hey, Mac geeks, it’s time for the 
MacCast, the show for Mac geeks by Mac geeks.” Other podcasts, such as Tales 
from the Archives (https://pfdbook.link/mopo), create an imaginative setting as 
the producers create a more complex intro with music, sound effects, and original 
dialogue. Whether elaborately produced or just a simple welcome, a consistent 
greeting serves to bring listeners into your corner of the podosphere.
In a spoken introduction, include the following elements:
» Show’s name
» Names of the hosts
» Location of the podcast
» Tagline identifying the show
Brainstorm a few ideas on how to introduce your particular podcast. For example, 
try these approaches on for size:
» “Good morning, Planet Earth! You’re listening to My Corner: A Slice of 
Cyberspace, and I’m your host, Tee Morris.”
» “From Washington, D.C., welcome to My Corner: A Slice of Cyberspace.”
» “With a perspective on politics, technology, and life in general, it’s Tee Morris 
with the My Corner podcast.”
As you see in these examples, you can mix and match the elements to drop into an 
intro. Come up with what feels right for you and your podcast and stick with it. 148 PART 2 The Hills Are Alive with the Sound of Podcasting
After you’ve put together your greeting, you can either record it and use it as a 
drop-in (an isolated audio clip that you can use repeatedly), or you can script it and 
record it with each session. Whatever method you choose, a greeting is another 
way of bringing your audience into your podcast.
Regardless of your episode’s overall length, try to keep your introduction short. 
For over a decade, Chuck had a 90-second intro full of music and audio clips to set 
the mood, but it was 45 seconds before he would jump in live with “Welcome to 
Technorama.” After some good advice from friends, he shortened the music to 
10 seconds to get the audience to the intro and the content as quickly as possible. 
Branding is one thing, but try not to go overboard — 10 to 15 seconds is a good 
guideline.
Exit, Stage Left: Outros
Now that you’ve reached the end of your message or time limit, what do you do? 
Do you just say “Thanks, everyone; see you next time” or a basic “Bye”? Or do you 
want to go out with a bit of fanfare? Whatever you decide, an outro is much like an 
intro — as simple or elaborate as you want to make it. Your outro is your final 
word, closing statement, and grand finale (at least for this episode).
Putting together an outro is the same as putting together an intro except you’re 
doing it at the end, so review the earlier suggestions for intros. You can keep it 
simple by saying “Until next time” and switching off. But before taking a shortcut 
to the end, check out this section for some ways to spiff up your outro.
Leave the audience wanting more
Your outro can be the final word from the host, and it should be your bow during 
the curtain call. A signature farewell is a classy way of saying “This podcast is a 
wrap. Thanks for listening.” On Technorama, Chuck and Kreg have a signature 
sign-off that starts with Chuck saying, “And until next time, a binary high five.” 
To which Kreg always responds, “1–0–1.” It has become such a well-known piece 
of the show that listeners writing or calling to leave feedback close their pieces 
with the same tag.
If you’ve edited your show and removed some mistakes, consider using those in a 
short series of outtakes (or bloopers) at the end. Chuck does this for his work pod￾cast Break Point (https://pfdbook.link/breakpoint). Outtakes humanize the 
host and entertain listeners — while keeping them listening right up to the end.CHAPTER 8 Cleanup, Podcast Aisle 8! 149
If you can’t think of anything overly clever, a consistent exit such as, “This has 
been my podcast, protected by a Creative Commons license. Thanks for listening” 
works, too.
Another possibility for your outro could be a scripted list of credits: websites 
where past shows can be downloaded, resources can be endorsed, and special 
thanks can be given to various supporters.
When listing credits, take care that your thank-you’s and acknowledgments don’t 
ramble for too long. Some podcasters reserve a full list of end credits for special 
podcasts, such as an end-of-the-season or even final episode. One minute is a 
good length for ending credits, providing plenty of time to mention relevant web￾sites, tuck in the obligatory “Tune in next week” statement, and ask for com￾ments and endorsements on your favorite podcast directory.
Coming soon to a media player near you
Just as television shows drop teasers of what will be coming up next week, pod￾casters can give hints as to what is planned for future podcasts. Montages of what 
is coming in the next episode give listeners a quick synopsis of what is to come.
Previews for future podcasts can be difficult to plan, mainly because of the spon￾taneous nature of podcasting that the medium prides itself on. Many podcasters 
have no idea what will be on the agenda for their next show until a short time 
before recording. However, if you can provide a glimpse of what’s to come, this 
kind of outro serves as a commitment to the audience that more content is on 
its way.
As mentioned with intros, your outro can use one of these approaches or combine 
them. Find what best fits your podcast and stick with it. The more consistency 
your podcast has, the more professional it will sound.3

You’ve finished your final edit. (And if you think we’re skirting blasphemy 
whenever we use the e word, trust us — creativity sometimes demands a 
sacrifice.) At last you’re ready to compress your mondo-super-sized AIFF 
or WAV file to the format that invokes traumatic memories from RIAA representa￾tives everywhere: MP3.
The MP3 format was designed to reduce the amount of data (via compression) 
required for digitized audio while retaining the quality of the original recording. 
MP3 files are the best way to keep audio small enough to make it a quick-and￾easy download, and it’s this format that podcasting uses to get content efficiently 
from podcaster to podcatching client. Although creating MP3s is a simple enough 
process, you do need to make some tradeoffs between quality and compression.
In this chapter, we help you understand the technical details involved in turning 
your raw recording into an MP3 file for distribution.
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Downsizing audio files with MP3 
compression
» Understanding bit rates and sample 
rates
» Dissecting ID3 tags154 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
A Kilobit of Me, and a Whole Lot 
of You: Understanding Kbps
The compression process begins with proper bit rate setting, measured in Kbps 
(kilobits per second). Bit rate is a method of measuring data transmission from 
one point to the next. The higher the Kbps value, the more data being transferred 
between two points. The more data being transferred per second, the better the 
quality of information. With each rate of data transfer offered by recording appli￾cations, you can digitally reproduce the various qualities of audio:
» 8 Kbps matches the vocal quality of a telephone conversation.
» 32 Kbps yields audio quality similar to AM radio.
» 96 Kbps yields audio quality similar to FM radio.
» 128 Kbps matches audio CD quality and is the most common Kbps setting 
used for MP3 compression of music.
» 192 Kbps is a higher quality Kbps setting and is used for MP3 music compres￾sion. You get better audio sound but larger data files.
You aren’t married to this listing of Kbps by any stretch of imagination. Tweak ’til 
you find the Kbps best suited for your podcast.
The higher the bit rate, the larger the file size, but the smaller the bit rate, the 
lower the audio quality. A good idea is to experiment. Compress your podcast 
using one bit rate, save it that way, and then change your bit rate to something 
higher (or lower), save it, and play back each clip to compare sound quality and 
file size. When you find that happy medium between quality and compression, 
stick with that. If you want to skip experimentation, refer to the suggested com￾pression settings outlined on the cheat sheet available online. (See the Introduc￾tion for details on the cheat sheet.)
Changing bit rates in Audacity is easy:
1. Import your audio (a WAV or an AIFF of your edited and polished audio 
file) into Audacity by choosing File  ➪   Import  ➪   Audio, locating and 
selecting your audio file, and then clicking Open.
After you’ve imported the audio into Audacity, it’s simply a matter of exporting 
it with new settings.
2. Choose File  ➪   Export Audio.
3. In the File Name field, name your file.CHAPTER 9 Shrink That Puppy and Slap a Label on It 155
4. In the Folder field, make sure you’re saving your audio in the correct 
location.
If you want to save to a different folder, click Browse and navigate to the folder 
where you want to save your file.
5. In the Format drop-down box, select MP3 Files.
6. In the Audio Options section, set Channels to Stereo.
7. Make sure that Sample Rate is set to 44100 Hz and Bit Rate Mode is 
set to Preset.
These settings, which are the default when you install and launch Audacity, are 
the best for your audio.
The Bit Rate Mode field has four values: Preset, Variable, Average, and 
Constant. Preset, as noted, is the recommended setting. A variable bit rate 
attempts to do a bit of compression — for example, the bit rate of a complex 
music sample would take more space than a spoken piece with several pauses. 
Although variable length audio sounds good, the actual playback time may be 
incorrect, causing a problem when listeners pause a long podcast. Average is 
similar to Variable in that it provides some fluctuations to the bit rate. However 
it’s not quite as “variable” as takes an average across the entire file and, as 
such, may not create files as small as a variable bit rate. A constant bit rate
ensures that the bits are output at a steady (constant) rate despite the type of 
audio (such as music, talking, or silence).
8. Click the Quality drop-down menu and choose a setting, as shown 
in Figure 9-1.
A setting higher than Standard makes a difference only if you’re using higher￾end (professional-grade) headphones for listening.
Saving at the Extreme or Insane setting preserves any faults and hiccups in 
your audio. If you edit and review your audio on pro-level equipment, you’re 
more than okay exporting your podcast’s MP3 at the Standard setting.
9. Set Export Range to Entire Project.
10. Click the Edit Metadata button at the lower left, and fill out your ID3 tags 
(see Figure 9-2).
For more on ID3 tags, see the section on ID3 tags at the end of this chapter.
11. Click the Export button.
After you set the bit rate for this file, it’s one-and-done — your settings are auto￾matically applied to other MP3 files created from this point.156 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
Care for a Sample, Sir? (Audio 
Sample Rates)
When you have a grasp of bit rates, you’re ready to move on to sample rates. This 
may strike you as a tad redundant, particularly when you see the list of common 
audio-sample rates. A strange déjà vu makes you wonder whether you’re in the 
FIGURE 9-1:
Audacity offers 
different bit rates 
for compressing 
your podcast 
to MP3.
FIGURE 9-2:
ID3 tags tell 
listeners at a 
glance who you 
are, what your 
podcast is, and 
the episode’s 
topic.CHAPTER 9 Shrink That Puppy and Slap a Label on It 157
real world or exist merely as part of The Matrix. No biggie — sometimes technol￾ogy is redundant.
As we discuss in the preceding section, bit rate is a measurement of how much 
audio data is transferred between two points, such as the computer and your 
headset. The sampling rate determines the maximum sound frequency that can be 
reproduced  — the value you set is twice the frequency value. For example, a 
44.1  kHz sample rate can reproduce sounds up to 22.05 kHz, which is slightly 
above the range of human hearing.
Here are a number of audio sampling rates in MP3 encoders:
» 8,000 Hz/8 kHz matches the audio quality of a telephone conversation.
» 22,050 Hz/22 kHz matches an audio quality similar to AM radio.
» 32,000 Hz/32 kHz matches an audio quality similar to FM radio.
» 44,100 Hz/44 kHz matches audio-CD quality.
» 48,000 Hz/48 kHz matches digital TV and film audio, and the maximum sound 
quality on professional-grade audio/video recordings.
As with your audio-recording applications, you have a range of sampling rates 
available — and you can also enter a custom sampling rate.
Using different sample rates in a compressed audio file doesn’t affect the file 
size — only the bit rate and duration affect file size. A high sample rate at a low 
bit rate can result in poor signal representation and thus poor quality, but if you’re 
using at least 64 Kbps (bit rate), there’s no good reason to use anything except 
44,100 Hz as the sample rate.
Be careful when using a sample rate other than a multiple of 11. Choosing a sample 
rate other than 11,025 Hz, 22,500 Hz, or 44,100 Hz can produce an effect similar 
to the voices of Alvin and the Chipmunks. It might be funny at first, but most lis￾teners won’t stick around to hear your entire message.
Changing or customizing the sample rate to your podcast’s needs is simple. Fol￾low these steps to customize sample rates in Audacity:
1. Choose Audacity  ➪   Preferences (Mac) or Edit  ➪   Preferences (Windows).
The Preferences window appears.
2. Click the Audio Setting option, and locate the Quality section on the right, 
as shown in Figure 9-3.158 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
3. In the Project Sample Rate and Default Sample Rate drop-down menus, 
select your desired bit rate.
You can instead enter a custom sampling rate by scrolling to the bottom of the 
list, selecting Other, and entering a number in the field to the right of the Other 
value.
If you’re entering a custom sampling rate, make sure you enter the rate in 
hertz (Hz), not kilohertz (kHz). For example, if you decide your sampling rate is 
44.1 kHz, you have to enter 44100, not 44.
4. Click OK.
Is it soup yet? Not quite yet. The final step is including ID3 tags, the subject of the 
next section.
ID3 Tags: The 411 of Podcasting
ID3 tags matter. They tell other podcasters who you are, tell listeners what your 
podcast is all about, give a quick overview of the current episode, and showcase 
the podcast’s artwork.
Including ID3 tags is the final step before you upload the podcast to your website. 
However, some experienced podcasters from the early days still refuse to tend to 
this detail. While the rebel in us admire their nonconformity, we as long-time 
FIGURE 9-3:
When changing 
audio sampling 
rate in Audacity, 
make sure you 
match settings to 
avoid playback 
problems.CHAPTER 9 Shrink That Puppy and Slap a Label on It 159
podcasters have something to say to those who defiantly refuse to tend to this 
final detail: Enjoy netcasting, you nimrods.
When you don’t add ID3 tags, your listeners can’t effectively organize the pod￾casts in a computer’s media player and drivers can’t be reminded of what they’re 
listening to on their commute. The bigger platforms, such as Spotify and Apple 
Podcasts, now insist that you complete ID3 tags.
Tell me about yourself: All about ID3 tags
Created in 1996, ID3 tags were added to audio files so that the artist, album, and 
track title could be displayed in a computer’s MP3 player when the file was played 
back. Current ID3 tags now include composer, bit rate, album art, and even genre.
Why would a podcaster want to bother with ID3 tags?
Look at a podcast you’re listening to, whether you’re old-schooling it on a porta￾ble MP3 player or listening through your desktop computer. Your podcast app 
probably organizes your various feeds by the date downloaded. Each individual 
podcast has an episode title, an artist, and a podcast (show) title. If the podcaster 
working with the ID3 tags is particularly savvy, artwork associated with your 
show is also displayed.
Now hop from podcast to podcast, and you can tell which people care — or don’t — 
about identifying their shows. Some podcasts use obscure numbers. But with the 
podcasts that have ID3 tags in place, you can look at your player and get an idea of 
exactly what you’re listening to.
When you add ID3 tags to your podcast, you set individual podcasts apart from one 
another, making each one unique but keeping it grouped with your podcast show.
IDentity crisis: Making ID3 tags 
work for podcasting
Some ID3 tags don’t apply to podcasting. Album? Track number? Composer? 
You’re podcasting, not producing music. However, you can redefine some tags to 
make them relevant to the podcasting medium:
» Artist name: You’re the artist behind this podcast, so let people know who 
you and your group are. Put in your name (or at least the pseudonym you’re 
using as a podcaster) or the name of your podcasting team. For example, 
Philippa Ballantine, August Grappin, and D.J. Steve Boyett.160 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
» Track title: This tag is the name or number of your episode. Examples are 
Show #19, Episode 3: One of Those Faces, BOOK THREE: Chapters 39 & 40.
» Album title: Enter the name of your show, your show’s website, or your 
network if you are part of a podcast network, such as Don’t Turn Around, 
Technorama, or Podrunner.
» Track number: Purely optional, this ID3 tag allows you to make sure your 
podcasts remain in some kind of sequential order. For podcast novelists, 
track numbers coincide with the chapter numbers. If podcasts follow a 
season of multiple episodes, the track number tag coincides with the 
episode in that season.
» Year: Enter the year you produced the podcast episode.
» Genre: Select Podcast, if it’s available.
When using Audacity, if you don’t see the Podcast genre, you can manually 
type Podcast in the field.
» Comments: Similar to comments you leave in XML, you can give a quick two 
or three lines of show notes for your podcasts. This field is a great place to put 
in any Creative Commons notices, websites for more information about the 
show and its hosts, and special dedications.
If you’re using another app for your ID3 tags, you may also be asked to complete 
the following additional tags:
» Album artist: Many times left blank, this tag can be tricky. You can use it 
to refer to the artwork artist, the production studio, or the creator of the 
podcast. Examples are Imagine That! Studios and Time Magazine.
» Composer: You can use this tag to feature the name of your producer, head 
editor, engineer’s name, or the studio where the show is produced. For 
example, The Shared Desk artist is listed as Imagine That! Studios but the 
composers are Tee Morris & Pip Ballantine because the show is co-produced 
and co-hosted.
» Grouping: You can leave this tag blank until you’re affiliated with a network or 
distribution hub. For example, QuickAndDirtyTips.com is where podcasting 
superstar Mignon Fogarty leads a team of podcasters who offer sound advice 
and quick life hacks.CHAPTER 9 Shrink That Puppy and Slap a Label on It 161
» BPM: If you’re into staying fit and are producing a workout podcast, you want 
to fill in this ID3 tag as BPM, for beats per minute. BPM is also good if your 
podcast is a house music–dance mix podcast. For example, a podcast showing 
off BPM is any one mixed by D.J. Steve Boyett (https://pfdbook.link/
podrunner).
On playback, ID3 tags appear on the listeners’ interfaces, offering a quick glance 
at the content of the podcast. Figure 9-4 shows a blast from the past, as it appears 
on the dashboard of Tee’s car.
A peril in working with ID3 tags is spelling errors. Audacity and other tag editors 
do not have a spellchecker. Some editors will create quick-fill databases to help 
you fill in the ID3 info. If you make a typo, the database also includes the 
misspellings going forward. Because many directories, search engines, and play￾ers rely on ID3 tags to organize and properly display your show, make sure to 
double-check the spelling of the show’s title, the names of people involved, and 
any other text you’ve added.

AUTOMATING WITH AUPHONIC
Post-production is one of the least favorite parts of podcasting. After you stop record￾ing, the temptation is to upload and move on with your life, but instead you need to 
edit, tag, upload, ugh!
One of the best ways to keep your podcasting enthusiasm from fading is to automate 
wherever possible. Enter Auphonic (https://pfdbook.link/auphonic). This online 
service takes your audio or video files and automates the post-production with tem￾plates to reduce the time it takes to do all those tasks, including tagging, adding an intro 
(for example, your show’s starting theme music) and an outro (your show’s ending

server, Dropbox, LibSyn, or Amazon’s S3 server (see Chapter 10). The free service offers 
two hours of processing per month. Depending on your show length and frequency, 
you may find it worthwhile to invest in a paid subscription to save time.
Auphonic supports a mobile app that enables you to literally press record, talk, stop, and 
have it do its thing. You simply write the blog entry to get your RSS feed updated. Chuck

You’ve figured out what you want to say and said it, you’ve gone through

In Chapter 1, we cover the process of selecting a hosting provider. In this chapter,

Although this easy-to-use method might be all you need to get your files onto

Show Art: Getting Graphic 
with Your Podcast
A big piece of successful podcasting is marketing, and a big part of marketing is 
branding. Your podcast logo, or show art, is an important part of your branding. A 
large percentage of listeners use apps where show art is prominently displayed, 
both in directories and in playback modes.
Apple requires show art if you want to register your podcast with its directory. So 
when getting ready to launch your podcast, you need to have a good look for your

Show artwork, commonly seen in many media players, is a nice option for pod￾casters who want to brand a podcast with a logo. Mur Lafferty’s Parsec-winning

1. Design your show art to the following specifications:
• Format: JPEG (.jpg) or PNG (.png) format
• Color mode: 8-bit channel, RGB mode
• Resolution: 72 dpi
• Size: 1400 × 1400 pixels (minimum) or 3000 × 3000 pixels (maximum)
2. Use a file transfer application or web page to upload your art to 
your web server.
If you’re using a service such as LibSyn, drag and drop the artwork file the 
same way you would upload an episode. If you’re hosting the podcast on your 
own, you can use Cyberduck or FileZilla to upload it on your server. For details 
on uploading files to a server, take a look at the “Uploading your files” section, 
later in the chapter.
3. Use your browser to navigate to your artwork file, and then copy its URL.
Once you’ve copied the show art URL (for example, https://www.yourpodcast.
com/images/artwork.png), write down the URL because you may need it when 
registering your show with various directories. Some hosting providers take care 
of this for you, but others require you to fill it in yourself (as in the next step.)

Many WordPress users install a plug-in (add-on software to extend the

the old one.

Adopting an Effective File-Naming 
Convention
In Chapter 9, we talk about the importance of ID3 tags. Equally important is how 
you name your podcast media files. A good podcast naming convention accom￾plishes the following:
» Easy sequential ordering: Files should not appear at random in your 
directory. They should line up — first, second, third, and so on.
» “At a glance” recognition for you and your listeners: Calling a podcast 
media file my media file doesn’t help much. Calling it Bob’s Fencing Podcast
certainly does.
Here’s an example of a well-named podcast media file, if we do say so ourselves:
Tech_Ep735_20240121.mp3
Although the structure may not be obvious, this filename adheres to both rules 
and even adds one:
» Tech: This abbreviation stands for Technorama. Starting the filename with 
these four letters organizes the files together in the media folder. Listeners 
FIGURE 10-2:
With the 
PowerPress 
podcast plug-in 
from BluBrry, you 
enter the URL of 
your show’s 
artwork in the 
Podcast Artwork 
section.CHAPTER 10 Move It on Up (to Your Web Server) 169
who can see only the filename on their MP3 player or computer can quickly 
recognize that files starting with these four letters belong to a particular show. 
Pretty much everyone listens to podcasts through some podcatching applica￾tion, but don’t discount the small segment of people who like to download 
files to their hard drive.
» Ep735: This file is Episode 735 of the show. Referring to each show with a 
sequential number is a good idea, giving you and your fans a common 
reference point that is easier than “Remember when you did that show with 
that one guy who said that funny thing? Man that was great!” Having the 
episode number right after the name also ensures that the files stack up 
correctly in the media folder, as well as in your listeners’ MP3 players. You may 
want to consider adding some leading zeroes so episode 8 (008) comes before 
735. Add more leading zeroes if you plan on doing more than 999 shows.
» 20240121: This is the date the file was posted, in year-month-day order. By 
using 01 instead of 1, dates from October (10), November (11), and December 
(12) don’t intermingle with January (1). True, in this case the files were already 
sorted by episode number, but you may choose to go with the date first.
Finally, an underscore (_) provides a clear distinction of each part of the filename.
Don’t use spaces or special characters in your filenames. Stick with A–Z, 0–9, 
hyphen, and underscore. Slashes (/, \), hash mark (#), ampersand (&), and other 
characters can cause problems when creating RSS 2.0 files or when FTP applica￾tions are handling the file. To separate elements, use an underscore or a hyphen 
(for example, use PFD_S05E01.mp3 or PFD-S05E01.mp3 instead of PFD Season 5 
Episode 1.mp3).
The following examples are from well-respected podcasters who all follow these 
guidelines:
» NMS-2017-04-01.mp3
» scienceontriplej20181006.mp3
» AC_EPISODE_021.mp3
» TSD-075.mp3
Note how each of them identifies the name or abbreviation of the podcast and 
provides a sequential way of ordering the files. If you leave out the episode num￾ber, as in the case of the Science on Triple J file, the year of the podcast before the 
month and day also ensures that all the files for a particular year are grouped 170 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
together. If the podcaster had used the month first, as people traditionally think 
of dates in the United States, files would be mixed based on the month they were 
released, regardless of the year.
If thinking about the date that way seems too strange for you, do what Chuck 
does — stick a sequential episode number in front of your date, and don’t worry 
about it.
Making Your Connection with 
an FTP Application
FTP (File Transfer Protocol) is the method by which you can transfer files from 
one destination to another over the internet. You likely transfer files every day, 
such as images from your phone to your photo library, or an email message to 
your desktop, or a media file from a podcast server to your podcast app.
Transferring your podcast files to and from the internet isn’t much different, at 
least on the surface and even at any depth necessary for podcasters to ply their 
trade. Lucky for you, specialty software makes the process as simple as dragging 
and dropping.
Some podcast hosting services, such as Liberated Syndication, or LibSyn (https://
pfdbook.link/libsyn), mentioned earlier in the chapter, make this simple 
process even simpler by providing a web form that handles podcast file uploads, 
as described in the last section in the chapter, “Uploading to a Podcast￾Specific Host.”
However, many experienced podcasters need more flexibility than the limited 
functionality a browser-based upload process allows. Regardless of what soft￾ware, forms, or other assistance you use to move files around, the concept of FTP 
is the same. FTP has been around for quite a while now, along with its archaic 
terms, such as the following two computer systems involved:
» Local host: The local host is the computer you are sitting in front of and 
initiating the file transfer from. If you’re using a laptop to connect to your 
web server, your laptop is the local host. If you’re at work and logged in from 
a workstation, your office computer is the local host. The local directory, or 
local path, is the folder on your local host that contains the files you want to 
transfer. You can change local directories at will, but most FTP programs 
have a default starting place.CHAPTER 10 Move It on Up (to Your Web Server) 171
» Remote host: The remote host is the computer or web server to which you’re 
connected. It’s likely the spot where you’re trying to get your MP3 files to go to 
allow others to download them. Not surprisingly, the remote host has its own 
remote directory (the folder on the remote system where you drop your files). 
Again, you can change or navigate through remote directories just as you can 
change file folders on your computer.
You need three pieces of information to initiate an FTP connection:
» IP address or hostname of your remote host
» Login name
» Password
Your hosting company should have provided this information to you. If you don’t 
have it handy, you need to find it because you’re not going any further without it.
All FTP programs do the same job but have slightly different methods of going 
about it. After you grasp the concept, using just about any FTP application is a 
simple process. Here are the general steps to follow to set up a connection in any 
FTP application (specific instructions for two popular FTP applications are avail￾able in the following sections):
1. Launch your FTP application and create a connection.
Because this step is what FTP applications are designed to do, they usually 
make this process simple.
2. Enter the IP address or hostname of your web server, your username, 
and your password.
This step identifies the remote system and shows you have access to the files 
and folders it contains.
3. Connect using either a button or a menu option.
The connection is established in a matter of seconds.
The following sections show you how to use Cyberduck (https://pfdbook.link/
cyberduck) and FileZilla (https://pfdbook.link/filezilla) to transfer your 
file from your computer to your hosting server. Both apps are free and available 
for Windows and Mac. You can find many FTP programs as freeware, shareware, 
and shrink-wrapped software, for every brand of up-to-date operating system. 
We chose these two for their ease of use and streamlined approach, but you can 
use the FTP program of your choice.172 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
Step-by-step (or quack-by-quack) 
setup for Cyberduck
Download the Cyberduck program from https://pfdbook.link/cyberduck to 
your computer and follow the installation instructions. After installation, follow 
these steps (for both Windows and Mac) to set up an FTP connection:
1. Click the Open Connection button on the top toolbar.
The Connect dialog box appears, as shown in Figure 10-3.
2. In the Server text box, enter the name of your server.
Depending on the requirements of your ISP, this name can be in the format of 
ftp.mydomainname.com or perhaps simply my_domain_name.com, replacing 
the italic text with the name or IP address of your web server.
3. In the Username and Password boxes, enter your username and pass￾word, respectively.
The hosting company should have provided this information.
If you’re using a Mac and want to store your username and password for the 
next time you connect, select the Add to Keychain option.
4. Click the Connect button.
If you entered things properly, you see the file folders on your remote web 
server. If you didn’t, you get an error message or a login failed dialog box. 
Check your server, username, and password, and then try it again.
FIGURE 10-3:
A properly 
configured 
Cyberduck FTP 
connection.CHAPTER 10 Move It on Up (to Your Web Server) 173
When your connection is established, choose Bookmarks ➪ New Bookmark. Give 
your newly created connection a catchy name and simply double-click the given 
name the next time you need to reconnect quickly.
Step-by-step setup for FileZilla
FileZilla (https://pfdbook.link/filezilla) operates much the same as 
Windows Explorer or Mac Finder, allowing you to drag and drop files between 
your PC and your FTP server. When you’ve downloaded the FileZilla program, 
follow these steps:
1. On the Login toolbar (shown in Figure 10-4), enter your host name, 
username, and password.
Leave the port blank. (It defaults to 21.)
2. Click the Quickconnect button on the toolbar.
3. When FileZilla presents a prompt asking if you want to save your pass￾word, select the option that you feel most comfortable with.
If you entered something wrong, you get an error message in red text in the 
upper window. Correct your mistakes and try it again.
FIGURE 10-4:
A properly 
configured 
FileZilla 
connection.174 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
If you entered everything correctly, a lot of text scrolls by in the uppermost 
window and content from your web server appears in the right window, labeled 
Remote Site. Congratulations; you’re now connected to your web server.
From here, you can navigate through the folders on your web server much as you 
do on your computer’s hard drive. You can move up or down the file system, find￾ing the spot where you want to drop your podcast files.
Did you notice the Port field in Figure 10-4? A network port is similar to a television 
channel. On the internet, applications may use a specific port; for example, your 
browser typically uses port 80 (unsecure) or port 443 (secure). On the other end, 
severs are listening to these ports for instructions so they can send the appropri￾ate response. By default, FileZilla uses port 21, which is an unencrypted (not 
secured) port. Depending on how your network is configured, you may get an 
error when you try to connect. A common quick fix is to go to the Port field in 
FileZilla and enter port 22, which is secure. You may also see port 22 in reference 
to FTPS (file transfer protocol secure).
A place on your web server for your stuff
Logging in to your web server for the first time can be an intimidating process. In 
this section, we show you how and where to place your podcast files so you can 
easily create links to them.
Don’t be intimidated by the odd directory names on your web server. The only one 
you really need to know is Public_html. Other hosts may call the directory www 
or simply html. If you don’t have one of those three, poke around until you find 
one that has a bunch of files that end in .html.
Look, but don’t touch. Going into folders doesn’t hurt anything, but doing silly 
things such as deleting, renaming, and moving files you know nothing about is a 
bad idea. All those strangely named folders do something, and they’re likely nec￾essary to make your website work correctly. Remember the proverb “’Tis best to 
leave functioning web servers lie.”
You may see lots of different files and a few folders. We show you how to add even 
more files to this system, so now is a good time to think about organizing and 
housekeeping.
Start by creating a special place to keep your podcast files. Making a new folder 
exclusively for your podcast media files not only separates your podcasts from 
your other critical web server files but also allows you to easily maintain the files 
in the future, including making backups and cleaning up obsolete files.CHAPTER 10 Move It on Up (to Your Web Server) 175
In your root directory (the top-level folder usually denoted with a forward 
slash, /), create a new folder called media. With Cyberduck, choose File ➪ New 
Folder, and enter the name in the resulting dialog box. On FileZilla, right-click the 
window with the details (date, size, permissions) on the remote file server and 
choose Create Directory.
After you create the new media folder, double-click the name of the folder to open 
it. You’re now inside your totally empty media folder and are ready to load it with 
your podcast media files.
Uploading your files
After you set up a folder for your podcast media files and decide on a file-naming 
convention, you’re ready to move your freshly named files to the web server. Both 
Cyberduck and FileZilla support drag-and-drop file transfers. If you’re new to FTP, 
the FTP program interface may be easier for you to use.
For Cyberduck, follow these steps:
1. Choose File  ➪   Upload.
2. Browse your system to find and select the podcast media files or folders 
you want to upload.
3. Click the Upload button.
UNMETERED AND UNLIMITED EXPLAINED
Okay, time for a few more terms you’ll want to be familiar with when dealing with your 
files and your hosting provider:
• Unmetered bandwidth refers to the policies of certain hosting companies that 
don’t base their monthly charges on the number of files transferred (podcasts 
downloaded) each month, such as LibSyn.
• Unlimited server space means you can host as many podcast media files as you 
want on the web server for the same flat fee. We don’t know of any hosting com￾pany offering unlimited server space currently, but you can get close. Many hosts 
offer gigabytes for a cheap price — sometimes even hundreds of gigabytes — 
which is effectively unlimited.176 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
For FileZilla, here’s what you do:
1. Navigate to the desired folder using the Local Site window on the left.
2. Select one or more files or folders or both from the window directly 
below the Local Site window.
3. Drag the selected files to the Remote Site window on the right.
Depending on the size of your file and the speed of your internet connection, the 
file transfer will take a few seconds or minutes. When the transfer is complete, the 
file appears in your FTP application and is ready to be linked in your show notes 
and RSS 2.0 feed.
Uploading to a Podcast-Specific Host
Podcast-specific hosting companies significantly simplify the uploading process. 
Many include web-based forms that take the place of additional computer pro￾grams to handle the uploading process. They also take care of archiving, RSS 2.0 
creation, and even ID3 tagging.
USING YOUR BLOGGING SOFTWARE TO 
UPLOAD
Many popular blogging tools allow podcasters to upload files without the need for spe￾cial FTP software. However, podcast media files often exceed the file size requirements 
for these services. For this reason, we recommend not using conventional blogging soft￾ware to handle your podcast media file uploads.
Consider that blogs are primarily used to communicate text. Although you can easily 
extend the functionality of a blog to support a podcast, the site management tools are 
designed for text and images. WordPress (https://pfdbook.link/wordpress) does 
allow file uploads, but the file size depends on system parameters that are often set by 
the web host and can’t be changed by the individual. You can upload, but it takes a sig￾nificant amount of work that exceeds the scope of this book.
Instead, we recommend using blogging software to manage the posting of your content 
after your file is uploaded to the server. In WordPress, you can do this easily by using 
the PowerPress plug-in (https://pfdbook.link/powerpress).CHAPTER 10 Move It on Up (to Your Web Server) 177
Although this web-based uploading process is simple, we prefer the flexibility of 
using an FTP application — or better yet a command-line interface. Okay, we’re 
old school.
For the purposes of illustration, we use an account with LibSyn in this section. 
Sign up with LibSyn (https://pfdbook.link/libsyn) and create your own

Chapter 11
Providing Show Notes
Show notes are brief summaries of each podcast episode. Show notes can take 
the form of an outline, a detailed bulleted list, or just a few sentences of text. 
In this chapter, we show you how to effectively use show notes to enhance 
your listeners’ experience and bring additional traffic to your podcast through 
search engines.
And where do listeners find these show notes? Simply enough, on the podcast’s 
website.
Additional traffic means additional bandwidth consumption, which can cause 
issues. Flip to Chapter 10 for tips on reducing the load on your server and for info 
about optional hosting plans that don’t charge for additional bandwidth.
Show Note Etiquette
Several schools of thought exist on how to approach show notes. Some podcasters 
say you should be brief, using notes only to hold URLs and other pieces of impor￾tant offline data that your listeners may not have had time to write down as the 
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Understanding good show note 
etiquette
» Using images effectively
» Posting with WordPress and LibSyn
» Posting with searchers in mind180 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
show was playing. Others suggest that show notes should be filled with informa￾tion on each and every concept touched upon in the show. Whether you prefer a 
more moderate approach or a deeper dive, your personal tastes and style go a long 
way in determining what is right for you.
Setting aside the level of detail you want to explain, you need to follow some basic 
rules of etiquette:
» Use intriguing and informative titles. In general, and to keep things simple, 
the title of your show notes should match your episode title. Your title is your 
pitch; you’re a huckster competing for the attention of listeners. Some 
listeners may know all about you; others could be seeing something from you 
for the first time. Include keywords in your title that accurately and specifically 
represent the contents of the episode. Your keywords should also generate 
some excitement and make the episode sound interesting and intriguing to 
potential listeners.
There’s a fine line between “intriguing and informative” and “clickbait” when it 
comes to titles and headlines. Avoid headlines that could come across as 
accusatory, toxic, or sensational.
» Include links to resources mentioned in the podcast. If you’re talking 
about a trip to the local museum, provide a link to the museum’s main site in 
your show notes. If you mention another podcast, link to it. If you mention a 
news story or an opinion piece, drop that URL in your show notes. Don’t forget 
about music credits and affiliate or sponsor links too! Good linking brings 
good karma, and it may provide some interesting “Hey, you linked to me!” 
comments and potentially helpful links from others.
» Think about being concise versus being complete. Show notes can be as 
simple as a bullet list of topics or as detailed as a word-for-word transcript of 
your show. It’s up to you. If you’re already scripting your podcast, it’s no extra 
trouble to post the script, but be aware of the length. A transcript for a 
3-minute show is easy for someone to read on their browser, but a reader 
might not want to go through a 45-minute transcript.
In Figure 11-1 Kreg composes his show notes for Technorama (https://pfdbook.
link/technorama). Each episode includes links, embedded videos, and bullet 
points about the topics covered. Is it a lot of work? It can be, but the result is a 
guide that listeners can follow easily.CHAPTER 11 Providing Show Notes 181
Planning the Post
The amount of time you spend planning your show notes is inversely proportional 
to the amount of time you spend doing your show prep (see Chapters 5, 6, and 7). 
If you forgot everything from your high school math class, allow us to paraphrase: 
The more you prepare for your show, the less time you’ll spend working on the 
show notes — and vice versa.
Examine the notes you used when you recorded your podcast. Did you talk about 
any websites? Find the URLs and make sure you spelled them right by testing 
them. We recommend the copy-and-paste technique for URLs rather than relying 
on your typing skills, especially for lengthy URLs.
FIGURE 11-1:
Technorama show 
notes implement 
a range from 
simple links to 
embedded videos 
related to the 
conversations.182 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
If you recorded or edited your show hours or days before you started this notation 
process, replay the media file with pen and paper at the ready. Listen for need-to￾know moments and jot them down as the show plays. After it finishes, use a 
search engine to find additional, relevant URLs to provide to your listeners.
It’s all in the details
Now is a good time to figure out what level of detail you want to employ in your 
show notes. Several factors can influence your decision, and audience expectation 
and personal choice are among the more important.
Here’s a good rule: The deeper you dive into a single topic on your podcast, the 
less detailed your notes need to be. That may sound counterintuitive, and please 
keep in mind this is only a general rule. For example, if your podcast episode fea￾tures a 20-minute interview with theoretical physicist Dr. Michio Kaku on his 
book Physics of the Impossible and how applicable those ideas are to the Star Wars
Universe, you likely won’t have much more than links to buy the book and rent the 
movie.
Show note details serve a couple purposes:
» Act as a table of contents for the episode
» Allow listeners to skip ahead if they so choose
As the podcaster, you can decide how much or how little you embrace these pur￾poses. Here are some approaches that other podcasters have adopted:
» Add a time stamp on segment or topic changes. Some podcasters include 
a time stamp whenever they change topics, which can be frequently depend￾ing on the show’s format and its host. Time stamps help your listeners find a 
particular topic when your podcast covers a range of them. Many podcast 
apps recognize the time stamps in the text and turn them into clickable links, 
enabling the listener to jump to a specific point in your episode. Consider 
adding them to your podcast description and ID3 tags. Keep in mind, though, 
that time stamps take a few extra minutes to create. Yes, it’s a terrific touch, 
but it’s a commitment.
» Write in complete sentences and paragraphs. Taking cues from the world 
of blogging, many podcasters, such as Michael J. Rigg in The Steamrollers 
Adventure Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/tsap), write show notes in prose, 
using complete sentences and paragraphs in place of bullet points and time 
stamps. This approach gives potential readers a flavor of the show without 
having to listen but can make key elements difficult to find. The prose CHAPTER 11 Providing Show Notes 183
approach works best for fiction-driven and role-playing game (RPG) podcasts 
or for short, quick podcasts designed for business.
» Create a simple one-line summary. Some podcasts, such as The TED Radio 
Hour (https://pfdbook.link/tedradiohour), take a minimalist approach 
and post simple one-liners or a few sentences that quickly sum up what the 
show will cover. We suggest that new podcasters not follow this lead because 
it doesn’t do much for helping attract new listeners. Many shows that take a 
quick summary approach enjoy a wider distribution method — for example, 
The TED Radio Hour is part of the NPR network and the TED brand carries a lot 
of weight.
Detailed show notes improve your search engine rankings and enable curious 
Google visitors to determine the value of an episode before listening. Show down￾loads and subscriptions can spike whenever your episode touches on hot topics 
because these keywords appear in show notes as well as blogpost tags. This spike 
in subscriptions demonstrates how powerful show notes can be.
A picture is worth a thousand words
You can also include a representative image or two in your show notes (refer to 
Figure 11-1). While random graphics serve only to increase your bandwidth con￾sumption and risk cluttering your page, well-selected images can add flavor and 
dimension to your show notes.
Before you add an image to your post, keep in mind these three considerations:
» Is the image protected by copyright? Simply grabbing an image off the 
internet can land even the most well-meaning podcaster in a heap of legal 
trouble. Here are a few options to help avoid copyright issues:
• Use your own images. No issues here because you own the images and can 
use them any way you like.
• Use Google Images (https://pfdbook.link/googleimages). When you 
search, choose Tools  ➪   Usage Rights and select Creative Commons 
license. Companies and personalities make these images available for 
public use.
• Identify the owner and ask for permission. Most people are eager to get their 
images displayed and are happy to say yes, provided they get credit.
» Can you link directly to the image, or do you need to copy it to your 
server? Some sites, such as Amazon.com, allow you to link directly to images 
from the source website. These sites have a huge technology infrastructure 
and can handle remote hosting images that appear on other sites. But many 184 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
smaller and personal sites can’t handle the load a popular podcast can put on 
their system if they allowed direct linking to their stored images. In these 
cases, copy the image to your own server before adding it to your page. If 
you’re going to do this, it’s good karma to provide an “image courtesy of” link 
to the original site. Again, this assumes you’ve received the appropriate 
permissions to copy the file. When in doubt, don’t.
Stay on topic. If you record a podcast about audio gear, for example, and use 
images of the latest Beats headphones, that’s fine because the images are 
directly related to your show. However, if you’re using images of Dr. Dre to 
promote your baseball podcast (dropping speech bubbles on Dre saying, 
“When I’m not creating my next hit record, I’m waiting for the next Full Count
podcast!”), you’ve crossed a line.
» Does the image fit on your page? Images too small or too large aren’t doing 
your listeners any favors. Make sure the image you select is the right size. You 
can add width=”x” height=”x” declarations to your image tags to control 
the size or use your blog engine’s editing presets to resize the image (better to 
go larger-to-smaller than the opposite direction), but keep in mind that this 
might distort the image. Previewing your post with resized images is a must.
Posting Your Show Notes
If you’ve planned and prepared, posting your show notes is easy. And if you’ve 
decided on the quick-and-dirty approach or don’t care to use show notes, this 
process can go quickly as well because there’s nothing to do, right?
In this section, we show you how to enter your show notes using WordPress and 
LibSyn. If you use another tool to make your posts, or if you create your notes by 
hand, you still get value out of these examples because we include things you 
should consider along the way.
Posting in WordPress
WordPress (https://pfdbook.link/wordpress) is free, easy-to-use blogging 
software that also works well for podcasters. Many hosting providers have a 
single-click installation, or you can choose to download the software and install it 
yourself (check out WordPress For Dummies, by Lisa Sabin-Wilson).
Before creating your show notes, you need to install a plug-in, such as Blubrry’s 
PowerPress interface (see Figure 11-2).CHAPTER 11 Providing Show Notes 185
Follow these steps to post show notes for a self-hosted WordPress site. Just be 
aware that these steps may vary slightly if you are posting on WordPress.com:
1. Log in to your website’s site administration page.
You can typically find a login link on any WordPress page. If you’ve already 
provided credentials, the link may say Site Admin. In most instances, the URL 
looks something like the following
https://www.your_domain_here.com/wp-admin
2. Choose Posts  ➪   Add New Post from the menu along the left of your 
browser window, as shown in Figure 11-3, to start a new posting.
You can also choose +New ➪  Post from the menu across the top of your 
browser window. The Add New Post page appears, where you will fill in the 
details of your new posting. Although this screen displays a lot of items, you 
don’t need to use them all to get started.

c. Test thoroughly before releasing a new episode.

following:
a. In the Publish pane on the right, click Edit next to the Publish Immediately field.
b. Use the time and date fields to determine when your post goes live.
c. Click OK to save your results.

11. When you’re satisfied with your show notes, click Publish (or Schedule if 
you scheduled publication for later).
12. To preview your post, click the Permalink URL just below the title.
Visiting your web page is a good idea to make sure everything looks as you 
expected it to. If it doesn’t, simply edit the post and save your changes.
Many podcasters with detailed show notes use the <!--more--> tag in their Word￾Press postings, as shown in Figure 11-5. Any text above the more link is displayed 
on the main page. Those who want to view the detailed content can click the link. 
If you have lots of show notes in your post, you can keep your front page tidy with 
a few bullet items for each episode, using a more tag to hide the longer list.
Posting on LibSyn
Because LibSyn (https://pfdbook.link/libsyn) is a dedicated podcast hosting 
provider, the steps for posting show notes are intuitive but a bit different than 
those in blogging software.
Follow these steps to post your show notes on LibSyn:
1. Log in to your LibSyn account.
2. Click the New button in the upper right and choose Episode.

LibSyn also allows text-only posts, which is great when you want to simply say,

3. Click the green box labeled Add Media File Link.
A window appears so you can browse your local hard drive.
4. Locate and select your episode file and then click Choose.

one on each line. Be sure to add URLs to any websites you mention.
To insert a URL in your show notes, click the link icon. In the pop-up window that 
appears, enter the URL and the text to display for the link. Optionally, in the Title 
file, enter text that will appear when the reader hovers a cursor over the link.

In the Open Link In field, select New Window. When the reader clicks your link, 
the new page will open in a new browser tab rather than navigating away from 
your page. Now the reader can return to your show notes by switching 
browser tabs rather than by using the back icon or browser history.
