Categories on LibSyn work like they do in most blogging applications. To keep 
this simple, choose General. However, you can also create a new category. 
LibSyn tools, such as its web page, media player, and smartphone apps, use 
the category to help you organize your episodes.
9. If you don’t want to use the default artwork for your podcast, go to the 
Artwork section and upload the custom episode-level artwork (if you 
have any).
Periodically save your work by clicking Save as Draft. If you want to get a 
preview of your episode, click the View button in the top right. Figure 11-6 
shows how your post looks on LibSyn before it’s posted.190 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
10. In the Publish section (at the bottom of the form), use the Availability 
field to determine if the post will be available immediately or if you want 
to schedule it for a later date.
If you make your episode available later, fill in the date and time fields that 
appear.
11. Click Publish.
That’s it! You’ve made your first post with show notes. If there are any errors, a 
message will be displayed and the fields where you need to make corrections 
will be highlighted.
As part of the running dialog in your show, mention that you have show notes. For 
example, in the middle of recording, say “We found a great deal on those at 
Frobozco. We’ll have a link in the show notes on our website.” Or at the end of the 
show, mention the show notes with contact information, as in “Don’t forget to 
stop by our website at www. . ., where you’ll find links to everything we men￾tioned in the show notes, information on how to contact us, and much more.”
Boosting Search Engine Rankings
One tangible benefit of quality show notes is the effect they can have on your list￾ings in search engines. Traditional search engines can’t scan and index the con￾tents of your podcast media files (yet). As such, you need to provide text for search 
engines to examine and evaluate for index inclusion.
FIGURE 11-6:
Filling out show 
notes on LibSyn 
is an easy 
process with an 
intuitive UI.CHAPTER 11 Providing Show Notes 191
Podcasters can pick up a lot of tips and tricks from bloggers and other website 
owners on how to boost search engine rankings, including changing the position 
of elements, using headings, meta tags, and image tags correctly, and back￾linking. That conversation is far beyond the scope of this book, so grab a copy of 
SEO For Dummies, 7th Edition by Peter Kent if you want to make a bigger splash.
In this section, we show you some best practices you can implement right away to 
make your notes (and podcast) more accessible to search engines  — and 
ultimately search engine users.
Loading up your titles
Search engines (and searchers) pay close attention to titles. You should consider 
the title of your individual podcast episodes every bit as important as the title of a 
given web page.
Some podcasters struggle to write effective titles. The biggest problem comes 
from confusing titles with descriptions. If your title starts with “In this episode,” 
stop right there. You’re writing the description, not the title. A title is a string of 
well-chosen and crafted words that has no room for baggage.
The best titles come from a re-examination of your show notes. If you haven’t 
made your notes yet, you may find coming up with a solid title tough. Here’s the 
process we suggest:
1. Read your show notes and create a bullet list of the key elements, 
thoughts, or themes covered in the show.
Let’s say you have a podcast on a classic car auction. In your show notes, you 
covered the following:
• Sleeping in the Seattle airport while the flight was delayed
• An interesting discussion during the flight with an 80-year-old man who is a 
car restorer
• Delving under the hood of the 1965 Ford Mustang
• Custom headers and exhaust systems
• A short interview with the owner of a 1972 Chevy Nova
• Taking a 1957 Chevy Belair for a cruise
• Listener feedback request: What’s your favorite classic car?192 PART 3 You’ve Recorded Your Voice: Now What?
2. Boil down each element to a single word or phrase, if possible.
Think about the people who might be interested in the contents of your show 
and pick common words they’re likely to search for. Following are potential 
episode titles from your bullet list:
• Sleepless in the Seattle Airport
• The Old Man and the Chevy
• Two Decades of Driving
• Making a Classic Your Own
These all make solid titles, giving searchers a good tease about what they can 
expect. What kind of title do you want for your episode? One with humor? 
Alliteration? Something more literal? Whether your audience is casual or, in the 
case of our classic car auction podcast, serious collectors and restorers, select 
the key points from your show notes and condense them.
Note how the title doesn’t cover everything. That’s the job of the description, 
where you can go into greater detail about those elements plus the many other 
things you talk about on the show.
Titles also carry keywords likely to be of interest to your audience. Think about 
how people will search and write your titles for that. Keep titles short, don’t try to 
cover everything, and employ more detailed descriptions to carry the rest of your 
story.
Soliciting backlinks
Backlinks are the Holy Grail of search engine optimization. A backlink is simply a 
link from someone else’s website to yours as opposed to a link from your site to 
another. In this case, you’re looking for outside locations to link back to your own 
site. Sites that have a lot of backlinks pointing to them are considered more 
important to the computers that control where your site shows up on a search 
engine. To get backlinks from others, you have to create links to their websites in 
your show notes.
Before you post your show, contact other podcasters, bloggers, and perhaps nota￾ble websites to see whether they’re interested in linking to you (and you to them) 
to generate interest in your podcast, rather than to a specific show. Many sites 
have a section for related sites, which can be an effective tool for drawing people 
to your site and your podcast. You can also find people who might want to backlink 
to particular episodes.CHAPTER 11 Providing Show Notes 193
When you’re soliciting backlinks from sources, make each email personal, provide 
the exact link you want them to use, and tell them why you think it’s important 
for them to link it.
You’ve posted your show and put your show notes online. It’s time to start solicit￾ing backlinks to individual episodes:
» Company backlinks: Write to the company that manufactures the products 
you covered to explain that you’re posting a review. Getting big companies to 
link to you can help your rankings.
» Courtesy backlinks: If someone helped you with part of your podcast and 
you mention it in your episode, let that source know. For example, send an 
email to the agency that booked your trip.
» Backlinks from fellow podcasters: If other podcasters cover topics related 
to your episode, let them know about it because they might be willing to spare 
you a backlink. It takes only a few moments of your time and is information 
they may welcome.
A fine line exists between asking for backlinks and spamming. If you can’t think 
of a good reason why a site should link to you, don’t ask for a link. Otherwise, just 
mention what your show is and what you’ve covered that might be of interest and 
let them decide whether they want to provide a backlink.4

Build a community around your show with a blog,

Promote your podcast through social media,

Chapter 12
Speaking Directly 
to Your Peeps
Communication can be defined in a multitude of overly complex ways. For 
the sake of argument (and not to copy each and every dictionary entry we 
can find), we define the term this way:
The exchange of information between two points.
Note that last part — between two points. To us, this implies a bidirectional flow 
of information, to and from both parties.
If you’ve had the pleasure of attending any productivity or team-building semi￾nars (note how well we can say that with a straight face), the presenters drive the 
message home: Effective communication is not a one-way street.
Over the past few years, podcasting has evolved into a more effective communica￾tion method than traditional media (such as radio or television). We all have com￾munication tools at our disposal — email, websites, phone lines — so why do 
podcast listeners seem to get more involved with podcasting? Two simple reasons:
» There seems to be a closer bond between podcast consumer and podcast 
producer. The simple fact that anyone can do this makes the producer seem 
more like a real person than a personality and, therefore, easier to relate to.
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Encouraging listener feedback
» Starting a discussion group online
» Finding conversations on the web
» Handling listener feedback, good 
and bad198 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
» The podcasters are asking for feedback — and getting it. Audience size doesn’t 
matter. We’ve seen some instances of shows with a couple hundred loyal 
followers where the podcaster has to spin off a second show just to handle 
the listeners’ feedback.
In this chapter, we show you some real-world examples of how to foster com￾munication between you and your audience, touching on a variety of methods and 
venues.
Gathering Listener Feedback
It must be a natural human reaction to fear the opinions of others. Perhaps it’s 
insecurity, but we think it has more to do with our culture’s constant reinforce￾ment of the “How are you?” — “I’m fine. You?” — “Fine.” meaningless chatter 
that precedes most of our conversations.
That cultural crutch, however, is left next to Tiny Tim’s seat when it comes to 
podcasting. Listeners, for whatever reason, are compelled to give real and mean￾ingful criticism. And podcasters, for the most part, take to heart those responses.
Of course, we’re speaking in general terms. Yes, flamers and trolls are out there 
with less than helpful opinions at the ready. Podcasting can’t change basic human 
nature for the ill-evolved, unfortunately.
You can foster good communication with your listening audience in a multitude of 
ways, such as
» Allowing and responding to comments on your blog
» Creating and visiting online discussion groups and forums
» Responding to listener email
» Participating in online social networks
» Leveraging voicemail (see Figure 12-1)
So let’s get cracking on how we make these methods of reaching out and touching 
someone work for your podcast.CHAPTER 12 Speaking Directly to Your Peeps 199
Fostering Comments on Your Blog
In an ideal world, all communication, feedback, rants, and raves about your show 
would take place in a neat little box, keeping things nice and tidy. But because that 
won’t happen, your best bet is to build a website that both enables and encourages 
communication in your own backyard.
In Chapter 11, we demonstrate how adding show notes can improve reaching new 
audiences on account of SEO (search engine optimization). Well, here’s one more 
reason show notes are important to your podcast — they serve as mechanisms for 
interaction.
In the world of blogs, this interaction is referred to as comments. Visit just about 
any blog, read a post, and you’re likely to find a small Comments link at the bot￾tom. Some podcasters get dozens of responses per episode. Some get none. 
Although the size of your listening audience and the frequency of your podcast 
affect the number of comments you’re likely to receive, it really has more to do 
with the connection users have with your podcast.
If you’re already using a blog, you usually don’t have to do anything special to 
turn on the comments feature because most software programs accept comments 
by default. If you decide comments are not for you or your podcast, turning off 
that capability is simply a matter of selecting the right option.
FIGURE 12-1:
When a show 
goes live, remind 
your audience on 
social media that 
you have a 
voicemail line and 
that you’d love to 
hear from them.200 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
Much like two co-workers chatting about last night’s The Late Show with Stephen 
Colbert, two or more listeners using your website to talk to each other about your 
show speaks to the attachment they feel to what you have to say (or play). From 
the first moments of attachment, you can nurture a community around your pod￾cast. Here are some simple ways to foster your following:
» Mention that you have comments on the blog as part of the intro, 
outro, or break.
» Include a one-line promotion of your podcast in your email signature.
» Ask questions during the show and direct listeners to your blog to leave their 
comments or opinions.
» Actively respond to received comments.
Communication will also develop among the listening community itself. Rather 
than talking to you, listeners will start talking to each other, and the 
conversation — and sometimes, the community — will take on a life of its own.
Sometimes, comments that take on lives of their own mutate into hostile take￾overs, and the conversation is lost amidst an onslaught of personal attacks either 
to other posters or to you, the podcast’s and blog’s host. Neither your community 
nor you need this kind of conversation. You may want to consider moderating blog 
comments. People can still post comments, but you get to approve those com￾ments for public consumption. When someone posts a comment, you get an email 
and, with a few mouse clicks, can chose whether to approve the comment. You 
have to do a little more work, but moderating ensures that all comments meet 
your quality standards.
You may find your name or podcast mentioned in other places on the internet via 
search engines (discussed both in this chapter and Chapter 11). We recommend 
that you get engaged with those discussions as well. Be sure to check back in a 
couple days for additional follow-ups — or if you’re responding to a blog com￾ment or forum, check to see if they have an option to alert you to replies.
Focusing on Online Forums
An online forum allows individuals to post their thoughts and ideas on a variety of 
topics — of their own choosing. Through a concept known as threading, multiple 
discussions can exist independently of all the others. Topics can get buried quickly 
in email discussions. Forums work differently, keeping all threads and topics 
available for clutter-free commenting at any time.CHAPTER 12 Speaking Directly to Your Peeps 201
As easy as it is to create places for these types of conversations, someone else may 
have already done it. Spend some quality time searching the internet for your 
name and your show. Maybe a devoted fan has already done the not-so-heavy 
lifting for you.
Using a hosted forum, like a hosted blog, takes away much of the burden of down￾loading, installing, and configuring the software. On the other hand, you don’t 
have quite the flexibility with a hosted solution as you would with a package you 
host yourself. One of the easier hosted forums is Tapatalk, shown in Figure 12-2. 
To get started, just follow these steps:
1. Browse to https://pfdbook.link/tapatalk.
2. Log in or click the Sign Up button and create an account.
Click either Google or Facebook to log in with one of those accounts, or click 
Continue with Email to create an account using your email. If you choose the 
last option, check your email to validate the email address.
3. Create a group:
a. In the Start Your Own section, click the Start New link.
b. Enter a group name (your podcast name). The short name is filled in 
automatically.
c. Provide a short description of your show, choose a color, and optionally upload 
a background image.
d. Validate that you are not a robot and click Continue.
FIGURE 12-2:
Tapatalk offers 
free forum 
hosting that you 
can use for your 
podcasts.202 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
4. Configure your group.
On the home page under Groups, locate your group, click the three vertical 
dots, and modify the group settings to your needs.
5. Send invitations.
Using the same etiquette as a distribution list, let the world know your forums 
are available. You can send an email or announce it on your podcast.
Keep in mind that some people might see invitations as spam.
That’s it! You can now customize your forum, start new posts, and spread the 
word about your newly created forum. Much like a discussion group, a forum can 
be promoted by
» Posting the address on your website
» Adding the address to your email signature line
» Mentioning the forum on each of your podcast episodes
You can find plenty of hosted forums options by running a Google search. Most are 
supported by web ads or voluntary donations. Moderating a forum takes three to 
four hours a week for troubleshooting, answering user requests, and keeping 
spammers and forum troublemakers at bay. Four hours is a modest investment 
considering the return is a strong community.
However, keep in mind that forums and communities take time to build steam. Be 
persistent, post every day, and constantly encourage your listeners to interact 
with you and your podcast in this manner. Above all, be patient!
Remember when we said podcasting has an element of marketing? Here’s another 
opportunity to take your name to the people. Developing a public forum means 
your information becomes available to a lot of potential listeners. They may find 
your forum and become interested in your podcast, rather than the other way 
around.
Like blog comments, you might find your name or podcast being thrown around 
other forums. We highly suggest you sign up and participate in the discussion. 
Respond to the comments — positive or negative. (See the section about dealing 
with negative comments later in this chapter.)CHAPTER 12 Speaking Directly to Your Peeps 203
Socializing with Social Media
Social media has become an integral part of our lives, whether you want to admit it 
or not. Online communities built around social media platforms are made up of 
profiles, posts, pages, instas, and threads for people of all ages and backgrounds. 
They can serve as one-stop shops, or combine popular forms of communication 
into a hybrid that gives you all the best features. Some platforms have been online 
longer than podcasting has been around, but podcasters rely on these platforms as 
a way to reach new audiences.
In this section, we cover several of the more popular networks. Yes, there are more 
than the ones highlighted here, but these are the ones getting a lot of the attention.
Find the networks that can get the word of your listeners back to you, help you 
create a better show, and make you a better podcaster.
Facebook
The house that Zuckerberg built, Facebook (https://pfdbook.link/facebook) 
has evolved from the “alternative to MySpace” (yeah, remember when MySpace 
was a thing?) to a one-stop shop offering a blog, a bulletin board, an online scrap￾book, video streaming, and a forum, all in one convenient location.
Registered users can create online Groups where comments can be circulated 
across others’ networks as well as your own Facebook connections. You can also 
establish a Page where you control the message being sent out to the public and 
boost posts that will appear in Facebook Feeds around the world. Facebook pro￾vides your listeners a quick interface through your computer and mobile device to 
not only offer feedback on what just went live but also preview what’s coming up 
next. You can also use your Group and Page to solicit voicemail or offer polls that 
serve as instant content for your show.
Facebook also offers Facebook Live (as we describe in Chapter 16), a video stream￾ing service where your mobile device’s camera captures where you are and what 
you are doing. While you’re filming, comments and reactions are shared with you 
and your audience. If you’re sharing video in your podcast feed, you can easily 
download your video and drop it into your feed. If you would prefer not to offer 
video, extract the audio from your Facebook Live segment and use it as a new pod￾cast episode. Facebook Live can be used as either a promotional device for your 
podcast or as content for upcoming episodes.
For more information on Facebook, pick up a copy of Facebook For Dummies, 8th 
Edition, by Carolyn Abram and Amy Karasavas.204 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
X (the platform formerly known as Twitter)
X (https://pfdbook.link/x) — although users defiantly still refer to it by its 
original name, Twitter — continues to be a fantastic and instantaneous way of 
beginning conversations, garnering feedback, and getting word out about your 
podcast while not becoming the distraction and productivity time-sink that Face￾book tends to be. Through its website or its mobile app, X lets you direct people to 
your blog when a new episode is available. Listeners can post what they’re listen￾ing to and comment on it. From X, the topics created on your blog or forum can be 
cited, and quick announcements can reach a wide variety of listeners in moments.
X delivers the instant gratification of posting content. However, it limits the char￾acters for each entry, preventing drawn-out announcements. The system is based 
on the premise of answering the question “What’s happening?” If you find some￾one is listening to your podcast, ask them for feedback. Good or bad, begin a 
simple chat and ask for the opinions of others in your X network. Use X to post 
teasers on upcoming episodes, ask for validation from comments found else￾where, and post relevant links either you or your listeners provide that tie back to 
your most recent episode’s topic.
Pinterest
Known more as a haven for DIY projects or slow cooker recipes, Pinterest 
(https://pfdbook.link/pinterest) is waiting for you and your podcasting 
street team to take full advantage of it. Think of the social network as a visual bul￾letin board — every time you post a new show, you go to your virtual bulletin 
board and post an image relevant to your show. That image could be your show art 
or a picture from your show notes. When your fans go to this board and click the 
image you just posted, they find themselves on your podcast’s site. That’s how 
boards on Pinterest work.
Create a podcast board for your show and then, when putting together show notes, 
incorporate relevant images for whatever you’re talking about. When you create a 
post — Pinterest calls this a pin — Pinterest will ask where to pull images from. 
Use your latest episode’s URL and then pick an image to represent the new 
content. Others in your Pinterest network can now interact easily with your pin 
by either leaving a comment or re-pinning it to their boards, reaching a whole 
new network.
YouTube
“My podcast is audio-only. What possible use could I have for YouTube?” Yeah, 
that’s what many new podcasters think, particularly as the challenge is that CHAPTER 12 Speaking Directly to Your Peeps 205
YouTube (https://pfdbook.link/youtube) doesn’t allow MP3 files to be 
uploaded. Everything has to be a video.
So why bother?
Billions — and that billions with a big ol’ b — of people are on YouTube, looking 
for content. For example, when people search for paranormal investigation (like Tee 
and Phil’s show, in Figure 12-3) on Google, the search results include YouTube 
content. Since YouTube is part of the Google’s algorithms, YouTube is one of the 
biggest distribution channels for your podcast. Getting your podcast on YouTube 
is another way of making yourself as visible as possible.
But how do you add video to an audio production? It’s not a big leap to use some￾thing like iMovie, Premiere Elements, or Zencastr to add video to your audio 
recording, especially if you’re hosting an interview. You can also take a fully pro￾duced audio track, drop a static image on a video track, and create a video ready to 
post on YouTube. Take a look at Chapter 16 for more about working with video in 
your podcasting production.
Instagram
Instagram (https://pfdbook.link/instagram) may not come to mind as a pro￾motional platform or communication channel for your podcast, but with some

ingenious approaches, the image-exclusive platform can give your podcast an

After your latest episode goes live, do the following to post from your 
smartphone:
1. Email your show art to your smartphone, and save the art in phone’s

The Links page appears.

You can use the main URL for the podcast, but the individual episode’s URL will

where to find the new content.
URLs are not active in Instagram posts.
If you’re creating an Instagram profile just for your podcast, you do not want an 
Instagram account that is nothing but images of your show art. Create Instagram 
show art that features images relevant to your podcast’s content or post other 
content that may be in tune to your interests or even the show’s interest. Repost￾ing the same image over and over again could get your account reported as spam 
and shut down.CHAPTER 12 Speaking Directly to Your Peeps 207

Threads
When Twitter became X, those who did not believe in the future of the rebrand 
began looking at alternatives. Meta, the people behind Facebook and Instagram, 
answered the call with Threads (https://pfdbook.link/threads), a throwback 
to the early days of Twitter but with a nice addition: When making a post, an 
option is automatically offered allowing the user to add another post attached to 
the original, creating a thread.
This new player in social media grants users access through a website or mobile 
app, and allows you to incorporate images, GIFs, video, and active URLs in 
your posts.
Approach Threads like Twitter in its early days, and enjoy the platform. It’s a solid 
option worth incorporating in your social media schedule.

Social media offers you these options and a whole lot more. These platforms are a 
breeze to set up, but you know what else is easy-peasy to set up? Voicemail. If you 
have a smartphone and if you can navigate through Google, you’re only a few 
clicks away from your own voicemail account.
Using Voicemail
One of the strengths of podcasting is that the content is so portable. Plenty of 
people listen to podcasts during their commute to work or on road trips. If your 
listener is away from a computer while listening to your podcast, interaction is 
difficult. How do you get comments from those listeners? Simple: Have them call 
in. Many podcasters set up a number through Google Voice (https://pfdbook.
link/googlevoice), shown in Figure 12-6, so people can call and leave a message.
To set up a listener line, follow these steps:
1. Sign up for a free Gmail account (https://pfdbook.link/gmail) if you 
don’t already have one.
2. Go to https://pfdbook.link/googlevoice.
3. Click the gear icon in the upper right.
4. Click Account in the menu to the left.
5. In the Account section, click the Get a Number button.
6. Review the Terms of Service and Privacy Policy and then click Continue.
FIGURE 12-6:
Google Voice 
is a free, online 
service that 
enables you to 
download 
voicemails in an 
MP3 format so 
you can easily 
play them back 
on your podcast.210 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
7. Enter the city or area code for which you would like to get a free 
phone number.
Note: This number doesn’t have to be in your area.
Choose a phone number that is easy to remember (such as 1234) or has a 
mnemonic (a memory pattern), such as a number containing 8735 (TREK) if 
you’re doing a Star Trek podcast.
8. Click Next to link your Google voice number with your mobile number.
Enable the Do Not Disturb option for your Google Voice number to send callers 
straight to voicemail. You can find this setting in the upper right by clicking the 
Receiving Calls drop-down.
9. When Google sends you a verification code, enter it on the screen 
presented and then click Verify.
10. Click Finish.
You can now tell the world about an easy way to leave you a voicemail. When 
someone leaves you a message, you’ll get an email notification!
Okay, Google Voice is fine and dandy if you’re calling from the United States, but 
let’s not forget that podcasting can reach anyone anywhere. How do you get in 
touch with listeners from Australia, South Africa, or Tierra del Fuego? You can 
encourage your international audience to leave you a voice memo through Face￾book Messenger or take advantage of WhatsApp. Their audio can come from any￾where in the world for free.
You may find yourself with so much voicemail that you’ll have to create a separate 
show for listener feedback. Listeners, including your authors, often get a kick out 
of hearing their comments aired to the world.
Building a Community with Discord
Discord (https://pfdbook.link/discord), which is available as a smartphone 
app, tablet app, website, and standalone desktop client, hosts millions of daily 
users and hundreds of millions of messages a day. While Discord is associated 
with console gaming, eSports, and streamers (something we touch on in 
Chapter 16), podcasters are turning to the platform to build a community. Discord 
is part online forum and part social media.
By establishing a server on Discord, you offer one location for your listeners or 
viewers to meet. Once on your server, you and your audience can keep in touch CHAPTER 12 Speaking Directly to Your Peeps 211
through text channels and voice channels, designated by hashtags, as shown in 
Figure  12-7. Discord enables you to not only develop and build a community 
around your podcast but also keep the conversation rolling in real time, offering 
fans a special online kaffeeklatsch (a German term that translates to coffee and gos￾sip, or friends getting together at someone’s house for a good old-fashioned chin 
wag) in a special meeting room.
And with the right apps and consent from those attending, you can record the 
Discord gatherings and offer them as podcasts themselves. Think of these shows 
as live feedback roundtables.
In this exercise, we assume that you have set up a Discord account, server, and 
channel. (If you need to help, check out Tee’s Discord For Dummies.) In the follow￾ing, we make a post about our latest podcast episode:
1. Go to and select your #general channel.
2. In the Message field, enter the following text:
A new Five Minute Paranormal reviewing the GhostTube app is up and 
**LIVE** right now, so have a look at https://youtu.be/E5S_DovBJAY.
What are your experiences with the GhostTube app? Let us know at 
**703.791.1701**!
To get a hard return (line break) in a Discord message, press Shift+Enter.

3. Press the Enter key or select the send icon to post your message.
This quick message lets members of your server know that your latest episode 
is now live and ready. Sure, if listeners are subscribed to your podcast, they’ll 
be notified by their podcast app of choice, but a little reminder in Discord 
doesn’t hurt.
Discord offers a terrific opportunity to build a community around your podcast. 
Check it out through your own server, or participate on other Discord servers and 
see whether this platform would work for you in connecting with listeners.
Seeking the Comments of Others
There’s an old saying about the best-laid plans of mice and men (and how they 
often go awry). That adage can be applied to when podcasters pick up a walking 
stick, throw a haversack over their shoulders, and proclaim, “I’m going on an 
adventure!” It’s a certainty that listeners, both fan and foe, will talk about your 
show to others in a variety of formats and on platforms where you have no control.
Forums, chat rooms, and social media threads exist that already deal with your 
podcasting topic. At some point, those people will find out about your show and 
start listening. Current research shows that these people will post reviews faster 
than Ken Miles’s now legendary 1966 Le Mans race. In fact, uneducated opinions 
on the internet stand as the only things faster than Miles’s legendary Ford GT40.
Welcome to the internet community.
You can keep your eyes and ears on these groups in a variety of ways to find com￾ments regarding your podcast. Doing so will give you valuable, direct feedback 
from listeners and let you respond quickly and easily. But before you set off on 
that journey, consider the warning passed on to Indiana Jones before setting off 
on an archeological quest: Be careful what you unearth.
Trying a general search
Is it just us, or do most people do a Google search for their own name at least twice 
a week? That’s a great way to see whether people are talking about you. Google has 
a gazillion pages in its search database and constantly crawls a good percentage of 
the web, finding interesting tidbits and adding more data with each pass.
When you search, try various combinations. If your name is a common one, such 
as John Smith, you’re probably going to get a lot of unrelated hits. Try adding the CHAPTER 12 Speaking Directly to Your Peeps 213
topic of your show to the search for more relevant results. For example, if 
your name is John Smith and you’re podcasting about underwater basket weaving, 
type John Smith underwater basket weaving in the Google search box. If your 
show name is unique or at least uncommon, try using the name of your show as a 
search term.
We realize that there are other search engines besides Google. Yahoo! and Bing 
produce fine results, as do a few others. If you have neither the time nor the incli￾nation to experiment with a dozen search engines, we suggest these three. They 
syndicate their results to other lesser-known (but equally valid) search engines.
Searching on a site, blog, or 
social media platform
As extensive and cool as search engines are, they can’t cover everything on the 
internet. Not only are there physical limitations as to how wide an area spiders 
and bots can cover, there are also self-imposed limitations set up by website own￾ers that inhibit a good indexing of the site. Take forums, for example. Some are set 
up in a manner that renders their internal pages invisible to the spiders and bots 
of even the best engines.
But most forums have an internal search engine that you can use to find the con￾tent in the forum — though you may be required to register with the forum to 
access its search engine. Blogs, forums, and even social media platforms have 
search features built in.
Dealing with Negative Comments
First, don’t panic.
Second, don’t respond. Not yet.
Third, let your blood pressure come down to a normal level.
Let’s face it. Anytime someone has a critical comment about us, we get an emo￾tional reaction. We call that being human, and it’s perfectly understandable and 
impossible to suppress. Following the impulse to reply right away leads you to 
discover the two reasons why it’s called a knee-jerk reaction: It’s a reflex to clash￾ing viewpoints, and you come across like a real jerk when you don’t think about 
your response before riding the emotional roller coaster. (The classic wooden ones 
like the Rebel Yell, Beast, or Grizzly. Yeah. Roller coasters. Cool.)214 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
When you’ve calmed down and are feeling more detached, reread the comment 
and plan your course of action. Here are some suggestions:
1. Reflect on the comment.
What does the comment say, really? Does the person make a valid point? Is 
there an area of improvement you should make? If the comment was specific, 
re-listen to the show in question. Did you say what the person said you did or 
stumble as bad as the person made it out to be? You may need a different 
perspective, so feel free to get someone else involved.
2. When you fully understand the criticism, decide whether or not 
you want to respond.
One option is to not respond. If you do choose to respond, consider taking the 
high road — do it respectfully. You can often disarm an inflammatory remark 
by politely accepting the comment with something like “Thank you for your 
feedback. I’ll take it in to consideration.” You may want to ask questions, get 
clarification, or propose a course of action.
3. Consider sending an email.
There’s no guarantee that the person will keep your email private. In fact, count 
on your email getting posted alongside the negative comment. Whatever you 
say in a private email should be something you would be willing to say in 
a more public forum. Keep your rebuttal rational, civil, and, above all, 
professional.
Consider negative comments as constructive feedback and an opportunity to 
improve.
AVOID THE BAITING GAME
After watching a scene in Bull Durham where an umpire goads Kevin Costner into insult￾ing him, Tee commented, “That’s so Hollywood.” His dad, a highly respected umpire in 
college and semi-pro baseball, said, “Actually, that’s not. That’s baiting. I’ve played that 
game too many times.” Although you may not think baseball umpires and podcasters 
share a common trait, they do: playing the Baiting Game.
Some people live to pick fights. Commonly referred to as trolls, they could be listeners of 
your podcasts or even other podcasters looking for a thrill in bucking the community. 
Usually the feedback from baiting contains personal attacks, misconstrued facts, flawed 
reasoning (if there’s any reason present), and a healthy dose of verbal insults akin to CHAPTER 12 Speaking Directly to Your Peeps 215
Negative feedback is never an easy thing to stomach, but look at the positive 
aspect: People are listening. And now they’re talking, blogging, and podcasting 
about you. Don’t rush out and say something irrational simply to drum up contro￾versy, but do realize that people will disagree with you now and then. You need to 
be ready to face that tough love when it comes your way.
More than anything, grow from the experience. Understand that anything you say 
in your podcast will be heard by a variety of people, with different backgrounds, 
experiences, and expectations. We’ve been on both sides of this and can count 
many times where the negative comments we received turned out to be some of 
the best feedback. We think we’re better podcasters for it.
punching someone in the ear and then running away. When this happens, don’t reply. 
Ever. By not replying, the words sent to you are a waste of time and effort on the 
sender’s part, not yours.
Not replying isn’t a guarantee that the baiter will simply disappear. You may need to 
moderate your blog to make sure people don’t post comments that you deem inappro￾priate. And as you moderate comments, continue to delete the trolls’ comments, so 
they never see the light of day.
The high road has a better view than the low. In some cases, like the Baiting Game, the 
best response is none at all.CHAPTER 13 Fishing for Listeners 217
Chapter 13
Fishing for Listeners
There are hundreds of thousands of podcasters. By some estimates, the num￾ber of subscriptions exceeds one billion. However, those numbers pale in 
comparison with the potential audience, which is somewhere in the billions 
of people worldwide with a broadband internet connection. Although the audience 
is large, the options for listeners are legion. How do you attract an audience to 
your podcast?
In this chapter, we show you a variety ways to get a larger listener base. Some cost 
money; others cost time. By using these methods, you can expose your podcast to 
the right people at the right time.
Getting Your Podcast Ready for Promotion
Whether you’ll be spending money or energy, you need to do some prep work 
before you start your campaign. Don’t rush a promotional campaign — take the 
time to carefully plan and execute it. Otherwise, you not only waste time and 
money but may also turn off potential listeners to your show, making it many 
times more difficult to attract them back for a second chance.
Polishing your presentation
New podcasters need a few episodes under their belt before they hit their stride. If 
you’re on podcast episode number three, you likely haven’t fleshed out your show. 
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Getting your podcast ready for 
advertising
» Using paid advertising
» Taking advantage of free advertising
» Generating buzz about your podcast218 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
Sure, you may have been planning your podcast for months, or had experience 
behind the mic on another medium, or nailed it from the beginning. But give 
yourself at least five full episodes to find the sweet spot. Experiment with show 
segments, running times, and the overall content flow to discover your strengths 
as well as your weaknesses.
Checking your bandwidth
In Chapter  10, we introduce you to the concepts of unmetered bandwidth and 
unlimited space. Each new listener consumes more of your precious bandwidth. 
For podcasters with limited bandwidth, getting more listeners can be an expen￾sive proposition.
If you’re using the services of LibSyn (https://pfdbook.link/libsyn) or 
another podcast-hosting company with unmetered bandwidth, you don’t have 
to worry about your bandwidth and can safely skip ahead to the next section. 
See Chapter  10 for more information about this and other affordable hosting 
services.
Many podcasters start out using a standard web-hosting service to host their 
podcast files and are quickly surprised when they run out of bandwidth. We’re 
more surprised about their poor math skills. Suppose your site is allowed 100MB 
of monthly transfers. On the 10th of July, you log in to your bandwidth stats page 
and see that you’re already at 60MB for the month. Will you make it? Here are the 
formulas to figure this out:
Bandwidth consumed / number of days so far this month = daily bandwidth rate
Daily bandwidth rate × 31 (maximum total number of days in the month) = total 
bandwidth needed
Now plug in your numbers to find out your daily bandwidth rate:
60MB / 10 = 6MB
Your site is consuming about 6MB per day. Now multiply 6MB by the number of 
days in the month (31) to get the total bandwidth you need for the month:
6MB × 31 = 186MB
You’re not going to stay within your 100MB limit. You will be roughly 86MB over 
your 100MB plan.CHAPTER 13 Fishing for Listeners 219
That sounds — and is — simple. As a real-world example, Chuck checked his 
bandwidth usage on July 18 and found that he had used 256.65GB in the first 
17 days. First, he finds out his daily bandwidth:
256.65GB / 17 days = 15GB average daily transfer rate for July
Then he finds out his total bandwidth for the month:
15GB × 31 days = 465GB
As you can see, he needs 465GB of bandwidth to get through the month, assuming 
his traffic stays steady and doesn’t increase. If his bandwidth ceiling was 500GB, 
he’d need to think twice before starting an advertising campaign because he’d 
likely hit that ceiling, and his hosting provider would likely shut down access to 
all those brand-new podcast listeners he just worked so hard to get. Not a good 
way for anyone to spend their time.
You should be using less than 50 percent of your monthly allotment of bandwidth 
before starting an advertising campaign. If you’re using more than that, you’ll run 
out of room and will have to seek alternative hosting options before proceeding. 
We cover some of these alternative options in Chapter 10.
Figuring out your USP
USP, or unique selling proposition, is a message that sells your podcast to potentials 
listeners. Although you probably aren’t charging money to listen to your podcast, 
make no mistake that you need to sell it to potential listeners if you’re considering 
advertising.
Why should a potential listener listen to your podcast? And more importantly, 
how can you, as the party advertising the podcast, present a message that makes 
a potential listener want to listen?
Plenty of books, websites, seminars, and post-graduate degree programs are 
dedicated to the subtle nuances of marketing and advertising. We’re not suggest￾ing you go that far. But we do suggest that you take a good, hard look at what you 
produce every week and come up with a concise and consistent message to pro￾mote your show.
For example, when Chuck was looking at advertising for his Technorama podcast, 
he found that the tech podcast market was saturated. Several dozen shows repur￾posed the same news about Microsoft, Google, and Apple. So Chuck chose to spot￾light the strange, bizarre, and unusual items that are typically passed via email 
from geek to geek — the steam-powered Nintendo DS, the motorcycle that folds 220 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
into a briefcase, and the device made of wood that adds binary numbers. Here is 
the USP for Technorama: Technorama takes a lighthearted look at the world of tech, 
science, sci-fi, and all things geek.
Sometimes calling in help from the outside is a good idea. Ask friends, family, and 
even listeners to tell you why they listen to your show. We’re talking about not a 
catchy slogan or jingle from a Madison Avenue marketing firm but a plain￾English (or your language of choice) way to tell interested people what your show 
is about and why they should be listening.
We can’t give you a step-by-step outline for this one. Spend a few days on it. Try 
it out on some folks first. When you find a message that fits, you’re ready to 
proceed on your advertising quest.
Exploring Various Advertising Options
You can advertise to give your podcast exposure to a larger audience in many 
ways, but no surefire solution exists. Although we’ve been podcasting since the 
early days, we still find the advertising process daunting. However, that doesn’t 
mean we can’t take a chance or two and see what happens, right?
If you’re interested in making money on your podcast, the first thing you’ll want 
to consider is ROI, or return on investment. Now we’re starting to sound more like 
Advertising For Dummies, so bear with us. Simply put, is the money you spend on 
advertising going to generate additional . . . anything? It doesn’t make sense to 
spend $5 or $5,000 if you won’t get more of something — listeners, advertisers, 
sponsors, money — in return. This is ROI in a nutshell.
IS ADVERTISING RIGHT FOR YOUR 
PODCAST?
Before launching your advertising campaign, apply some Jurassic Park logic. In that 
movie, Jeff Goldblum’s character chastises the dinosaur-resurrecting mogul with “Your 
scientists were so preoccupied with whether or not they could, they didn’t stop to think 
if they should.” Good advice for podcasters and mad scientists alike.
For many, podcasting is a labor of love and not a money-making proposition. As such, 
spending too much money or time on advertising may make a passion seem a heck of a 
lot more like a job — and you probably already have one of those.CHAPTER 13 Fishing for Listeners 221
Give me a boost, Facebook
Still the largest of social media platforms, Meta’s Facebook is a good place to con￾sider a modest financial investment to reach a larger group people with hopes of 
converting them into loyal listeners. This is the same reason your Facebook feed 
has ads for shaving razors, car batteries, or pillows to help you stop snoring. 
Someone wants you to see what they have to offer and somewhere in their mas￾sive collection of data, you fit their targeted profile — so why not turn the tables 
and start reaching people who fit your profile?
For a price, boosts allow you to expose your Facebook post on the timelines of 
people who don’t directly follow you or those who may have shared your content 
in the past. The price is determined by how many people you want to reach.
Let’s assume you’ve already set up a Facebook Page for your podcast as one of the 
many ways you and your listeners can stay in touch. As you post a new episode, 
you mention it on your podcast’s Facebook Page, which has 30 followers. You 
want to reach more people, so you share your post to the 500 followers on your 
personal account (mostly family, friends, and co-workers) in hopes that some of 
them will share it.
How do you reach people that you haven’t already met to let them know about 
your podcast? A boost is the Facebook equivalent of going to a party and shaking 
hands and introducing yourself and your show. When you boost, you determine 
the amount to spend and define who you want to target.
For years, Dave Slusher’s Evil Genius Chronicles (https://pfdbook.link/evilgenius) 
has been vocal about not advertising and not working toward a huge listener base. 
Dave makes a podcast for one person — Dave Slusher. If other people hear about the 
podcast and decide to listen, great, as long as they enjoy it and don’t expect him to be 
something he’s not. Fame — even the moderate fame a popular podcaster can 
achieve — is a double-edged sword. Before long, emails and voicemails are flying in 
with ideas, suggestions, and even mandates for how you can make the show better for 
the listeners. Don’t forget that fan is short for fanatic, and fanatic people don’t always 
behave rationally.
Choose this path with caution. “Doing it for the love” probably means you can (and 
should) forgo advertising your podcast. Advertising is not a fast way to get more listen￾ers, but it is a fast way to burn through money without a lot to show for it.222 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
Boost a post containing your episode, not your page. People will be more interested 
in hearing your breakthrough research on stopping telomere breakdown to extend 
a cell’s lifespan than just knowing about your science research show.
You should have no problem finding your post to boost. Facebook practically 
pushes it in your face when you view your own timeline. Chuck found it odd at 
first to see Technorama ads with the latest post on his timeline. Then he looked 
closely at the top of the ad and discovered the text “Only you can see this preview 
until you run this ad” and a blue Boost Post button. When you click the Boost Post 
button, the window shown in Figure 13-1 appears.
Facebook gives you a few ways to choose the target audience for your ad. Scroll 
through the UI featured in Figure 13-1, and you’ll find many options to consider 
for targeting your ad, the duration of the ad campaign, and so on.
On the Boost Post page, scroll down to the Audience section for the options relat￾ing to who should see your ad. We suggest you focus on the following details:
» People You Choose Through Targeting: When you click this option, note the 
pencil icon, which allows you to choose a specific gender, age range, location, 
and detailed targeting (based on demographics, interests, and behaviors).

Think of detailed targeting as your way to match keyword searches with 
people. If you just did an episode with a noted independent horror film

movies, and movies.
» People Who Like Your Page: This option doesn’t sound like it’s going to get 
you anything more than people who already like your page and are likely to 
see your content, but thanks to Facebook’s magical algorithms, it also targets 
people who like other pages similar to yours.
» People Who Like Your Page and Their Friends: This option lets you place 
your ad on the timeline of not only people who already follow your page but 
also their friends. It’s almost like paying everyone to share your link for 
you — does that make you feel dirty too?
» Create New: This final option is similar to the first one but enables you to 
create groupings of targets to save and use on multiple ads. This feature is 
handy if your show has a variety of topics on a regular basis. For example, if 
you have a science podcast, you might set up one group that targets people 
interested in space exploration, NASA, and Mars, and a second group for 
those interested in DNA, gene sequencing, and genetic mutations. That way, 
when you finish the interview with the rocket technician, you can target the 
right group and repurpose that group weeks or months later when you air the 
tour at JPL.
Of course, there’s always the subject of money. How many people can you reach 
and how long do you run the ad? Fortunately, you can choose a budget that works 
for you, starting with just a few dollars and running for as little as a day. The more 
you spend, the larger the potential audience. The pop-up window has a special 
section that helps you calculate the cost before you commit. No surprises here that 
will force you into a second mortgage, thank goodness. You can make your pay￾ment by credit or debit card, PayPal, or an online banking account.
If you’re like most podcasters, you’re working on a budget, so save it for special 
shows. If you do a music podcast and just landed a spectacular interview with 
Alice Cooper, that’s a good candidate to boost!
Be sure to read the Facebook Advertising Policy (https://pfdbook.link/
fbpolicies) before launching your campaign. Yes, it’s a lot of text, but it can save 
you from a nasty surprise that gets your ad rejected. Chuck learned this on his first 
attempt when he discovered that images on boosted posts must not contain 
more than 20 percent text — frustrating.
While Facebook is a major player in social media, this may change over time. We 
encourage you to look into other avenues for ad campaigns such as PayPerPost 
(https://pfdbook.link/payperpost).224 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
Insta-traffic with Instagram
In Chapter 12, we walk you through the process of switching your Instagram pro￾file from a personal account to a business profile. With a business profile, you can 
offer your listeners and viewership quick and easy access to your podcast’s email 
and voicemail — and you can also boost your posts. Take one of your images and 
turn it into a sponsored post, reaching beyond your own network to Instagram￾mers who may not be following you but share interests relevant to your podcast.
When you activate your (free) business profile, Instagram connects to either a 
Facebook account or a Facebook Page that you manage. Along with the option to 
go to a profile and tap a Contact button, all your posts will offer you the option to 
promote that post. When you promote a post, your Instagram post — the image 
and its accompanying text — appears in the feeds of the audiences you target. If 
this sounds similar to what happens when boosting Facebook posts, it’s because 
you’re working with the same audiences you are building with Facebook. Remem￾ber, Meta owns both Facebook and Instagram.
And promoting a post on Instagram is just as easy as on Facebook:
1. Launch your Instagram app.

b. Enter the website URL. We recommend that you paste a link to the episode 
you want to promote.

When you set up a boosted Facebook post, you can simultaneously run a boosted

Writing press releases
Some podcasters have enjoyed enormous success attracting new listeners with 
press releases. On the positive side, you have a lot of room in a press release to talk 
about your show. On the negative side, you have no guarantee who — if anyone — 
will pick up and run your press release.
Writing an effective press release is an art form. It has to appeal both to the man￾aging editor of the publication considering your release as well as to John Q. Pub￾lic. Here are some ideas to help you write a good press release:
» Hook them early. This is also known as BLUF, or bottom line up front. 
Whatever you have to say, be sure to say it first and grab their attention.
» Tell a story. Your press release should convey what you want to say in a 
short, concise manner. Start with the basics of good journalism: who, what, 
when, where, and why. Three or four paragraphs made up of two or three 
sentences each is a good guideline to follow. Try to answer the most common 
questions people will have about your announcement.
» Target the press release. Write a press release suitable to your target 
audience and send it to those likely to run it. You’ll likely have to write more 
than one version. A story about “hometown boy writes successful book on 
podcasting” is more likely to get published in the college alumni school paper 
than USA Today.
» Include the tag line. Use your USP (unique selling proposition). This is the 
perfect place to give someone that 30-second pitch about your show.
» Add some quotes. Include a few lines from the people involved. Keep the 
quotes positive and upbeat.
» Provide appropriate URLs. Don’t forget to provide links to your site or sites. 
Sometimes you may get so wrapped up in the wording of a press release that 
you forget the point of the press release: to get people to come to your site 
and try your content.228 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
Read a number of press releases to get a feel for the marketing aspect.
If you know people who have written press releases in the past, see if they can 
lend some help — if not, reach out. Remember that the podcasting community 
can be a helpful network.
PR Newswire
PR Newswire (https://pfdbook.link/prnewswire) is the cream of the crop 
when it comes to online and offline distribution of your press release. PR News￾wire has the capability to send your press release to thousands of media outlets, 
ranging from newsprint, radio, websites, television, and more.
The company also provides editorial services for your press release as well as tips 
on how to write a release that’s more likely to get results. If you’re serious about 
getting the maximum exposure for your show and are thinking about using 
a press release as part of your strategy, PR Newswire is worth considering. 
However, many independent podcasters find the cost (around $300 to start) 
prohibitive.
PRWeb
PRWeb (https://pfdbook.link/prweb), shown in Figure 13-4, offers paid and 
donation-based services, and it boasts a good number of media outlets. The main 
difference between the two services is the means of the distribution. PRWeb works 
to put your press release in front of thousands of online media outlets. Offline 
media sources may subscribe as well, but the primary distribution is online. Using 
mostly online distribution isn’t a bad thing. After all, your podcast is an online 
service, and you’re likely most effective reaching an audience who is already 
online. We’ve had various write-ups in online and offline media, and we’ve always 
received more traffic and attention to our websites from the online sources.
For $99, PRWeb distributes your press release (among many other things) in 
search engines and news sites. Additionally, press releases at this level are 
reviewed by the PRWeb editorial team, providing valuable feedback on ways to 
make your release more meaningful and more likely to be picked up by various 
other news organizations around the internet.CHAPTER 13 Fishing for Listeners 229
Promoting Your Podcast
Before you start throwing money toward an advertising campaign, consider all the 
ways you can spread the word about your podcast without a financial investment. 
You should probably be doing these things even if you plan on tossing out 
some cash.
Optimizing your site for search engines
In Chapter 11, we give you several tips on how to make show notes more appealing 
to search engines. You can employ similar methods to your entire website so that 
it receives the maximum exposure and visibility to search engines.
For more information on optimizing your site, we highly recommend Search Engine 
Optimization All-in-One For Dummies by Bruce Clay and Kristopher B. Jones (Wiley). 
And in Chapter 14, we go into using ChatGPT to make SEO a little easier.

If you’re looking 
to get the word 
out about an 
upcoming 
high-profile 
episode for your 
podcast, PRWeb 
is there to help.230 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
Submitting promos to other podcasts
Podcasting has been called a great hall of mirrors because many podcasts spend at 
least some time talking about other podcasts! This feeling of community adds to 
the distinctiveness that is podcasting and is, for the most part, accepted by the 
general podcasting audience. The podcasting landscape continues to grow and is 
so fractured that many listeners look to their favorite podcasters for help finding 
other podcasts.
The majority of corporate podcasts see other podcasts as competition and are as 
likely to talk about another podcast as a traditional broadcaster is to talk about 
another station across town. Even some independent podcasters make a point not 
to talk about other podcasts, simply because they don’t want to add to the hall of 
mirrors effect.
One of the more widespread ways podcasters talk about other podcasters is with a 
promo, which is an audio clip that describes your show. Other podcasters insert 
this clip into their shows — play it on the air, so to speak — thereby presenting 
your message to their subscribed and downloading audiences.
Promos are a great way to let other people know that your podcast exists. Listen 
to other podcasts to find a few that play promos. Note the average length of the 
promos they play and the type of content being presented. Is it all serious busi￾ness, or is more light-hearted humor involved?
Query other podcasters and offer your promo. In exchange, offer to play a promo 
for those podcasters gracious enough to play your promo. Some podcasters are 
good at making promos and even offer to make promos for free for other 
podcasters.
One final note about sending out promos: Ask. Unless the show specifically says 
“Submit your promos to us at . . .,” be a good podcasting citizen and send the 
podcasters a note asking whether they’d like to run your promo. Requests that 
start off with “I listen to your show every week because you . . .” are likely to get 
a better response than those starting (and ending) with “Please run my promo.” 
Consider taking a reverse approach, as well. If you listen to a podcast and want to 
run their promo on your show, ask if they have a promo and offer to play it. Most 
podcasters are more than happy to contribute and reciprocate.
Recording your promo
You’ve done the hard work by figuring out what makes your podcast special; 
now you need to sit down and record your promo. Doing so isn’t difficult. We tend 
to enjoy the ones that come from the voice of the podcaster the most. Here are a 
few tips:CHAPTER 13 Fishing for Listeners 231
» Write your script. Or don’t. Some folks are happy flying off the cuff. But in 
the interest of time, we recommend putting some thoughts down on paper 
and running through them out loud to see how long it takes. Most promos 
are under a minute long.
Keep your promo between 30 to 90 seconds long. Anything over 90 seconds 
may be a hard sell to get other podcasters to run it.
» Add effects and music from your podcast. If you use the same music in 
your show each week (see Chapter 8) or have some special sound effects that 
brand the show as yours, include them in your promo. Effects are a great way 
to tie in your promotion to your show, giving new listeners assurance that 
they’ve subscribed to the right place.
» Don’t forget your website URL! Too many podcasters provide the link 
to their podcast feed. Useless, in our opinion. Instead, repeat the URL of 
your website, where it should be painfully simple to subscribe to the RSS 
feed for your podcast. Now here’s hoping you picked an easy-to-remember 
domain name!
» Include a link to the promo on your website. Recording a promo and sending 
it out to a few podcasts is great, but what about all the other people who you 
inspire to make their own podcast? Chances are good that if you put a direct link 
to your promo or create a web page featuring your promo (or promos), other 
podcasters will grab that file and include your promo in their shows.
» Speak clearly and enunciate! You know the name of your show very well, 
so you might say it so quickly that it doesn’t come out as clearly as it should. 
When you introduce your show to someone who has never heard the show 
name before, slow down. Say your podcast’s title as if you were introducing 
yourself to someone new. While researching for this book, we listened to 
several promos where the podcaster said the name of the show so fast that 
it reminded us of those misheard lyrics in a song. Was that Jimmy Hendrix 
podcast actually called “Kiss this guy dot com”? If it weren’t for a printed URL, 
we wouldn’t have any idea what their show was called or where to find it.
When you send other podcasters your promo, save some email bandwidth 
and send the link on your website rather than the file itself. Make it easy to 
download the promo file from your site!
Giving interviews
Podcasting is the fastest growing medium we’ve seen . . . ever! If your podcast 
covers a new area of the world or addresses an underserved market, people are out 
there who want to talk with you about it.232 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
Contact the publications, websites, and other media outlets that cover the 
industry your podcast covers or that might take an interest in you, as Tee and Phil 
did when reaching out and landing an interview with their alma mater (see 
Figure  13-5). Send them your press release, along with a personalized note 
telling them about your show and stating that you’re happy to do an interview.
Most interviews today are conducted over the phone or using a videoconferencing 
platform such as Google Meet or Zoom. A handful are conducted via email. Prep￾aration can make the difference between a poor interview that never sees publica￾tion and a well-delivered interview that keeps the audience  — as well as the 
interviewer — engaged and entertained.
FIGURE 13-5:
When looking to 
promote their 
paranormal 
podcast, Phil 
Rossi and 
Tee — both 
graduates of 
James Madison 
University — 
reached out to 
their alma mater.CHAPTER 13 Fishing for Listeners 233
Here are a few tips to make your interview go swimmingly:
» Eliminate the BS factor. If you have only a passing interest in the subject for 
which you’re trying to pass yourself off as an expert, you’ll quickly be discov￾ered, thrashed repeatedly, and left out for the buzzards. The people who are 
interviewing you are probably experts in their fields, so don’t try to come off 
as something you aren’t. Be open and honest about your experience and 
focus on why you’re doing the podcast. Even if you’re considered a subject 
matter expert, remember that your listener may not be. You might want to 
consider simplifying things on behalf of the listeners new to the subject. Of 
course, this depends on your listeners and the subject being covered.
» Mention your website over and over. Remember that the listeners, readers, 
or viewers you are being interviewed in front of have no idea who you are and 
what you are about. This is your chance to sell yourself and your podcast. If 
your interviewer is good, you’ll be given ample opportunity to mention your 
website and podcast. If not, it’s up to you to find chances to drop your 
podcast’s name and URL.
» Stay positive. If the interviewer knows anything about podcasting, they’ll likely 
ask questions on the future of podcasting, the death of radio, amateur versus 
professional, and all sorts of other controversial topics. Unless your podcast is 
about podcasting, we recommend rising above the din. This conversation 
doesn’t serve you or the audience. Point out how your podcast addresses the 
issue and resist the temptation to get into an argument — unless your podcast 
is about arguing with interviewers. In that case, go right ahead.
Generating buzz
All the processes we outline in this chapter are geared toward one thing: generat￾ing buzz for your podcast. The more folks you can get talking about your show, the 
better. We don’t buy the “any publicity is good publicity” line, but we do think 
“Hey have you heard about?” conversations are the best form of advertising 
you can get.
Sometimes, you need to take the message to the masses. Find a discussion group 
or an online forum germane to your podcast’s area of interest and start posting. 
When you post, follow standard forum and community etiquette — don’t start out 
with “Hey, I’m new to the group and have this great podcast!” Instead, listen in 
on the conversation, comment on a few threads, and get people used to your voice 
before you hit them with the come-listen-to-my-podcast pitch. In fact, the best 234 PART 4 Start Spreadin’ the News about Your Podcast
way to pitch your podcast is to never utter those words. Instead, offer up things 
like “Last week on my podcast, I covered the very thing you were talking about, 
Jill.” It shows you’re paying attention to the conversation and not just looking to 
spam a newsgroup or mailing list with your podcast URL.
A fine line exists between tasteful self-promotion and outright spamming. Gen￾erating buzz isn’t the same as advertising. Advertising has its place, but most 
forums don’t welcome it. If you can’t decide whether your post contains too much 
advertising, it probably does.5

Chapter 14
Podcasting and AI
Artificial intelligence (AI) covers a lot of ground. There’s ML, LLMs, DSPs, 
VAEs, and so many other acronyms they could turn alphabet soup into a 
seven-course meal. In this chapter, we focus on generative AI and machine 
learning (ML), and how they can streamline your podcast workflow from audio 
and video editing to your blog posts to social media.
Machine learning has been around for a few years, but generative AI (GenAI) 
broke into the public consciousness in late 2022 with ChatGPT, which we cover 
later in the chapter. The race began as software companies, AI developers, and 
entrepreneurs looked for ways to tap AI’s potential to save time, produce more, 
and work more efficiently. Podcasters were no exception in benefitting from this 
innovation. As we discovered while researching this chapter, a lot of cool stuff is 
available that can save you time and help you work not only efficiently but 
ethically.
Until recently, the way most people thought about editing a podcast was to import 
the recorded audio into an app such as Audacity or Adobe Audition and edit the 
waveforms (those squiggly lines representing the audio). Those days may be 
numbered as new apps apply AI and make it even easier to import your audio (or 
video), generate a transcript, edit the transcript, and change the related audio. 
You can even update the text and have AI create new audio so you don’t have to 
re-record!

The field of artificial intelligence changes constantly, so we encourage you to join 
online forums, go to in-person meetups, and subscribe to podcasts about the 
evolution of AI.
Editing Made Easy with Hindenburg PRO
The Hindenburg PRO app enables you to import audio and create a text transcript 
(or manuscript, as they call it). Then, as you edit the text, the audio is updated. For 
example, suppose you were talking about an upcoming date in your recording and 
said, “Join us for our live event coming up in June — no wait, that should be July. 
Join us for our live event coming up in July.” Much like editing a document, simply 
select and delete the text you don’t want. When you export your creation, the June 
mistake is gone.
When you’ve finished editing the text, you simply export the audio. If you prefer, 
you can still access the waveforms and edit them in a more traditional fashion, 
while the software keeps the manuscript up to date.
Hindenburg PRO also offers multi-track editing, automatic leveling as you import 
audio files, and a free 30-day trial. Follow these steps to get started with Hinden￾burg PRO:
1. Get the software:
a. Navigate to the Hindenburg website at https://pfdbook.link/hindenburg.
b. Create an account and set up a service level to suit your needs (or use the free 
30-day trial.)
c. Click the Buy button in the Hindenberg PRO section or the Trial button at the top 
of the page for Hindenburg PRO.
d. Download the software, and then follow the standard installation instructions 
for your computer.
e. Open the Hindenburg PRO app, and enter your username and password. If you 
purchased the software, also enter your license key.
2. On the left side of the Hindenburg PRO screen, click the track labeled 
Speak. Then import your audio:
a. Choose File  ➪   Import or click the import icon on the toolbar.
b. In the window that appears, navigate to and select the audio file on your local 
hard drive.
c. Click Open.CHAPTER 14 Podcasting and AI 239
Hindenburg PRO automatically sets the audio levels, saving you time by raising 
the lows and bringing down the loud spots.
If you have music in addition to your spoken words in the imported recording, 
go to Hindenburg PRO’s settings (choose Hindenberg PRO  ➪   Settings), click 
the Advanced tab, and deselect Perform Automatic Leveling after Import and 
Record. As your music fades in or out, automatic leveling may try to bring up 
those low spots, thus making the fade ineffective.
3. Click the header on the audio track (the part with the name of your audio 
track), and choose Tools  ➪   Transcribe to create the manuscript.
4. If your audio track has multiple speakers, select the Multiple Speakers 
option in the Transcribe popup window that appears.
Hindenburg PRO automatically recognizes and labels the speakers in the 
manuscript. By default, each speaker has a name, such as F2 S1. To change the 
speaker name, click the first label and rename it. All instances of that speaker 
are updated. Repeat this for each additional speaker to make it easier to 
determine where to make edits.
5. When prompted, click OK to download the transcription services, 
allowing you to work offline if needed. When the download completes, 
click OK again.
This prompt appears only the first time you use the transcribe tool.
6. Click OK in the Transcribe popup window.
The manuscript appears in the bottom half of the screen, as shown in 
Figure 14-1. When the transcription is complete, Hindenburg PRO displays 
the text in the Manuscript box.
7. Click Close to close the Transcribe pop-up and complete the process.
8. Edit the manuscript in the bottom half of the screen.
Use your cursor to select text in the manuscript; the corresponding section of 
the waveform above will be selected as well. You can do the reverse: Select 
parts of the waveform and the corresponding text in the manuscript will be 
highlighted.
• Once the text is selected, use basic editing operations such as cut, copy, 
paste, and delete.
• If the text is incorrect in the transcript, use ⌘ +Enter (Mac), or Ctrl+Enter 
(Windows) to edit the text. Just note that changing the text does not affect 
the audio because Hindenburg PRO does not have text-to-speech 
capabilities.240 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
9. After you have completed your edits, click anywhere in the audio section 
(top half of the screen.)
The audio track labels are now selected and the manuscript section is dese￾lected. This is important because the next step is to export. If you were to leave 
the manuscript section selected, you would export the manuscript.
10. Export the file:
a. Choose File  ➪   Export, or click the export icon on the toolbar at the top of the app.
b. Provide a name for your file and identify the file format. If you plan to continue 
editing in another app such as Audacity or Audition, export the file as 
WAV. If your production is complete, export to MP3. For most cases, you’ll 
be fine using Med Quality (Stereo, 128kbps) for the Encoding setting. Check 
out Chapter 9 for more information on bit rates and file sizes.
c. Click Save.

and follow the instructions to upload an audio sample of your voice. (You
