SPEAK UP, SOUND OFF: GenAI AND THE 
HUMAN VOICE
Having software re-create your voice for quick corrections is incredibly cool. Your 
podcasting authors can speak of a time when pickups — the time when speaking 
mistakes — would slow down production. Now tools such as Descript can do the heavy 
lifting, turning a hiccup into the perfect take. But when AI reads an entire paragraph or 
an entire passage, something really cool becomes incredibly unsettling.
Descript is classified as a large language model (LLM), a subset of deep learning (DL). 
The AI learns by listening to samples of your voice, studying details such as cadence, 
pitch, tone, and emphasis. When provided with a few words here and there, LLMs can 
easily re-create a single word. Offer an LLM hours of your voice (and yes, creating hours 
of your voice is easy to do when podcasting) and the AI service can produce more 
robust results. ElevenLabs (https://pfdbook.link/elevenlabs) has its own 
generative voice AI service that can create an uncanny reproduction of your voice 
reading pages of content. With such a timesaver, why bother recording?
(continued)244 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
Adding ChatGPT to Your Toolbelt
Now we get to the application both revered and reviled. ChatGPT (https://
pfdbook.link/chatgpt) exploded on the scene in late 2022. Since then, millions 
of people have typed their questions and commands (known as prompts) and 
the chatbot has given its GenAI-based answers.
ChatGPT is a juggernaut of innovation, allowing you to get a head start on various 
writing projects. ChatGPT is not always perfect, but it continues to evolve. It’s also 
controversial, with students and professionals alike being called out for acts of 
plagiarism.
Depending on how you apply it in your podcast production, ChatGPT can serve as 
an incredible timesaver in your podcasting workflow. Imagine that you’ve just 
finished recording your podcast and have used one of the applications mentioned 
earlier in this chapter or perhaps another tool to create a transcript. You can ask 
ChatGPT to create a show summary or social media entry based on the transcript 
text, as shown in Figure  14-4. Within seconds, you have an accompanying 
blogpost and several posts for Threads, Facebook, and Instagram ready to go. 
ChatGPT, thanks to a simple prompt, allows you to quickly get on with life!
Well, almost.
Remember that part about not always being perfect? Think of ChatGPT as an 
assistant — a very new assistant. We recommend that you review anything AI￾generated before making it public.
In the following steps, you create a social media post based on the transcript of 
your latest episode:
1. Create a transcript of your audio.
You can use one of the applications mentioned earlier in this chapter or 
another tool.
Well, LLMs can’t get right certain things: variety of inflection, intent, and — in many 
cases — pronunciation. Where AI excels in efficiency and productivity, it lacks in cre￾ativity and originality. The human voice is still essential in creating quality audio con￾tent. There is also a matter of authenticity, which will only grow in value the more AI 
emerges into this field.
(continued)CHAPTER 14 Podcasting and AI 245
2. Copy the text of the transcript.
3. Navigate to ChatGPT’s site at https://pfdbook.link/chatgpt.
4. In the text box at the bottom, type the following command (also referred 
to as a prompt):
Summarize this podcast transcript for a social media post explaining what was 
talked about.
5. Paste your transcript after the colon and press Enter.
In a few seconds, ChatGPT produces a few lines of text based on your 
transcript.
6. Carefully review ChatGPT’s response.
If you don’t like what you see, repeat Steps 4 and 5 until you get something 
close.
7. Copy the text from the browser window, and paste it into your blog 
software, social media site, or elsewhere.
8. Edit the text to make it sound like your own voice and to fix errors.
9. Save, post, or publish the final content.
When in doubt, return to Step 8. Read and re-read what ChatGPT creates from 
your prompt, and then craft the result to how it would sound if you were to read 
it out loud. Although ChatGPT learns your style based on its interactions with you, 
this type of intimacy doesn’t happen right away. Watch those early responses to 
your prompts carefully. Then, after a few podcast seasons are under your belt, 
watch those responses to your prompt carefully. (Yes, we’re repeating ourselves. 
You’ll find out why as you keep reading.)

Having a transcript of your podcast not only helps ChatGPT when composing 
show notes and social posts but also adds a layer of accessibility to your audio and 
video production. Now, with a clean and accurate transcript attached to your 
show, deaf and hard-of-hearing folks can follow along with the show. Addition￾ally, posting the episode’s full transcript online can help with SEO because that 
text would be highly searchable.
Experiment with different ChatGPT prompts to get different results. For example, 
if your summary was good for a blog post but too long for social media, try adding 
something like in four sentences.
PUT YOUR HAND IN THE (BLACK) BOX, 
YOUNG ATREIDES: UNDERSTANDING 
HOW ChatGPT LEARNS
While exploring the world of artificial intelligence, you might come across the term black 
box technology. In simplest terms, it’s how a majority of AI innovations are described in 
how they work. A group of computers make up a layer, where data is entered and pro￾cessed and a result is generated. These layers are referred to as the following:
• Input: Where the data is entered.
• Hidden: Where the data is run through a set of routines and processes. Basically, 
this is where the “magic” happens; and the more hidden layers you have, the more 
intense the processing and the better the results.
• Output: The results of your data being processed.
With ChatGPT, it’s unclear exactly what happens in those hidden layers. Even AI devs 
sound like The Doctor, describing what’s happening at this point as a “timey-wimey￾wibbly-wobbly” process. Hidden layers are a secret wrapped in an enigma, smothered 
in special sauce.
But as far as how ChatGPT writes its results, what we know is about as dangerous as 
the Gom Jabbar at Paul Atreides’s neck.
First, ChatGPT looks at your various prompts to learn your style of writing. The more you 
interact with it, the closer the results will sound as though you wrote them. Many AI bros 
out there will encourage you to have conversations with the app to teach it your writing 
voice. This is one way ChatGPT learns how to write desired content from your prompt.CHAPTER 14 Podcasting and AI 247
Helping AI Generate the Best Results: 
Prompt Engineering
AI works with you to create content. The AI can’t generate good content unless 
you offer the model a good prompt. Your request should be clear, concise, and as 
close as you can get to what you want, either textually or visually. To get the most 
out of your GenAI experience, you need to understand prompt engineering, an 
approach where you structure your prompt with the following elements: 
» Main subject: Is the focus of the content an object, a person, or a location? 
Lead your prompt with the main subject.
» Scene description: Is the scene indoors or out? Are you in the wilderness or 
in the Great Hall? You can be creative in setting the scene, but keep your 
description simple and concise.
» Emotional tone or atmosphere: Happy, sad, mysterious, dark? Here’s where 
you add depth to your work.
» Styles: If you’re dealing with text, you might stipulate SEO-ready or AP style. If 
you’re working with visuals, you might specify anime, photo-realistic, or a Van 
Gogh painting.
» Additional elements: For text, you might set a word limit or ask for a bulleted 
list related to your topic. Visual elements might include a lighthouse in the 
background, a storm in the distance, or a sunny day.
» Perspective: First or third person? Close-up or wide shot?
» Action: An example of a textual action is asking for a depiction of climbing a 
mountain. A visual action is walking a dog or driving a car.
» Context: Are you experiencing a dream? Are you lost in New York City? Is 
everything happening at an amusement park? The more context you can 
offer, the better.
The other way it learns how to write content for you is by scouring the internet for previ￾ously published content — content written by other people — and then generating it in 
your voice. No citations given, no sources credited, opening the door for accusations of 
plagiarism. Even if these accusations never make it to a courtroom, your reputation can 
be damaged.
If you are using ChatGPT for anything beyond simple prompts (“Write me a 200-word 
description based on this transcript. Make the summary optimized for search engines.”), 
be ready to edit, be ready to rewrite, and be ready to give credit where credit is due.248 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
» Any relevant keywords: If the image you’re creating is from a video game, 
drop in keywords like video games, Destiny 2, Mass Effect, Halo, Guardians.
To practice prompt engineering, in this section you work with Leonardo.AI 
(https://pfdbook.link/leonardoai). This online app is free to the public and a 
favorite location for GenAI. You too can offer your work to the home page display 
of users’ AI-generated creations. Perhaps you can create show art for your podcast 
and its host blog.
To get started using Leonardo.AI, follow these steps:
1. Get the app:
a. Go to https://pfdbook.link/leonardoai and either create an account or 
use Google, Microsoft, or Apple to activate your account. Along with the 
browser version, mobile versions are available for iOS and Android.
b. Follow the on-screen instructions to set up your username and get started. The 
screen shown in Figure 14-5 appears.
2. In the left sidebar, click Image Generation to enter creation mode.
3. In the Type a Prompt area, enter the following and then click the 
Generate button:
Space Warriors defending Earth from aliens
If you hover your cursor over the Generate button, you’ll see “This Will Use 20 
Tokens.” When you first sign up to Leonardo.AI, you start with 150 tokens. Each 
AI generation will cost you 20 tokens, but within 24 hours, your credits will

result offers atmosphere, depth, and detail absent from the first prompt.
There’s also a delightful nod to Bungie’s legacy — before the game studio made 
Destiny 2, they created Halo. So yeah, we see what you did there, Leonardo.AI.
You can’t master prompt engineering overnight. You have to work with the 
prompts and their composition to unlock the potential of what AI can create in 
text, images, and — if you’re looking ahead to impressive visuals for upcoming 
productions — video and audio.
The examples in the community gallery in Leonardo.AI include the prompts that 
were used. Study these prompts and keywords to refine your attempts at prompt 
engineering. You might find a cadence and pattern to follow that will help you tap 
into what GenAI can do for you and your podcast.

The Ethics of AI
We saved the best part for last. Buckle up.
In the time it took us to write this edition, artificial intelligence has evolved into 
something better. And by the time you read this, its capabilities, efficiencies, and 
benefits will have expanded exponentially. If you ever wondered about a good fit 
for “The genie has escaped” analogy, look no further than AI.
AI can be a polarizing topic, especially in creative circles. While AI enthusiasts (or, 
as Tee calls them, AI bros) beat their chests and proclaim, “You can’t stop the 
future, Luddites!” and artists go on strike over studios considering AI as a poten￾tial solution for script generation, video production, and even actors-on-demand, 
developers continue to push boundaries without fear or forethought. Sora from 
OpenAI is the disruptor-of-the-moment at the time of this writing.
We talk a bit about Sora later.
Innovation is a beautiful thing, but it shouldn’t come at the cost of ethics. Ethics,
in its most basic definition, consists of the standards of conduct whose authority 
is determined by reason rather than custom. Practical concepts that you might 
encounter when looking at ethics include the following:
» What is good for me, and what is considered the overall social good?
» What is the relationship between what is good for me and what is good for 
society on a whole?
» What is my moral obligation to a certain concept (in this case, AI)?
» How does the free will come into play here?
» What is the real worth of positive morality in regards to innovation?
Bet you didn’t expects concepts this deep in a For Dummies book. Let’s stay on 
brand, lighten the mood, and look at the ethics of AI as it applies to one of our 
favorite episodes of the original Star Trek. Captain Kirk and Mister Spock are 
evaluating the latest creation from Dr. Richard Daystrom: the M-5 computer. 
Daystrom, who created the original computers of the Enterprise (and all the other 
vessels in Starfleet), believes he has created the next step in space exploration. 
Spock shares his evaluation of this game-changing technology with his captain:
Spock: The ship reacted more rapidly than human control could have maneuvered 
her. Tactics, deployment of weapons, all indicate an immense sophistication in 
computer control.CHAPTER 14 Podcasting and AI 251
Kirk: Machine over man, Spock? It was impressive. Might even be practical.
Spock: Practical, Captain? Perhaps. But not desirable. Computers make excellent 
and efficient servants, but I have no wish to serve under them.
This is from “The Ultimate Computer” (Season 2, Episode 24), written for televi￾sion by award-winning science fiction author D.C. Fontana. It premiered in 1968, 
nearly a month before 2001: A Space Odyssey opened at the box office.
Let that marinate for a moment.
While we open the door for ribbing by summarizing our thoughts on ground￾breaking technology referencing the same series that brought us some of the most 
cringe-worthy moments in science fiction (“Miri” and “Spock’s Brain” — we’re 
looking at you), Star Trek gets it right with this episode. “The Ultimate Computer” 
is prophetic in not only the motivation behind M-5 but how Kirk questions 
whether M-5 is the good thing that Daystrom and Starfleet make it out to be. Even 
Forbes (https://pfdbook.link/forbes) pointed out in 2011 how well the episode 
dealt with the implications (and potential dangers) of AI.
Ethical AI, though, is not drawing proverbial lines in the technological sand. It’s a 
discipline and an approach to AI that asks difficult questions, and what makes it 
even more difficult to implement is that everyone involved in an AI-driven project 
must be on board with how AI is applied and how far it’s implemented.
For example, suppose you’re using Hindenburg PRO with your podcast. Is it 
unethical to have Hindenburg PRO create a manuscript from your podcast? 
Certainly not. While it does replace the work a human would undertake as a 
transcriber, you still need someone to review it for accuracy. What about the easy 
corrections when unexpected mistakes pop up? Is there a breach of ethics in edit￾ing the text and updating the audio without the voice artist being present? Nope. 
It’s all good and aboveboard, provided the voice actor understands that pick-ups
(the quick re-recording of certain lines if a mistake occurs) are covered. If 
everyone involved is good with that, you’re still ethically solid.
But let’s say Chuck, without Tee’s permission, fed ElevenLabs hours of Tee’s voice 
and then created a bumper for Technorama, saying “This is Tee Morris, and when 
it comes to podcasts, nothing beats Technorama!” Hey, it’s not even 30 seconds 
and Tee’s a friend of both the podcast and Chuck. No big deal, right? Well, the fact 
that it was without Tee’s permission is a double-whammy of unethical applica￾tion, even if it turned out that Tee would have agreed to it. Another consideration 
is that Tee didn’t record a word of it, but it’s his voice. What else could we get Tee 
to say? What if, using ElevenLabs, Chuck writes an essay or an editorial and feeds 
their AI Tee’s voice to create a full podcast with his voice?
This is when the slope gets particularly slippery.252 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
COME WITH ME AND YOU’LL BE IN 
A WORLD OF AI GENERATION!
In February 2024, an ambitious special-events vendor calling itself The House of 
Illuminati announced their premiere of Willy’s Chocolate Experience. The website (now 
offline) featured GenAI artwork (like the one pictured here) promising a fully immersive 
experience of fun and imagination for the entire family. Much in the same vein of 
Doctor Who, Harry Potter, and the works of Vincent Van Gogh, audiences would lose 
themselves in the wild and wonderful world inspired by Willy Wonka, Golden Tickets, 
and (of course) chocolate.
The event opened and, shortly after, went viral. For all the wrong reasons.
The event — much like its marketing — featured GenAI in every aspect. Photo opportu￾nity backdrops were the same surreal GenAI artwork as featured on the website. The 
scripts, according to Paul Connell, cast as the Wonka-inspired lead Willy McDuff, were 
“15 pages of AI-generated gibberish” (https://pfdbook.link/wce). Actress Kirsty 
Paterson, the sad oompa-loompa photographed in an ill-equipped candy lab, told 
Vulture, “When I got the script the night before I was like, ‘Oh, this is not . . .’ I kind of CHAPTER 14 Podcasting and AI 253
AI is a helpful tool for detecting errors and making minor corrections, creating a 
transcript from your production, and drafting a summary of your podcast and its 
many episodes. AI is a fantastic timesaver for leveling audio, detecting sync points 
in video, assisting you in creating a multicamera setup, and generating SEO key￾words that you can use to help your podcast appear in the top results of search 
engines. Can AI create an entire episode based on a script and a voice sample you 
give it? Yes. But remember, just because you can do a thing, doesn’t mean you 
should do a thing  — especially without permission. Using your own material 
wherever possible is best, but creating content with AI comes at a severe risk 
because AI learns by using other people’s material as its source. Ethics should 
always be regarded when AI is utilized in content creation.
Particularly unsettling are the AI innovators themselves barreling forward while 
AI bros cheer them on from the sidelines and boardrooms where deals are made. 
In a recent interview with the Wall Street Journal, the chief technology officer of 
OpenAI, Mira Mirati, was more than happy to celebrate the rollout of Sora, a GenAI 
app that could create high-resolution video from a simple prompt. The interview 
(https://pfdbook.link/sora) was progressing at a good pace, and it looked like 
Mirati and OpenAI had achieved another amazing milestone — until WSJ’s Joanna 
Stern asked a more-than-fair question:
Stern: What data was used to train Sora?
Mirati: We used publicly available data and licensed data.
Stern: So, videos on YouTube?
Mirati: I’m actually not sure about that.
Stern: Okay. Videos from Facebook, Instagram?
thought it was AI-generated” (https://pfdbook.link/vulture). While House of 
Illuminati’s sole proprietor, Billy Coull, insisted that GenAI was not used for the event’s 
script, the BBC discovered in 2023 that Coull had listed 17 self-published novels on 
Amazon, all released within the same month. An Amazon review described one of his 
titles as having “all the hallmarks of being written by AI.”
And to top off all this: Not a bit of chocolate to be found anywhere.
If you want a look at unethical AI in practice or AI without regulations or restrictions, the 
stories surrounding Willy’s Chocolate Experience offer a case study. Without ethics in 
place, AI is no longer an innovation disrupting business as we know it but a train wreck 
of great ideas in the hands of people who have no clue how to see a creative work to its 
final release. It’s essential to regard AI as less of a creative resource and more as a tool 
to help creative people fine-tune what they’ve made from inspiration to fruition.254 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
Mirati: You know, if they were publicly available, publicly available to use, there 
might be the data, but I’m not sure. I’m not confident about it.
Stern: What about Shutterstock? I know you guys have a deal with them.
Mirati: I’m just not going to go into the details of the data that was used, but it was 
publicly available data or licensed data.
Publicly available data covers a lot of ground. How ethical would it be to train an AI 
model using several YouTube podcasts? How would you feel if you — a content 
creator — saw an AI-generated advertisement featuring your face? Did you give 
permission for this? While your video is available to the public on YouTube, does 
that mean anyone can re-create your likeness for their production without your 
permission or without compensation to you?
With concerns of deep fakes, misinformation, and misrepresentation online, 
ethics is not a luxury but a necessity. Mirati saying that she is not sure about the 
details in the data used to train AI doesn’t satisfy those whose ethics could 
potentially be violated — and it isn’t a good look, especially when your title is 
chief technology officer.
Ethics will always ask the tough questions when it comes to AI. When those blindly 
pushing AI state, “AI solutions will free up time for creative tasks” it will be the 
ethical folks who ask, “What exactly does that mean?” or “Like what?” We’ve 
heard that AI will allow us humans to engage in more interesting pursuits. We’ve 
also heard that sentiment before, just phrased a little differently:
“You’re frightened because you can’t understand it. I’m going to show you. 
I’m going to show all of you. It takes four hundred thirty people to man a 
starship. With this, you don’t need anyone. One machine can do all those 
things they send men out to do now. Men no longer need die in space or on 
some alien world. Men can live and go on to achieve greater things.”
— Dr. RICHARD DAYSTROM
Star Trek, “The Ultimate Computer”
written by D.C. Fontana
Whoever says Star Trek isn’t quality science fiction isn’t paying attention.CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 255
Chapter 15
Podcasting on YouTube
I
n its first year, podcasting established itself as an exciting audio option. It was 
truly “The People’s Radio” for your computer, promising a fresh alternative to 
cookie-cutter corporate radio. A few innovative podcasters, though, took a 
closer look at the way RSS 2.0 worked and thought, “Wait a second — if we can do 
this with audio, what about video?” These brave few decided to put moving 
pictures to their podcasts, and video podcasting came to be.
With YouTube now recognizing podcasts and even offering their own directory for 
podcasters, video might be a viable platform for your show. Working with video 
isn’t easy but can be just as rewarding as working with audio. In this chapter, you 
learn about the video component that podcasting offers producers everywhere, set 
yourself up on YouTube, and work with an editing program to create your first 
video podcast.
A Hostile Welcome: The Demands of Video
“A Hostile Welcome” might come across as an imposing title for this section, but 
after working with video for three decades and some change, we can state that it’s 
accurate. While video presents incredible possibilities for podcasters of all levels, 
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Working in audio versus working 
in video
» Signing up your podcast for 
a YouTube account
» Editing in Adobe Premiere
» Creating your first podcast on 
YouTube256 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
anyone new to the medium discovers quickly that video production is not easy. At 
all. Following are some of the challenges to working with video:
» The learning curve: Despite what the commercials for laptops insinuate, 
editing video is not and never has been a push-button technology. A lot goes 
into video, even at the most basic level. And even with years of content 
creation, you can still learn about other essential skills, such as adjustment 
layers, multicam editing, and opening title templates from third-party vendors.
» More production resources: With audio, the aim is to create the cleanest, 
most polished auditory experience. When you work in video, as shown in 
Figure 15-1, you have a lot more to contend with than just unwanted noise 
pollution. Now you wrestle with lighting, camera angles, wardrobe, make-up, 
and more. Editing also proves more difficult because timing now has a visual 
aspect. In YouTube videos, jump cuts (going from one scene or camera angle 
to another without a transition) are common and the technique is a timesaver 
while editing. But is it professional? Debatable, at best. Video demands time, 
attention, and patience.
FIGURE 15-1:
Video production 
entails working 
with factors such 
as lighting, 
wardrobe, 
location, and 
camera angles.CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 257
» Episode frequency: The added demands of production make posting new 
content a challenge. Even a biweekly posting schedule poses an incredible 
undertaking with casting, pre-production, filming, editing, and post-production — 
while maintaining a day job that is probably financing your ambitious production. 
And if your cast and crew are volunteers, even a monthly posting schedule 
can be daunting. Count on your editing crew in the early stages of your video 
podcasting to be a crew of one: You. If you’re not proficient in editing, producing 
an episode may take longer than you think.
A buffer of episodes you’ve already edited and have ready to post is essential 
when working with video. Before launching your podcast, have two to three 
months’ worth of video episodes in the can, ready to post. While that might 
sound extreme, you’ll find that cushion valuable when working with video.
» Audio or nothing: Some apps and platforms will not approve your feed if you 
feature video episodes. Spotify syndicates only certain video podcast feeds. 
(Think the top of Spotify’s podcasting food chain, and you’ll understand what 
certain entails.) So while video is tempting in many ways, you may create 
content that goes unseen.
OH, YOU TEASE!
We don’t deny that video can take a lot longer to record, edit, and upload than its 
audio-only counterpart. But you can also make quick, short videos to promote your 
podcast, whether it’s audio or video! Many podcasters have turned to services like 
Instagram Reels and YouTube Shorts to make short vignettes, or teasers, to promote 
a show with a larger production. While at your favorite convention, expo, or weekend 
event, you may find yourself gathering interviews or some other footage for your regu￾lar show. Take a few minutes to tell your audience what you’re working on. Grab your 
mobile phone and do a quick broadcast, or put together a 1-minute moment that offers 
a sneak peek as to what you have planned for your next creative project (like this con￾versation Tee is having with his studio mascot, Benedick the GamerCat, during a tense 
moment in The Last of Us).
(continued)258 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
Suppose that you decide to add a video component to your podcast. Contending 
with the preceding obstacles, you may edit your video with Apple iMovie, Apple 
Final Cut, DaVinci Resolve, or Adobe Premiere. After the episode is ready, you’ll 
face a new set of challenges:
» File size: A 1-minute video clip shot at a high-definition resolution (1920 x 
1080 pixels) comes out to roughly 50MB, give or take a few megabytes. 50MB 
for 1 minute of HD video. And you want to create a 15-minute video episode. 
“How do I get my file size smaller?” podcasters ask on various discussion 
boards about video. The reply — “Make a shorter episode” — may come 
across as snarky, but it’s the only solution. The saying “You can’t fit 25 pounds 
of sugar into a 5-pound bag” applies to video production, as well.
» Bandwidth demands: Bandwidth, especially if your podcast is 500MB per 
episode (and remember, that’s 10 minutes of video), can be costly. If you 
launch a podcast with 1,000 listeners, for example, you’ve gone through 
50GB of bandwidth for that one episode. If you find an additional 2,000 
subscribers in your second week, you’ll potentially burn well over 1TB of 
your monthly bandwidth within your first two weeks. What about the 
weeks — and the listeners — to come? What does your web host offer, 
and can it handle this kind of traffic for your podcast?CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 259
A majority of video podcasting still manages to occur in various resolutions, run￾ning times, and production quality levels, most of them backed by major studios 
or production houses with healthy budgets. Some innovative filmmakers are cre￾ating amazing productions with shoestring budgets, but these podcasts are few 
and far between.
YouTube: We’re Here to Help
In 2005, Chad Hurley, Steve Chen, and Jawed Karim founded what was to become 
the home of video content on the internet: YouTube. From the humble beginnings 
above a pizzeria and a Japanese restaurant in San Mateo, California, YouTube 
started with user clips limited to 100 megabytes and 30 seconds of footage. By 
December 2005, within the year of their official launch, the site received 8 million 
views a day. In 2024, nearly 20 years later, YouTube was receiving an estimated 
5 billion views a day, generating nearly $30 billion in revenue per year.
If you were wondering why there’s a chapter in this book about podcasting on 
YouTube, there it is.
YouTube is now powered by Google, and when you couple the world’s largest 
resource for video content with the most powerful search engine on the internet, 
you have an incredible ally for you and your desire to add a video component to 
your podcast.
In July 2022, YouTube introduced a podcast directory in response to user feedback 
and the growing popularity of podcasters who add video to their once audio-only 
production. This dedicated page for podcasts is shown in Figure 15-2. Podcasting 
as a platform was now media being delivered to you on a (usually free) subscrip￾tion system. When people subscribe to a podcast, they receive a notification on 
their computer, mobile device, or media center that new episodes are ready.
If you have a Gmail account, good news: You have a YouTube account. But more 
importantly, does your podcast have a YouTube account? If not, follow these steps:
1. Go to https://pfdbook.link/google and click the Create Account 
button.
2. Click Personal.
You have two options for creating an account: Personal and Business. The 
business account requires that you set up a business profile. You can choose 
to do this later. For now, keep it simple and create a personal account on 
Google.260 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
3. For the account contact, enter your name or your podcast’s title.
This is a personal preference.
4. For the account username, enter your podcast’s title.
5. Create a password or a passphrase and confirm it.
While passwords should be difficult to crack but easy to remember, passphrases 
are now recommended by cybersecurity experts as upping the difficulty level for 
being hacked. Passphrases involve more characters, so if you take something easy 
like “I am a podcaster” and rework it as “!am@p0dcasteR” for your password field, 
you’ve created a very strong passphrase that meets the criteria of many platforms.
6. Enter a phone number, preferably a mobile number.
If you need to verify your login to your Gmail or YouTube account, your phone 
will be an option for receiving notifications from Google. This is to be sure that 
only you or your trusted associates are logging into the account.
7. After reviewing the terms of service and the privacy policy, click Next to 
complete the process.
You’ve set up a Google account, which automatically sets up your YouTube account. 
You can personalize your account with a profile picture (preferably your podcast’s 
logo), personal details, and additional safeguards to protect your data.
You can now go to YouTube (https://pfdbook.link/youtube) and start filling in 
the blanks of your account, more commonly referred to as a channel. Create a 
sharp-looking banner across the top, fill in the details behind the profile, and lay 
down the foundations of your YouTube channel like the channel featured in 
Figure 15-3. To find out how to make a channel feel more personal, read YouTube 
Channels For Dummies by Rob Ciampa, Theresa Go, Matt Ciampa, and Rich Murphy.

Adding the Video Element: Creating 
a Podcast for YouTube
Now that you’ve created your YouTube channel, you have to decide how you 
will bring video into your audio-centric podcast. For now, all you need are the 
following:
» A video-editing program, such as DaVinci Resolve, Blender, Apple iMovie, 
or Adobe Premiere Pro
» A plan
Keeping it simple: Introducing video 
to an audio podcast
The entry-level approach for podcasts on YouTube is to place your show’s logo on 
the video track from beginning to end, and use that as your video component. 
Easy, simple, and . . . dull as ditchwater.
FIGURE 15-3:
Add details to 
your YouTube 
channel to make 
the best first 
impression on 
new viewers.262 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
Yes, it’s the easiest way of getting your audio on YouTube, but this quick-and￾dirty approach will have a limited shelf-life with a YouTube audience. It would be 
nice to have something to look at. So let’s go and give this video some extra flair:
1. Launch your video-editing software.
Many applications are available to help you edit and produce video. For this 
chapter, we work with Adobe Premiere Pro. These steps might be a little 
different if you’re not using Adobe Premiere, but the basics should be 
the same.
2. Create a project, and import your podcast episode and a folder of several 
images related to your project.
In Chapter 8 we talk about royalty-free music for your podcast. Membership to 
services such as Envato Elements may also offer access to royalty-free images 
(and video!) that you can use in your video.
3. Drag the podcast audio from your Project window to your timeline 
window, and release it on the audio track.
For this exercise, we use a 16-minute episode.
4. Go to the folder of images you imported and drag-and-drop one of the 
photos to the beginning of the video track.
Your video editor should offer ways for you to scale your photo to fill the 
screen, if necessary.
5. Grab the end point of the photo you just dropped onto the video track, 
and drag it.
To follow along with the example, drag the end point so that the duration of 
the photo is close to 3 minutes, as shown in Figure 15-4. Different video editors 
have different ways of lengthening a still image or a block of text, but many 
support a simple drag-and-drop process.
FIGURE 15-4:
You can lengthen 
the running time 
of a clip to a 
duration you 
need for your 
project.CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 263
6. Go to the folder of images and drag another photo to the end point of the 
previous photo.
7. Grab the end point of this new photo, and drag it so the duration of the 
photo is close to 3 minutes.
8. From the Transitions folder, click and drag Dissolve to the meeting point 
of the two images.
Dissolve (or fade) is a simple transition from one image to the other. You 
should have a variety of transitions at your disposal. Feel free to play around 
with them accordingly.
9. Repeat Steps 4 to 8 until you reach the end of your podcast.
The last image of your podcast should be the show’s logo.
10. Choose File  ➪   Save to save your work.
11. Choose File  ➪   Export  ➪   Media to create your video content.
You now have a video track of related images running the length of your 
podcast. Kind of cool, huh?
You’ve added a basic video component to your podcast and are ready to upload the 
episode to your YouTube account. But what if you want to do something with a bit 
more polish? Something that puts you right up there with the professionals? That 
requires a little more planning and a lot more resources. But the result will be 
incredibly awesome.
Pro-level video podcasting: Working with 
a multicam setup in Adobe Premiere
To see a multi-clip, or multicam, setup, look at Ghosts & Grit with Jack Osbourne. 
Jack Osbourne, the son of the Prince of Darkness himself, Ozzy Osbourne, has an 
incredible story to tell. On Ghosts & Grit, he shares that story: a passion for mixed 
martial arts, mental health awareness, pop culture, and what he is now known 
for  — paranormal investigation. It’s a simple setup: one camera on Jack and 
another on his guest. While Ghosts & Grit is available as a podcast on various plat￾forms such as Spotify and Apple, it’s also on YouTube.
How hard is it to make a podcast that looks like this? Here’s what you need: 
» Two cameras, preferably the same make and model
» Adequate lighting for you and your guest
» A clean(ish?) set264 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
» Both you and your guest looking presentable (nice wardrobe, hair styled, and 
so on)
» A digital recorder or a mixer to record the mic audio from your host and guest
Don’t worry about syncing camera and audio. You do that shortly. At the end of 
your recording session, you should have the following:
» Camera 1 (host) with audio and video
» Camera 2 (guest) with audio and video
» Master audio of both host and guest, balanced if possible
Now launch Adobe Premiere Pro. It’s time to get to work:
1. Create a new project and import your podcast from camera 1, camera 2, 
and the master audio.
2. In the Project window, select the camera 1 clip, the camera 2 clip, and the 
master audio.
3. Do the following:
a. Right-click any clip and select Create Multi-Clip Camera Source Sequence. You 
will then see the Create Multi-Camera Source Sequence window, seen in 
Figure 15-5.
b. Set the Sync Point to Audio — Track 1.
c. For Camera Names, select Enumerate Cameras.
The Multi-Clip Camera Source Sequence uses an artificial intelligence algorithm 
to sync your master audio with the video of your two cameras. If this is the first 
time you’re taking advantage of the multicam feature of Premiere, congratula￾tions on using AI in your production. To read more about AI and podcasting, 
see Chapter 14.
4. Go to the Program window and make sure the toggle multicam view icon 
(shown in the margin) is on.
5. Go to the Project window and find the multicam clip you just created. 
Right-click the clip and select New Sequence from Clip from the menu.
The sequence created from the multi-clip appears as one track of video and 
one track of audio. As shown in Figure 15-6, the left side contains the available 
sources for your sequence. The right side is your sequence-in-progress, where 
you can see which camera you’re currently trained on.
6. Begin the playback of your podcast by clicking the play icon in the 
Program window.CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 265

7. When you’re ready to change camera angles, click the desired camera in 
the left section of your Program window. When you want to change 
angles again, click the other offered camera.
If you click between camera angles and then stop playback, the moments you 
clicked appear in the timeline window as edit points in the sequence.
You can fine-tune the timing of any edit point by using the rolling edit tool 
(shown in the margin), which allows you to move an edit point earlier or 
later in a sequence.
8. Repeat Step 7 until you’ve finished reviewing the podcast.
Your sequence is now roughly edited.
If you find out that you’re on an angle you don’t want to be on, right-click in the 
sequence on the clip featuring that unwanted camera angle, and locate the 
Multi-Camera option in the offered menu. Select the camera you want to jump 
to, and the clip will switch to the desired angle.
9. Save you work by pressing Ctrl+S (Windows) or ⌘ +S (Mac).
10. To process your video for presentation, press Ctrl+M (Windows) or 
⌘ +M (Mac).
Multicam editing takes your production to a professional level and is an incredible 
timesaver when working with multiple camera sources. It may take some practice 
before you become proficient, but it’s worth the effort.
GET ON MY LAWN: THE EASE OF 
PODCASTING WITH STREAMYARD
When we describe recording a video element to your podcast, we make the bold 
assumption that everyone involved in your podcast — your co-hosts or potential 
guests — is in your home studio. However, some guests, especially subject matter 
experts, might not be able to come to your place and record.
Enter Streamyard (https://pfdbook.link/streamyard), which captures incom￾ing audio from the sources you establish on their website. You and the contributor 
at the remote location connect your video and audio settings with the Streamyard 
server. Although Streamyard sounds no different than a Zoom call, it enables you to 
brand content with logos, incorporate pre-recorded clips (such as intros, outros, and 
segues for segments in your podcast), separate audio files (for post-production mix￾ing), and create automatic recordings made available immediately for downloading. 
Streamyard offers many of the perks of in-studio recording with the capability to 
connect anywhere in the world, Wi-Fi permitting.CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 267
You can see a two-camera setup and its results in Jack Osbourne’s paranormal 
podcast, Ghosts & Grit. Being able to jump from angle to angle breaks up the mono￾tony of a single-camera setup and can even increase the pace of your podcast.
For additional camera setups and ideas, look at the three-camera setup in Viva la 
Dirt League’s We Were Told We Should Do a Podcast. Each host has their own camera, 
and the editing in this podcast is quick, clean, and polished. Video productions, 
though, are not always limited to two or three cameras. VLDL’s other YouTube 
channel, Viva la Dirt League D&D, features Adventures in Azerim and The Misfits. In 
their gaming productions, VLDL’s setup includes the following:
» Camera 1 (Robert Hartley, Dungeon Master)
» Camera 2 (one side of the table, featuring two actors)
» Camera 3 (another side of the table, featuring two actors)
» Camera 4 (elevated camera focused on the gaming table)
» Camera 5 (wide shot of table, DM, and all actors)
» Master audio (the DM and party members are all mic’d)
The results are incredible, and time flies by as you watch the players’ reactions to 
whatever Rob throws at them.
With each additional camera, keep in mind the additional demands for lighting 
and sound. Start simple with your video and then see how you can improve your 
production.
If you decide to work with a multicam setup, make sure your cameras are the same 
make and model. Mixing different models will result in varying results because 
not all camera optics are the same. Are there ways to correct this in post￾production? Possibly, but it may not be perfect and will require more time.
You may notice that the longer your podcast, the longer it takes for your video 
editor to process the video. The amount of RAM and processing power dictate how 
quickly the processing will take. Tee’s laptop, for example, is running a 2GHz 
Quad-Core Intel Core i5 and 16GB of RAM. If he’s creating a 5-minute video, the 
processing time can take between 30 and 60 minutes. Processing a 30- to 
40-minute production, with multiple tracks of video and audio and incorporated 
effects, can take 3 to 4 hours. When it comes to your usual posting time, remem￾ber to allocate time for the processing of video.
Now that your file is processed and ready for posting, it’s time to make your 
podcast happen on YouTube.268 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
Crossing the Finish Line: Taking Your 
Podcast to YouTube
YouTube channel all polished and prepared for visitors? Check. Media created for 
uploading? Check.
Now, here’s the thing to know about uploading and walking away from a video on 
YouTube. You really can’t do that anymore. A good amount of work goes into let￾ting people know something about your podcast.
Tell us about yourself: The Details window
You’re about to upload your video file to the YouTube servers. Once it’s there, it 
has to be processed for both standard and high-definition playback. This process￾ing is unlike the processing we did in Adobe Premiere. In YouTube, the processing 
creates a uniform format that people can play on any computer from any location.
And that’s okay because as your video processes, you’re going to work through 
other details that need attention. For each post on YouTube, you should complete 
the following:
» Title
» Description
» Thumbnail promoting your video
» Which playlist will feature your video
» Tags for search engines
» Category for your video
» Scheduling time for your video to go live
The better you complete these details, the easier it will be for Google and YouTube 
to find your production.
You begin the process by uploading your video and walking through the various 
steps needed to make sure you appear properly in YouTube.
1. Go to YouTube (https://pfdbook.link/youtube), single-click the create 
icon, located in the upper-right corner (and shown in the margin), and 
select Upload Video.CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 269
2. Either drag-and-drop your video to the window, or click Select Files and 
find and select your media on your computer.
The Details window appears.
3. In the Title field, replace the file title with the title you want for your 
podcast episode.
You can reuse the podcast title for your YouTube entry or create a more 
SEO-friendly title. For organization and consistency, it’s best to use the same 
title here as you used for the audio-only entry.
4. In the Description field, enter a brief description of your episode (seen in 
Figure 15-7).
To be consistent, use parts of your show notes here.
Many of the fields in the Details window have character limitations, so if your 
original entry has too many characters, you may have to edit it to fit. URLs 
relevant to your podcast episode are best shared here.
We skip the Thumbnail option for now. Later, we describe the details to 
consider when creating a thumbnail.

The Create a New Playlist dialog appears.

Podcasts are designated by a special tag in the listing of playlists on your 
channel.
8. In the Audience section, determine whether or not the podcast is 
appropriate for kids, and then click Show More.
9. Under Tags, come up with search terms (tags) relevant to your podcast 
episode.

this process until you are close to or have reached the limit, which is 
500 characters.
Tags are the terms you expect YouTube users to enter when searching. Tags 
are one of the most important details about your podcast to complete.
Coming up with good tags can be difficult, so we recommend turning to 
ChatGPT to suggest keywords. To find out how ChatGPT can help you with 
relevant keywords as well as episode summaries and more, see Chapter 14.
10. Under Category, choose the option appropriate to your podcast.
11. Review the screen for additional details to add. When you’re ready, click 
the Next button.
One page down. You’ve completed the toughest part of the YouTube listing. Next, 
you simply copy and paste the show notes portion of your podcast into YouTube.
Adding more to your podcast: 
Video elements
With the Details screen completed, you’re taken to the Video Elements screen. 
Video elements are little additions to your video that help people navigate your 
channel or invite them to visit other relevant podcasters on YouTube. On this 
screen, you can add the following to your video production:
» Subtitles: YouTube can provide automatic closed captioning for your 
production. If the quality of the subtitles aren’t to your liking, you can edit 
them or provide your own. The subtitles can be presented in another 
language but you’ll want to make sure any foreign language translation is 
using the proper grammar.CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 271
» End screen: Many YouTube creators end their productions as shown in 
Figure 15-8, with a subscribe button and a suggested video where people can 
find out more about their channel or interests. The end screen option here 
offers a few templates as well as action items for viewers.
» Cards: If someone is viewing YouTube on a desktop or laptop computer, a 
card can slide out at the top corner of the screen, encouraging the viewer to 
see what you, the video producer, recommend for further viewing. If you want 
to offer your audience a few action items while viewing your podcast, consider 
using cards. And after you do, click Next to move on.
Making sure you’re above board: Checks
From their first year online to the present, YouTube has tangled with creators over 
the issue of copyright. Who has permission to share comedy, music, and charac￾ters and settings? Even lawyers on YouTube (like the Legal Eagle himself) have 
stepped into this discussion and explained repeatedly what is and is not protected 
under satire, fair use, and other arguments.
YouTube is not taking any chances. The next window you see after clicking Next is 
the Checks window. The algorithms at YouTube and Google check that your video 
content is being used correctly and with permissions in place. Sometimes, you 
may receive a warning stating that you’re using content for which the artist grants

permission. Other times, there may be a copyright that prevents you from mon￾etizing the video. Investigate the issue and decide whether you want to re-edit the 
video, re-upload the fixed video, and re-enter all that you’ve just entered. (Oye!) 
Or, if you’re not interested in monetizing this video, you can let it slide.
This is YouTube’s final check to make sure that you (and the platform) are doing 
everything in your power to abide by copyright laws.
If everything looks good here, click that Next button one more time.
Drop it like it’s HAWT: Publishing your video
You’re one click away from making your podcast live on YouTube. At the bottom 
right of the Visibility window, the Next button now reads Publish. When you click 
that, you’ll be all set (because, at this point, your video has probably finished pro￾cessing). YouTube will list your podcast in their directory, and your content will be 
available as a video component.
We know you want to click that Publish button, but give us just a few more ticks.
The Visibility window is more than just going live with your episode. This is where 
you set up whether you want your video to be seen immediately or go live at a later 
date. Here’s a breakdown of what Visibility offers:
» Private: If you’re working with a group of people who have a say in how your 
final podcast runs, you might want to let them review the podcast before 
making it live to the public. Setting a video to private restricts who can view it. 
You must manually enter email addresses to generate invites to the people 
for whom you want to grant access to your video.
» Unlisted: Setting the visibility to unlisted prevents the public from knowing 
the podcast is there. Invites are not required — you can share the link to 
anyone you like. Just keep in mind that they can also share the link.
» Schedule: If you’ve followed our advice and built a buffer of video content, 
you may want to time the release of your audio and video podcasts simulta￾neously. In the Schedule section, you can time both your audio and video 
media to go live at the same time. Before the video goes public, it will remain 
private, meaning only you and your crew can watch it before it goes live.
» Set as Premiere: This option is a fantastic dipping-of-the-toe into the waters 
of going live with a studio audience. Set a date and time a week or two in 
advance to give people notice that you’re hosting a premiere. You can also set 
up your premiere with a countdown clock, building the hype to the video’s 
launch. Build anticipation for the premiere by circulating a link that takes CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 273
people to the video on YouTube, as shown in Figure 15-9. YouTube automati￾cally uses the thumbnail you created for the video as poster art. From here, 
viewers can be notified when the video is about to go live. When it does, a 
chat window appears to the right of the video so you can interact with others 
during the premiere.
And then, of course, there’s the option to make your video public, right here and 
right now. But before you make your video live, look at Figure 15-9 again. See the 
artwork? Hold on a minute! Your thumbnail is still set on the default that YouTube 
thought you’d want to use, right? Yeah. We should have a look at that.
Grabbing attention: The art 
of the thumbnail
In this section, we describe what goes into creating an eye-catching thumbnail. 
Rather than simply choosing a freeze frame from the episode, you should think 
about what your thumbnail says at a glance about your YouTube video. Although 
the value of thumbnails continues to be a subject of speculation and debate by 
YouTube legends, we’ve noticed that these same people still put a lot of thought 
into their thumbnails. So as passionately as some argue that thumbnails don’t 
matter, we believe that spending a little time with this final detail is worthwhile.
FIGURE 15-9:
Setting up a 
premiere allows 
you to hype your 
upcoming video.274 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
The real question on everyone’s content-creating mind is what makes a good 
thumbnail?
» Size: Low-resolution images do not cut it for thumbnails. A thumbnail must be 
1280 x 720 pixels. The image can’t exceed 10MB for podcasts, so try to keep 
the image compression as economical and efficient as possible.
» Formatting: Your thumbnail should be a JPG or PNG. GIFs are allowed but 
rarely resize properly. For your podcast playlist, the thumbnail should have an 
aspect ratio of 1:1; your episodes can still be the 16:9 aspect ratio used in 
YouTube players and previews.
» Faces: Apparently, the lizard part of the brain reacts positively to faces. We’ve 
seen thumbnails that go for subtlety, and we’ve seen some that resemble the 
animations of Chuck Jones. We’re still working on this aspect of creating a 
solid thumbnail.
» Stunning scenery: It helps to have an eye-catching background. Are you 
chatting about specific celebrities in your podcast? They can be in the 
background or the foreground, with your face looking from the side. Or you 
can go for atmosphere. If you’re talking about historic events, find a public 
domain image, but then apply a filter to give the photo a mysterious glow.
» Brevity is the soul of wit: Keep the text on the thumbnail brief and simple. 
Grab your audience with a catchy headline and a typeface that’s chunky 
enough to pop against your backdrop.
» Scalability: Your thumbnail should look balanced from a design point of view 
in its full size but also readable and discernible when scaled down for mobile 
devices and smaller resolutions, such as the sidebar directories featured 
throughout YouTube.
The secret sauce behind a good thumbnail is subject to debate and subject to 
change. Tee found a good amount of traffic on his Five Minute Paranormal with the 
thumbnail featured in Figure  15-10. Having KallMeKris and CelinaSpookyBoo 
flanking his own skeptical expression helped, as did following the preceding tips.
Using the preceding list as a guide, create something in your favorite graphics 
program that you think best captures the mood and the feel of your video episode. 
The examples in Figures 15-9 and 15-10 might provide an idea of the direction 
you’ll want to follow in making a thumbnail for your video.CHAPTER 15 Podcasting on YouTube 275
You’ll quickly decide if you love or hate working with video. For us, video is a rich, 
deep, and incredibly cool playground where an idea takes form in front of us. Sure, 
that happens when working with audio. And it can sometimes be a lot easier to 
explore the galaxy or dive into the depths of a dungeon with audio alone. But with 
practice, you can expand your creative reach with video and put together amazing 
projects. Start by keeping it simple, and then challenge yourself.

be said about podcasting as well as streaming, the topic of this chapter. To under￾stand how streaming affects podcasting, it helps to look at how podcasting first 
dabbled with video, including live podcasting.

Going Live
Live podcasting in the early years (pre-2010), audio or video, was unreliable and 
dependent on your computer’s processing power. Even with cutting-edge hard￾ware, results were not always as expected. Live events on the internet were 
reserved for production companies with deep pockets, and only those with high￾performance computers could watch and interact with the hosts. It was, for a 
time, a technology only for the elite.
Then, in the summer of 2011, Emmett Shear of Justin.tv led a spin-off of the plat￾form focusing on video games, allowing gamers to broadcast their gaming live on 
the internet. This spin-off was called Twitch (https://pfdbook.link/twitch), 
pictured in Figure 16-1, and remains the go-to definition of streaming, where data 
compression rates, bandwidth, and ISP upload and download rates strike a bal￾ance and offer an opportunity for anyone to stream live content. Today, Twitch is 
returning to its roots with streams featuring everyday life, special events, cooking 
classes, and yes — podcasting.
A less technical definition of streaming is a platform offering consumption or 
broadcasting of content, either time-shifted or in real time, on a global platform. 
(Sounds familiar, doesn’t it?) With the development of broadband and technology 
able to handle robust data streams, streaming services now offer audiences and 
content creators broadcast-quality entertainment online. In recent years, other 
streaming platforms have gone live and are now popular with content creators of 
all backgrounds:
» YouTube (https://pfdbook.link/youtube): Powered by Google, YouTube
was the original hub for creative video works. As discussed in Chapter 15, with 
FIGURE 16-1:
Twitch.tv offers 
podcasters a 
platform to 
connect with 
listeners and 
interact with 
them in real time.CHAPTER 16 One Giant Leap for Podcasting: Live Streaming Content 279
the capability to stream either directly from your smartphone or mobile 
device, or from a studio in your home or office, YouTube made streaming 
original content (including a recording of your podcast) possible.
» Facebook Live and Facebook Gaming (https://pfdbook.link/facebook):
In August 2015, Facebook launched Facebook Live, a streaming platform fully 
integrated with the largest social media platform in the world. Facebook Live 
notifies your network when you go live, offering a tiny inset video in the lower 
corner of your app or browser window that takes you to the stream with a 
single click. Facebook Gaming, a variation of Facebook Live, grants viewers the 
ability to interact with gamers, similar to Twitch.
» Instagram Live (https://pfdbook.link/instagram): Known for its 
“captured in the moment” photo galleries, Instagram launched their live video 
platform in August 2020. Fully integrated with the app, IG Live sends a quick 
notification that you are live and streaming to everyone in your Instagram 
(and Facebook) network.
You may want to launch a streaming channel to introduce a live element to your 
podcasts, or you may want to introduce a video component to your production. 
The simplest reason to stream content, though, is that it’s just plain fun! 
Tee began his own Twitch channel in September 2017 (https://twitch.tv/
theteemonster), and through all the breaks, changes, and modest numbers he’s 
seen since, he still relishes the moment he goes live, as shown in Figure 16-2.
All this is interesting, you’re probably thinking, but this is a book on podcasting. 
What does livestreaming have to do with podcasting? Well, after years of working 
in content creation, we’ve found a relationship between podcasting and

of ghost hunting,

streaming, especially in how you can streamline your workflow in creating an 
episode. So how do you stream content? More importantly, how do you record a 
podcast on a stream?
Good news: If you’re streaming, you already did most of the hard work.
Setting up a Streaming Account
In this chapter, we make the bold assumption that you have set up your podcast￾ing studio and are ready to record video. To get started on this adventure in 
streaming content, you need the following:
» Camera (or multiple cameras) installed
» An active account on a streaming platform
» Audio-recording software installed
» Streamlabs installed
If you’re thinking “Wait, what?” we recommend that you pick up a copy of Tee’s 
Twitch For Dummies, which goes into detail on what you need to make a livestream 
happen, such as cameras, streaming gear, and additional computer upgrades.
If you’re set up on a streaming account, good on ya for being ahead of the curve! 
If you aren’t, don’t worry because in this section you set up an account on Twitch. 
The steps here are Twitch-centric but are easily applied to other platforms such 
as YouTube or Facebook:
1. Go to http://twitch.tv and select Sign Up in the top-right corner.
You can watch Twitch streams without being signed up with the platform. 
However, if you want to take advantage of the chat features, you need an 
account.
2. Come up with a username.
Your username, which appears in chat, can be a nickname, a play on words, 
your own name, or almost anything. Just make sure that you don’t violate the 
terms of service when you create it. (For more on Twitch’s TOS, visit https://
www.twitch.tv/p/legal/terms-of-service/.)CHAPTER 16 One Giant Leap for Podcasting: Live Streaming Content 281
3. Create a password or a passphrase and confirm it.
While passwords should be difficult to crack but easy to remember, pass￾phrases are now recommended by cybersecurity experts. More characters are 
involved in passphrases. For example, you can take something easy like “I am 
a podcaster” and rework it as “!am@p0dcasteR” for your password field, as 
shown in Figure 16-3. This very strong password meets the criteria of many 
platforms.
4. Enter your birthday.
The date you enter, which is based on an honor system, verifies your age 
to make sure that you’re 13 or older.
5. Enter a mobile number or an email.
The email you use here is where all notifications and any news from 
Twitch are sent.
6. After reviewing the terms of service and the privacy policy, click Sign 
Up to complete the application.
Congratulations! Your Twitch account is now active.

Twitch channel

minutes and 
is free.282 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
You have a place on Twitch, but the state of your account is a lot like the state of an 
X (formerly Twitter) account newly launched, where the profile and bio are blank. 
Take some time to complete your profile and set up a channel with all the bells and 
whistles needed to make the best first impression when people show up. (For details 
on how to do this — again! — see Twitch For Dummies.) With a basic account up and 
running, we recommend taking a tour of Twitch by visiting live streamers running 
your favorite video game, hosting an open chat session, or recording a podcast.
Next up, you install a component from RØDE that will make streaming incredibly 
easy (and yes, fun!) on a PC or a Mac.
Setting the Stage: Working with RØDE 
Streamer X and Streamlabs
In Twitch For Dummies, Tee talks in depth about Open Broadcaster Software, or OBS
(https://pfdbook.link/obs), and Streamlabs (https://pfdbook.link/stream
labs), two video-broadcasting applications that turn your computer and any 
audio-video components connected to it into a broadcast studio. In this section, 
you get a quick look at Streamlabs.
Before you start, however, you need a capture card to enable your computer to go 
beyond its built-in video card. We want to flex a bit here, so we bring in RØDE 
Streamer X, an all-in-one solution that lets you podcast and stream anywhere.
Streamer X is the hardware you need to stream, and Streamlabs is the software 
you need to go live online.
Streamer X: Livestreaming 
wherever you are
Do you recall our best friend in podcasting, the RØDECaster Pro II mixer (dis￾cussed in detail in Chapter 4)? Streamer X is RØDECaster’s little brother and your 
best friend in livestreaming. Streamer X is a video capture card, an audio inter￾face, and a control bay, all contained in a compact and durable design that is also 
portable.
Streamer X’s inputs and features include the following: 
» Two HDMI ports (one THROUGH, one IN)
» Two USB-C ports for connecting to two computers or consolesCHAPTER 16 One Giant Leap for Podcasting: Live Streaming Content 283
» Two headphone ports (one 3.5mm for gaming headsets, one ¼ inch for 
headphones) offering zero latency monitoring
» Input for XLR or ¼-inch jacks with a built-in ultra-low-noise, high-gain preamp
» Audio and video control for devices attached to Streamer X
» Fully customizable control pads for audio effects, voice effects, and MIDI 
commands
This device, shown up close in Figure 16-4, might seem like a hefty investment at 
$399, but it’s an impressive addition to your podcast production that offers you so 
many streaming options. Streamer X can make your livestream happen anywhere 
you and your computer happen to find yourselves.
Streamer X can run off your computer’s power supply from the USB-C 1 port, 
offering a lot of opportunity for podcasting and live streaming on-the-go. How￾ever, Streamer X may tap into resources your computer needs to livestream, 
affecting the overall quality of your production. We recommend that you use an 
independent power supply with your first few streams to take full advantage of 
what the device can offer. When you’re comfortable with Streamer X, start testing 
its limits when running off your computer’s power supply.
FIGURE 16-4:
Streamer X is 
your all-in-one 
solution for 
streaming on a 
Mac or a PC.284 PART 5 Podcasting the World of Tomorrow . . . Today!
With Streamer X out of its box and an idea taking form in your head for your first 
stream, let’s get your studio set up. For this exercise, you set up Streamer X for 
streaming video games — the default setting for livestreaming. However, during 
this setup, we also provide non-gaming content options:
1. Plug Streamer X into a power supply.
2. Plug one end of a USB-C high-speed data transfer cable into Streamer X’s 
USB-C 1 port and the other end into your computer.
Streamer X’s included USB-C cable is made for high-speed data transfer. If you 
are looking to upgrade or replace your USB-C cables for this device, make sure 
you purchase USB-C cables that support high-speed data transfer.
3. Plug an XLR microphone into the mic input port.
4. Plug a pair of headphones into the headphones jack.
5. Plug a gaming console into the HDMI IN port.
While we feature a gaming console here, this input can be used to connect any 
device you’re capturing, such as a video camera or another computer.
6. Plug an external monitor into the HDMI THRU port.
The external monitor, which supports up to 4K60 high dynamic range (HDR) 
video, enables you to view the feed coming into the HDMI IN port during 
capture.
For full 4K support, use HDMI 2.0 (or above) cables for the connections 
between HDMI devices.
Now, with Streamer X connected, you can create a scene in Streamlabs resembling 
Figure 16-5. So let’s keep pushing forward and set up Streamlabs.
Streamlabs: Your livestreaming 
control board
Now that you have the hardware in place, it’s time to set up the software. Stream￾labs allows you to set up different scenes, such as Livestream Starting Soon or Be 
Right Back, alongside a main scene where you feature the content you’re actively 
producing. The application also provides overlay templates in different themes, 
moods, applications, and colors.
With just a few clicks, your stream can be transformed from basic to breathtaking. 
And, if you have the processing power, your overlays and widgets can be ani￾mated, adding an even more dynamic look to your channel.CHAPTER 16 One Giant Leap for Podcasting: Live Streaming Content 285
The best attribute of Streamlabs is how insanely easy it is to get cracking with it:
1. Download and install Streamlabs at https://pfdbook.link/streamlabs.
2. Double-click the Streamlabs icon to launch Streamlabs.
3. Log in with Twitch, YouTube, or the streaming service of your choice.
The Streamlabs application, with your credentials, syncs with the various 
streaming platforms.
4. In the top-left section of Streamlabs’ screen, click the themes icon (shown 
in the margin).
The screen shown in Figure 16-6 appears.
5. Find and select a theme for your stream.
You can search through the various templates or use the Scene Themes 
category, located in the top left. Click a template’s preview image to see a 
full-screen preview of it. While some themes are part of a paid subscription 
to Streamlabs, many themes are free.
You can use the Game Category filter to narrow the search parameters. Filters 
make the decision process a little easier.
For non-gamers reading this, don’t let the Game Category menu throw you off. 
You can still find incredible themes for your livestream in the various catego￾ries featured in Streamlabs.
6. When you find a theme you like, click the Install Overlay button at the 
top-right side of the template’s preview.
You now have a template in place. Yeah, it’s that easy.

In planning for your stream, you should have an idea of how to customize this

And You’re Live! Podcasting in the Moment
After your stream is set up in Streamlabs, you’re ready to go live. If you’re 
working with friends, either remotely or in your studio, make sure everyone is 
ready 15 to 30 minutes before your scheduled go time so you can run the final 
audio and video checks. Your pre-show prep should also cover any notes you or 
others in your show want to talk about.
With everything agreed upon, questions answered, and equipment checked, it’s 
time to hit your Go Live button:
1. Launch your audio recording software and start recording.
Part of your pre-show prep should also be making sure your DAW (digital 
audio workstation) is recording and ready to go.
2. Go to Streamlabs and confirm that you’re in your Starting Soon scene.
3. Click the Go Live button in the lower-right corner of the app.
Instead of starting a stream at the scheduled time, streamers tend to launch a 
stream a few minutes before to give people time to receive notification and 
arrive at your channel. Tee usually starts his Stream Starting Soon reel (a 
collection of clips from previous streams) 15 minutes before appearing live in 
front of the camera.
4. Begin your podcast, making sure to check your chat window during the 
show for comments or questions.
5. When you sign off on your podcast, wish your chat audience farewell and 
thank them for hanging out on your live stream.
6. End your stream and stop your recording software.
Welcome to the streaming workflow that includes podcasting. You have an audio 
recording of your podcast, but you also have a video stream that can be down￾loaded and edited either as a special video episode or stripped of its audio and 
edited as a backup recording.CHAPTER 16 One Giant Leap for Podcasting: Live Streaming Content 289
It’s a Stream! No, It’s a Podcast. No, 
It’s a Stream and a Podcast!
So what does streaming the recording of your podcast bring to your production? 
Why is going live with video essential to an audio podcast?
» Live interaction with your audience: A live audience adds a level of 
excitement and energy to your production. Something you or one of your 
co-hosts say can spark audience commentary. A special guest with a shared 
interest (gaming developer, fantasy author, voice actor) may inspire questions.
» A platform for a new audience to find you: New visitors to your chat may 
want to catch up with previous shows, find out how your show began, and are 
SERIOUSLY? A SELFIE STICK?
The much-maligned selfie stick has been a source of ridicule or, for those really lost in 
their own narcissism, embarrassing moments of clumsiness. But when it comes to film￾ing on your smartphone, a selfie stick saves your arm from cramping and offers your 
video a bit of stability. With the right angle and leverage, your selfie stick can become a 
collapsible gimbal and provide better options for capturing people and even activity. For 
additional polish to your self-shot video, you can also employ any model of the DJI 
Osmo Mobile Gimbal, a gimbal solution for your smartphone.
Just remember to be well aware of your surroundings. Getting the best shot for your 
podcast will not matter one bit if you are recovering in a hospital (or worse). And yes, 
Tee (on the right) was being very careful with the vintage steam train slowly advancing 
behind him.

curious as to what your podcast is all about. Bringing an audience into a show 
is a great way to attract new listeners.
» Videos featuring bonus content: Once your stream is concluded, streaming 
platforms offer options for exporting streams to other locations like YouTube 
or Vimeo. You can offer these uploads as embedded videos in your show 
notes. You can also edit and isolate footage not found in the final podcast, 
offering these clips as incentives for your audience to attend a live stream or 
to support your podcast on a membership-supported platform like Patreon.
» Automatic backup recording: We know all too well that redundancies are a 
beautiful thing. Wonderful once-in-a-lifetime interviews and engaging 
discussions with co-hosts are lost when a recording fails. By incorporating 
streaming into your podcast’s workflow, you automatically create a backup 
recording ready for production.
You can use the audio from your stream as your podcast file as well. To retrieve 
audio from your stream, do the following:
1. Go to your streaming platform and access your directory of previous 
broadcasts.
On the Twitch platform, you go to the Video Producer section. Note that Twitch 
erases broadcasts from its servers after two weeks.
If you make your account a Twitch Turbo account ($11.99 a month), previous 
broadcasts are removed after 60 days.
2. Find your podcast stream and select the option to download the episode 
to your computer.
In Twitch, go to the Video Producer section and select your podcast stream 
to download, as shown in Figure 16-8.

like your early steps with podcasting, upgrades to enable streaming don’t have to
