Chapter 17
Ten Things We Wish We 
Had Known When We 
Started Podcasting
Oh, the things we’ve seen (and done) in the last 20 years! There’s an old 
expression that says, “experience is the comb that life gives you after 
you’ve lost all your hair.” The contents of this chapter are a collection of 
lessons we’ve learned by things we’ve done or not done as well as what we’ve 
observed from other content creators. We’re firm believers of the philosophy that 
when you get a new role or responsibility, seek out a mentor. Let this chapter be 
your Yoda, Luke. For a Jedi podcaster, soon you will be, hmmm?
Use Your Mic Effectively
We love the saying “Content is king and quality is queen. If the queen isn’t happy 
then nobody is happy.” That is to say, if nobody can hear you clearly, they won’t 
be coming back. Using one or more microphones properly is a key component to 
quality content. Yes, we’ve already covered loads of information about micro￾phones in Chapters 2 and 7 (including an embarrassing tale by your authors — we 
wish someone would have told us how to use the mics properly sooner).
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Suggestions for getting started
» Lessons learned when doing 
interviews
» Tips on lighting and video296 PART 6 The Part of Tens
Beyond the technical details, however, we have a few tips for looking and sound￾ing your best without going into debt: 
» Know how to use your mic. Earlier in the book, we share the classic tale of 
how Tee was speaking into the end of a side-addressable mic. Knowing how 
to use your microphone may not seem as simple as you first thought. For 
example, did you know that nearly all side-addressable mics also have a front 
and back? One morning, Chuck was speaking with a co-worker via video 
conference and recognized that the person’s audio was low despite the fact 
that he was close to the mic and addressing it properly from the side — but it 
was the wrong side. Chuck suggested rotating it 180 degrees to address the 
front of the mic, not the back, which solved the problem. His co-worker, who 
shall rename anonymous to protect his reputation, was appreciative.
» Have one mic per guest. If you’re doing a podcast with multiple guests, be 
sure everyone has their own microphone. Passing a single mic around can 
cause noise. Placing a single mic in the center of a table means not everyone, 
if anyone, is close enough to be heard — and are probably not addressing it 
from the correct side or angle. We know that having extra mics is a particular 
challenge when going on the road, so do your best to plan accordingly with 
equipment and environment.
» Test your audio. Go beyond the classic “check one, check two” test to ensure 
that the audio is being picked up. Recently, Chuck was watching a high-quality 
4K video, but the audio quality was poor. The person in front of the camera 
was wearing a lavalier mic clipped to their shirt, but it was clear that the audio 
wasn’t from that mic but from the mic on a phone several feet away. As a 
result, the audio was very low, sounded distant, and was full of room echo. 
A simple test recording and playback would have revealed the failure and 
resulted in an easy remediation for this situation. Try saying a few words and 
playing it back to make sure everything is in order. In many cases, you won’t 
get a second chance to re-record.
Make Sure Others Can See Your Screen
At some point, you may have the opportunity, desire, or requirement to share 
content on your screen as part of a video. Make sure everyone can clearly see what 
you’re displaying. This tip is for podcasters and anyone who shares their screen 
during a video conference.
Sure, you have an ultra-wide monitor with super-high resolution and the infor￾mation on your screen looks good to you. But when you share it, consider whether CHAPTER 17 Ten Things We Wish We Had Known When We Started Podcasting 297
someone watching on a tablet or phone can make out what’s on your screen. Here 
are a few tips to help with that:
» Use a large font and few words. We won’t get into tips for putting together a 
presentation slide deck because that could be another book in itself. However, 
if you stick with a few basic tips, you’ll ensure that your presentation 
can be seen:
• Use a font size that can be seen. Make it big — 24pt or larger is always welcome.
• Reduce the number of words on the slide. If you increase the font size, you’ll 
reduce the number of words you can get on the screen, but that’s fine. 
Remember, your slides are visual aids. They should consist of short 
thoughts like “Drive costs down” or “Humans return to the moon.” If you 
have more than seven words for any bullet item, it’s time to think of a 
shorter way to say that thought.
• Consider using more images and fewer words. A picture is worth a thousand 
words. Why not let the visual aid support your idea rather than simply be a 
transcript of what you’re going to say.
» Zoom in. When the viewer can’t read the very tiny text, icons, and graphs on 
a shared screen, often the problem is that the podcaster is using a large 4K 
(or higher) monitor. They share their entire screen but the content on the 
screen is too tiny for most viewers to read. When sharing your screen on a 
1080p HD display, open your browser and start by zooming in to 125 percent 
(see Figure 17-1). The higher the resolution of your display, the more you’ll 
have to zoom in. You might need to zoom in to 200 percent or more on some 
high-res displays.
FIGURE 17-1:
Ensure that 
everyone can see 
your screen 
clearly.298 PART 6 The Part of Tens
» Share just an app window or tab. Instead of sharing your entire display, 
check your software (such as Zoom or StreamYard) to see if you can share just 
the app or tab. For example, Microsoft PowerPoint has a window mode (check 
out the Slide Show menu under Set Up Slide Show). With this option, you can 
run PowerPoint in a window, share that with your viewers, and still see other 
things on your screen such as notes, chat, or people raising their hands. The 
key here is to plan what you’ll be sharing. If you’re sharing only a single 
browser tab and your app opens another tab, you’ll see the new browser tab 
just fine but your audience will be stuck viewing the original browser tab — 
and you’ll have to back up, stop sharing, and re-share the new tab (or revert to 
the entire screen; see the preceding tip). Awkward!
» Confirm that everyone can see the screen clearly. Just because you can 
see things clearly doesn’t mean everyone else can. Just like testing your audio, 
test your sharing too. If possible, test before you go on the air. A co-host or 
producer is always useful for providing feedback. If you don’t have one of 
those, try joining your broadcast with a second device and see how it looks. 
Or go with the old tried-and-true method: Ask your audience if they can see 
clearly and adjust accordingly.
Have a trackpad? Use the pinch-zoom gesture with your browser window to zoom 
in to what you want to show your audience. This directs the viewer’s attention 
where you want it and away from any potential distractions.
Do your best to remove distractions. Some of your viewers love looking at things 
like what plug-ins you have installed, how many unread emails you have, or what 
apps are currently running rather than looking at your presentation. Keep their 
attention focused where you want it.
Share Your Podcast the Right Way
While reading this book, you may have become slightly overwhelmed by the num￾ber of file formats. Fear not. When it comes time to export your masterpiece for 
distribution, we’re here to help keep it as simple as possible. For audio, try to 
import and edit raw files (WAV) and then export as MP3. Nearly every audio player 
works well with MP3. While the landscape is ever-changing and some players may 
have others features, MP3 is your best common denominator. For video, import 
and edit files that are as high resolution as possible (raw) and then export to MP4. 
Much like MP3 for audio, MP4 for video is a commonly recognized format.CHAPTER 17 Ten Things We Wish We Had Known When We Started Podcasting 299
Some hosting providers will convert your file format when you upload it. For 
example, you can upload MOV video files to YouTube and it will play them just 
fine. If you download the same video from YouTube, you will get an MP4 file, but 
the settings may not be the quality you expect. Check with your hosting provider 
to see if they have any special recommendations or provisions.
Some options can significantly affect the quality of your audio and video. For 
example, compression (see Chapter 1) greatly affects the quality and size of your 
podcast file. The higher the compression, the smaller the file and the lower the 
quality — but also the lower the cost if you’re paying for bandwidth from your 
hosting provider (see Chapter 10). For MP3 audio files, a good place to start is a 
44,100Hz sample rate, a 128bps bit rate, and joint stereo. If you drop the sample 
rate or bit rate any lower, your audio quality could suffer. For MP4 video files, our 
recommended starting settings are H.264 encoding, 30 frames per second, and a 
1000 kbps data rate.
And as always, we suggest you test, test, test. Review your video on different 
devices to see how it looks and sounds, and then adjust as necessary. As with the 
audio, lowering the frame rate or data rate lower may lower your file size but may 
also make the video jittery or blurry.
Have a Plan
Way back in the early days of podcasting, the two of us would come up with an 
idea, slap together a website, and start recording. This approach often resulted in 
several challenges, including coming up with new topics for each episode, lining 
up guests, delivery consistency, and even getting the co-host to show up. The lack 
of a plan meant struggles, and when production was a struggle, the show often 
didn’t last long.
By contrast, when Chuck’s team at his day job suggested he start a podcast in 
which he interviewed people in his software developer ecosystem, management 
wanted to know that the time and money invested would provide a return to the 
company and the audience. The project was given a green light only after Chuck 
presented a plan outlining the title, tag line, objective, proposed episode length, 
social media plan, costs, and  — the tricky part  — episode topics for the first 
six months.
The new show’s format was quite different from his past shows, which relied on 
recent news stories for their content. The effort involved in scheduling, recording, 
editing, and delivery was greatly reduced because of the work done up front. 300 PART 6 The Part of Tens
Several years have passed, and the show is still humming along with a pipeline of 
upcoming content. Depending on your plan and your show’s format, you may be 
able to record episodes in advance (or out of order), which gives you the opportu￾nity to take a vacation now and then while maintaining a consistent delivery 
schedule.
The bottom line is to have a plan. Think about the show’s format, delivery sched￾ule, and time commitment.
Create Five to Ten Episodes 
before Going Live
You have a great idea for a show and are eager for the world to hear your message. 
It’s all so exciting! We know, we’ve been there (more than a few times). However, 
we recommend that you pull back on the reins a little and create five to ten epi￾sodes before publishing them publicly. Think of these as rehearsals to not only see 
how easy it is to create new episodes but also to get an idea of the workflow and 
what the result should be.
These rehearsal episodes enable you to solicit feedback from others and are an 
opportunity to learn about your show’s format and perhaps discover a few handy 
options in your software and hardware that can save you time and frustration. You 
may even decide to throw out one or more of those early episodes. Many experi￾enced podcasters have said, “Wow, were we awkward back then!” The episodes 
that pass the test can become your buffer.
We know you want an audience and are excited to get started. However, you never 
get a second chance to make a first impression. Why not offer your audience the 
best experience by practicing a few times before going out on that big podcasting 
stage?
Don’t Rubber Stamp Your Next Podcast
After you have your first podcast show up and running, you may be considering 
another. Hey, why not? It’s fun and perhaps profitable and you’ve already gone up 
the learning curve to launch your first show. A common trap is thinking “I’ve 
done this before. I have all this experience. I’ll make my new show much like my 
last one.”CHAPTER 17 Ten Things We Wish We Had Known When We Started Podcasting 301
Not so fast!
Each show has its own signature, vibe, personality, or whatever you want to call 
it. Rubber stamping your new show stifles your creativity and potential. Podcast￾ing has a lot in common with acting. After all, both are performances. For exam￾ple, the character that actor Tom Hanks played in Forrest Gump was the life of the 
entire movie. In comparison, the same actor as Colonel Tom Parker in Elvis had a 
different vibe. Different makeup, different clothing, different personality. That’s 
how you need to think about your performance as well as other show elements, 
such as artwork, music, website, and your voice.
Going from a solo show to having a co-host is a major change. Going from a news 
podcast about molecular research to audio drama is a really big change! While the 
experience you’ve gained with hardware, software, social media, and perhaps 
even bringing your own knowledge of molecular research to a thrilling fictional 
story is all useful, the essence of the show should be different.
Control the Environment for 
Audio and Video
In Chapter 7 we discuss ambient noise and how you may want to include or exclude 
it depending on your show’s format. This section builds on that by giving you 
some suggestions on how to best control the environment of your recording space 
and avoid pitfalls that may not be evident until after the recording:
» Silence your studio as much as possible. The more you can reduce 
background noise while you record, the happier you’ll be. Computer fans, 
air conditioning units, and other noises you normally ignore can add up to 
humming or buzzing on your podcast. Control what you can and work around 
the rest. For example, ask your landscaper to send a text before arriving with 
the loud trimmer and leaf blower, and adjust your recording schedule 
accordingly.
» Keep out the light. Unwanted light in a video podcast can be worse than 
errant sound in audio. Perhaps the lighting in your studio is fine — until the 
winter, when the sun comes directly in the windows and hits the camera lens. 
A simple solution is to use light-blocking (honeycomb) blinds.
» With video, every detail matters. Obviously, the clothing you wear on an 
audio podcast is of little consequence, but add a camera and venture into the 
wonderful world of video and your wardrobe is the first element you need to 302 PART 6 The Part of Tens
consider. Here are a few general rules as you talk to the camera. Different 
rules may apply to different situations:
• Stay away from all-black, all-white, plaid, and striped clothes. They can cause 
contrast issues and moiré effects in your video.
• Avoid a distracting background as well as a completely neutral background. In 
some of Tee’s early video offerings, he was filming against a blank, neutral 
background. A void, if you will. And it was dull. A coat of paint, a variety of 
flat decorations, and a Litra Beam LX from Logitech G later, Tee’s video 
productions leveled up and offered a bit of balance and personality to his 
productions, as shown before and after in Figure 17-2. Even something as 
simple as a curtain makes for a good background.
Here’s a neat lighting trick: Shine colored lights onto a simple black curtain 
behind you. By having the lights close to the curtain, you can create a 
simple, illuminated background for your video.
• Lighting is essential. Try to reduce shadows on your face. You may have to 
get creative with your lighting. Consider using a ring light or multiple lights 
from different angles.
Be aware of reflections if you or your guest wears glasses. You may have to 
adjust the angle of the camera or lighting to reduce the reflection from the 
lenses.
» Don’t sit between the camera and a bright light source. Sitting in front of a 
bright light source such as a window with daylight pouring in puts the camera 
in conflict. You’ll look like a silhouette. If you try to change the camera settings 
so you can see your face, the background light will be very intense. If possible, 
move the camera and your workspace so that the daylight from the window is 
behind the camera and lighting your lovely face.
FIGURE 17-2:
Simple applica￾tions of lighting, 
color, and 
decorations, as 
shown here with 
Tee in the 
evolution of his 
studio, can 
change the mood 
of a podcast.CHAPTER 17 Ten Things We Wish We Had Known When We Started Podcasting 303
Your Guests’ Audio Should Sound 
as Good as Yours
Here’s a situation we’ve run into over the years: You’re using Zoom, StreamYard, 
or some other software to record an interview, but your guest doesn’t sound good. 
Here are some tips to help you close the gap on differences between their audio 
and yours:
» Do a tech check. Before the interview starts, guide them through a few tasks 
to try to improve their audio. It’s helpful to study the options of your recording 
rig. Here are some tips for your tech check that take only a few minutes and 
can result in a better listener experience (if only just a little):
• Have your guest move as close as possible to the microphone.
• Check their input device. We’ve experienced plenty of people who were not 
using their USB microphones properly. Either the software was configured 
to the incorrect audio input or they were not addressing the microphone 
properly (see Chapter 7). If your guest doesn’t have a fancy USB micro￾phone, look for alternatives to the built-in microphone. For example, if they 
have earbuds paired with their computer, have them try those and see if 
the earbuds are an improvement to the audio.
• Check their audio levels in the software. A common problem with remote 
guests is that the audio is too low (or too high.) Spend a few minutes with 
them going over the software settings to adjust their input level. Some 
apps or websites have an automatic gain control (AGC) setting, which tries 
to automatically adjust the input level instead. Sometimes this works well 
but other times it can be nothing but trouble.
» Take care of audio levels in post-production. If you’ve done what you could in 
the tech check but your guest’s audio is still quieter than yours, the only way to 
fix it is in post-production. A tool such as Auphonic (https://pfdbook.link/
auphonic) might help by leveling, or bringing the high spots down and the low 
spots up in your audio. Chuck has been using Auphonic to do a final clean up on 
his Technorama podcast for years. Auphonic processes up to two hours per 
month for free; if you need more capacity, you have to get a paid subscription.
» Realize that your guest doesn’t have a great setup. You’re a podcaster now 
and have cool gear. Your guest is probably talking into a built-in mic on a 
laptop at arm’s length. Don’t stress trying to have everyone sound as though 
they’re in the same room speaking into high-end equipment. When you’re 
listening to a podcast and the host sounds great but the guest sounds as if 
they’re calling in from a phone halfway around the globe, you recognize and 
accept it. Your listeners will too.304 PART 6 The Part of Tens
Check your software and hardware to see if you can record each guest on a sepa￾rate audio track (or channel.) This allows you to adjust for different audio levels 
in post-production. Zoom and StreamYard offer this feature with a paid 
subscription.
Don’t Wing It with Interviews
Find out as much professional and personal information you can about your guest 
before heading into the interview. You may discover potential tangents to explore 
or something you have in common to help drive the conversation and create a 
friendly atmosphere. Remember, you’re there on behalf of your listeners. In some 
cases, your listeners will know more about your guest than you do. (In Chapter 6, 
we cover interview skills and techniques in detail.)
Chuck tried doing an unprepared interview one time, and let’s just say it was an 
interesting experiment. He was at the Experimental Aircraft Association (EAA) 
fly-in in Oshkosh Wisconsin in 2006. While Chuck finds airplanes fascinating and 
is a passenger quite a bit as part of his day job, he’s not a pilot. So when Chuck’s 
friend Steve spotted the Honda Jet with its interesting wing design, Steve sug￾gested that Chuck go do a quick interview. Why not? Holding a microphone builds 
courage in Chuck for some reason! He has a standard series of questions and this 
wasn’t the first interview at the convention, so he was feeling pretty good. How￾ever, the interview was short and lacked depth because he didn’t have expertise in 
this field, and the lack of planning was obvious very quickly after the standard 
questions were used up. Fortunately for him, the interview was targeted more at 
general geeks rather than aviation enthusiasts.
Tee had a similar situation, when someone recommended an author whose books 
had been banned from certain libraries. The desire to feed the content machine 
kept Tee from his usual research. Within the first five minutes, Tee discovered 
that the banned book was a misunderstanding and the author was not only fea￾tured but was doing a signing that weekend. The interview descended into a sur￾real series of name drops, hard-to-confirm accomplishments and accolades, and 
awkward pauses. At the end of the day, Tee remained without a presentable 
episode.
Better preparation in both these circumstances would have made for better 
interviews.CHAPTER 17 Ten Things We Wish We Had Known When We Started Podcasting 305
Look at the Camera, Not Your Screen
Here’s another one for the podcasters with video. Maintain eye contact with your 
camera as much as you can. Looking at the camera is a hard skill to learn and not 
natural. As humans, we would rather look at another human face than a dark hole 
into the abyss. Nevertheless, looking directly at the camera makes a big difference 
to the viewer. It means you’re talking to them, not distracted by something off 
screen. It makes them feel like they’re part of the conversation rather than a 
bystander. Even if your camera is sitting on top of the monitor, focus your eye 
contact on the camera!
We’ve seen podcasts and webinars where the host is looking a few degrees left, 
right, or down, and it feels disingenuous. “What are they looking at? Why aren’t 
they talking to me? Don’t they like me? Is there something more interesting over 
there?” No, they’re just looking at the interview guest, their notes, or the produc￾tion software on their screen.
However, don’t give a creepy death stare to the camera during the entire recording 
or livestream. You may need to make the occasional eye contact with your in￾studio guest, switch between screen layouts, or share a screen image as part of 
your production. After all, you have a lot to do as a podcaster. The occasional 
interruption is understandable. Just make sure to return your gaze to the camera 
as much as possible. If you want a good example of this, study the people deliver￾ing the news.
Chuck puts googly eyes on the webcam sitting atop his monitor to help him focus 
there. Another idea is to use a sticky note with the words Look here!CHAPTER 18 Ten Types of Podcasts to Check Out 307

We find inspiration in listening to and watching other podcasts and think

No pressure.
Where to begin? Where to begin? With each edition of Podcasting For Dummies, your 
humble authors sit down and put together our own top ten lists of podcasts, and 
here’s what we’ve noticed: There are a lot of podcasts out there. While some of our 
listening habits have remained steady and constant as the Northern Star, our taste 
can sometimes shift into unexpected offerings; if you listen to Astronomy Cast

constant. We listen to a solid cross-section of types of podcasts, not just in the way 
of genres but in production values. So with a quick edit of the outline and a change 
of mindset, this chapter offers you Ten Types of Podcasts to check out.
With these ten kinds of podcasts as a starting point, you can easily begin to fill 
your media player with a wide cross-section of interests, passions, and projects. 
Later, you might want to check out podcasts that your favorite podcasters

Tech Podcasts
If you’re thinking about podcasting, you likely have a comfortable knowledge of 
computers, the internet, and blogging. But regardless of how technologically 
savvy you are, like any aspect of life, you should never stop learning. That’s why 
you should subscribe to at least one tech podcast.
The agenda for a tech podcast is (surprise!) technology. All geek, all the time. Geeks, 
nerds, wizards, and tech help gurus sit behind a microphone and pull back the 
curtain on how your computer works, how a podcast’s RSS feed can be better, and 
how to make your time behind the keyboard more efficient.
Tech podcasts come delivered to you in a variety of skill levels and on a variety 
of topics:
» Macs: Some excellent podcasts for Mac users include the Macworld Podcast
(shown in Figure 18-1 and available at https://pfdbook.link/macworld) 
and the iMore Show (https://pfdbook.link/imore), covering the latest 
developments coming from Cupertino, recent product reviews, and the odd 
opinion or two on the direction of Apple.
» PC and general computing news: Many tech podcasts, such as This Week in 
Tech (or TWiT, as it is lovingly referred to by its hosts and listeners, available at 
https://pfdbook.link/twit) or Daily Tech News Show (https://pfdbook.
link/dtns), are generic in their approach to operating systems, following a 
mindset that a computer is a computer and the rest is mostly bells and 
whistles.
» Technology perspectives: Computer Talk Radio (https://pfdbook.link/
ctr) and Technorama (https://pfdbook.link/technorama) give personal 
and (in many cases) light-hearted perspectives on technology in society and 
go beyond the geek-speak, addressing issues on how technology can affect 
just about everything.CHAPTER 18 Ten Types of Podcasts to Check Out 309
Whichever podcast you feed into your podcatching client, find a tech podcast 
that’s right for you and allow yourself to grow into your geekdom. There’s a lot to 
learn about computers, smartphones, the Internet of Things, and other cool James 
Bond gadgets available at your local electronics store. When you have the basics 
down, these podcasts allow you to unlock the potential of these devices and go 
beyond your expectations.
Independent Media Podcasts
Media mavericks are alive and well and doing just fine in the 21st century, finding 
a new promotion channel with podcasting.
Independent labels, where the artists also work as promoters, producers, and 
holders-of-all-rights, have control over where their music plays, how often it is 
played, and how much it will cost you. Many indie musicians who are having 
trouble getting exposure and radio airplay will grant permission for podcasters to 
use their music. What does this cost the podcaster? A few moments of time and a 
spot or two, such as, “This music is brought to you by . . .” and “Visit this band 
online at w-w-w-dot .  .  .” In return, the musician is exposed to a worldwide 
audience.
Musicians such as The Gentle Readers, Michelle Malone (both featured on Evil 
Genius Chronicles at https://pfdbook.link/egc), George Hrab, and Rubber Band

Banjo (both featured on Tee’s Parsec-winning podcast Billibub Baddings and The

Science Podcasts
The approach to science made popular by Bill Nye the Science Guy, Mr. Wizard, 
and the Mythbusters is rampant in the podosphere. Here are some science pod￾casts that we recommend:
» Astronomy Cast (https://pfdbook.link/astronomycast): If you’ve always

» Hidden Brain (https://pfdbook.link/hiddenbrain): This podcast explores 
questions about why we do what we do and its effect. Shankar Vendantam 
and his pantheon of knowledgeable experts use storytelling to help us 
understand ourselves better. He covers everything from snap judgements to 
how to prevent being manipulated. This is truly a wonderful show to improve 
your wellbeing.
» The Brain Science Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/brainscience): When 
it comes to the human brain and its mechanics, Dr. Ginger Campbell, MD,

features the latest books about neuroscience as well as interviews with 
leading scientists from around the world. You might think that neuroscience 
would appeal to a niche audience; but with over 7.5 million downloads, 
Dr. Campbell’s podcast continues to educate the world about how our 
brains work.
» Therapy for Black Girls (https://pfdbook.link/tfbg): Hosted by Dr. Joy 
Harden Bradford, Therapy for Black Girls focuses on making mental health 
topics more relevant and accessible for Black women. Dr. Bradford taps into 
pop culture to illustrate psychological concepts to overcome the stigma 
surrounding mental health issues. This podcast aims to make mental health 
topics accessible and relevant.

Self-Development Podcasts
Why not broaden your horizons — personal or professional — with podcasting? 
For example, say you’ve always wanted to learn Spanish. You could shell out some 
bucks for the “Teach Yourself Spanish” series, or you could listen to the free 
SpanishPod101 podcast (https://pfdbook.link/spanish101). SpanishPod101 is a 
podcast that is part of a multiple-device approach across PDFs, online forums, 
and smart devices that culminate into a survival guide for a new language. With 
its podcast as the first step, SpanishPod101 helps you navigate situations at the 
restaurant, at the bank, or if your car breaks down.
Here are just a few examples of subjects offered in the podosphere:
» Education: Podcasts can be like your favorite class after the bell has rung, 
offering material devoted to what you might not have heard while in middle 
and high school. Stuff You Missed in History Class (https://pfdbook.link/
symihc), hosted by Tracy V. Wilson and Holly Frey, offers details behind Lady 
Jane Grey, Executive Order 9066, and comedians Abbott & Costello, each 
episode an in-depth look at events from our past. Or if you’re working on your 
language skills using the Duolingo (https://pfdbook.link/duolingo) app 
(it’s a blast learning a language this way!), you can increase your immersion 
with the Duolingo Podcast, specific to what language you’re learning.
» Career development: Cathy Heller, author, business, and life coach speaks 
with successful authors, celebrities, entrepreneurs, spiritual leaders, and 
everyday heroes about how they transformed their lives and ways you can do 
the same on The Cathy Heller Show (https://pfdbook.link/cathyheller). 
And when it comes to incredible visionaries — Bill Gates, Norman Lear, and 
Michelle Obama, to name a few — the TED Talks podcasts (https://pfdbook.
link/tedtalks) offer all kinds of great career advice from those who shape 
the world.
» Personal development: Are you looking to change your eating habits or 
maybe just find calm in the stress of daily life? Podcasting can bring a healthy 
alternative to your current digital lifestyle and maybe even lower blood CHAPTER 18 Ten Types of Podcasts to Check Out 313
pressure and cholesterol points in the process. For a quick daily bit of 
positivity, be sure to check out Let It Be Easy (https://pfdbook.link/
susiemoore) with Susie Moore. In about six minutes, she gives her spin on 
everything from Imposter Syndrome to your passion. For those wanting their 
health and philosophy peppered with a bit of Bruce Lee, dive deeper into a 
martial art you study (or are thinking about studying) with whistlekick Martial 
Arts Radio (https://pfdbook.link/wkmar).
» Money management: If you’re looking for advice on managing your money, 
check out Money Girl (https://pfdbook.link/moneygirl). Or if you’re trying 
to manage finances after you have made the ultimate life investment (Hint: It 
involves the words “I do” or a variant of that), try His & Her Money Podcast
(https://pfdbook.link/hisandhermoney), which focuses on joint financial 
plans and their balance in a strong, marital relationship.
Whether you’re tuning in to one of numerous podcasts sponsored by universities 
and colleges or to an enthusiast who wants to share and swap resources with you, 
all this continuing education is available online, in audio, and at no charge.

This section wouldn’t be complete without mentioning Manager Tools (https://

However, the podcasts themselves are not the end game. The Manager Tools teams use

written show notes for each episode, a roadmap on how to roll out their tools, and their 
online interview tool (which alone is worth the price).

Comedy Podcasts
Sometimes you just need a good laugh. A bonus with comedy podcasts is having 
those good laughs categorized, digitized, and waiting for you, only a tap or click 
away.
Humor, though, is in the eye of the beholder and the ear of the listener. Perform￾ing a search on comedy podcasts will offer you many, many choices, ranging from 
kid-safe comedy to performing arts to adults-only discussions with an irreverent 
approach. Comedy covers a lot of ground, but straight-up comedy podcasts are 
not intended to do anything other than entertain.
The way these podcasts make you laugh covers a wide spectrum of how humor is 
defined. Here are a few comedy podcasts that we enjoy:
» Comedy4Cast (https://pfdbook.link/c4c): The unpredictability from 
Comedy4Cast’s host, Clinton Alvord, is one of the elements that adds charm to 
the show. Sometimes you get a tongue-in-cheek review of an electronic 
gadget; another time you hear a parody of another popular podcast, or 
Clinton could be offering a comedy serial. Whatever is on the menu provided 
(continued)CHAPTER 18 Ten Types of Podcasts to Check Out 315
by cast and crew, Comedy4Cast has but one goal: having you pull over to the 
side of the road so you don’t drive into the ditch, or setting the hot coffee 
aside so as not to spit it on your keyboard. Disclaimer: Comedy4Cast is not 
responsible for damages incurred while listening.
» The FuMP (https://pfdbook.link/fump): The FuMP stands for “The Funny 
Music Project” and features a cavalcade of comedy musicians that include 
Devo Spice, the great Luke Ski, Worm Quartet, Power Salad, Nuclear Bubble 
Wrap, Carrie Dahlby, Carla Ulbrich, Robert Lund and Spaff, Raymond and 
Scum, and more! All of the music showcased in The FuMP is protected under a 
Creative Commons license so listeners know they can share the music or even 
make their own music video to it, as long as no one makes money at it. The 
FuMP also operates as a network, offering website visitors the links to artists 
for additional laughs beyond the podcast.
» Alison Rosen Is Your New Best Friend (https://pfdbook.link/ariynbf):
This podcast began as an online talk show, streamed from journalist Alison 
Rosen’s Brooklyn apartment. This window into a comic writer’s life evolved 
into interviews that center on people’s genuine struggles, both in her back￾yard and around the world. Alison Rosen Is Your New Best Friend has been 
described as a marriage between Seinfeld and Charlie Rose, but Rosen 
considers her podcast a chronicle of daily life and how we tend to laugh 
at what we as humans deal with.
» The Geologic Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/geologic): No, wait, The 
Geologic Podcast is a podcast featuring the music of musician and author 
George Hrab! Hold on — The Geologic Podcast is a scientific and skeptic 
podcast featuring the musings of musician and author George Hrab! 
Actually . . . yes, The Geologic Podcast is all this and a comedy podcast guaran￾teed to get you thinking, all while rocking your socks off. A lot happens in this 
podcast, so best hold on to something when you dive in.
Slice-of-Life Podcasts
Comedy is prevalent in all the various genres of podcasting, especially with the 
podcasts that just take a look at life and give it a perspective. Sometimes you need 
humor to deal with loved ones, life’s unexpected pitfalls, or just the world as a 
whole. Slice-of-life podcasts offers audiences a chance to laugh at the headlines, 
to slow down with an in-depth look at a specific topic, or to look at a lifestyle. The 
subjects covered are varied, but the podcast serves as a window into the hosts’ 
world. True, most podcasts are; but in these podcasts, it’s about the moment, the 
here and now, and what is happening in their lives. From this casual and candid 316 PART 6 The Part of Tens
approach comes a podcast that might make you laugh, might make you cry, and 
just might make you look at something with a whole new perspective.
» C’est La Me (https://pfdbook.link/cestlame): Hosted by Amelia Rose 
Zimlich, C’est La Me (This Is Me) offers itself as that friend who is always there 
to celebrate your victories, listen to your gripes, and help you improve. As you 
listen, have your notepad ready. Amelia has insightful questions for you to 
answer on your own.
» The Way I Heard It with Mike Rowe (https://pfdbook.link/twihi): Mike 
Rowe, best known for his snarky-but-sincere approach to America’s working 
force in Dirty Jobs, brings all his dry wit and endless charm to The Way I Heard 
It. This podcast tells new and slightly unexpected stories of celebrities, history, 
and icons of culture, passed on from person to person, generation to 
generation. It’s a quick listen — usually under ten minutes per episode — 
and is guaranteed to make you smile.
» The Creative Solutions Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/creativesolutions/):
Produced by author, speaker, and entertainer Izolda Trakhtenberg, this podcast is 
a weekly talk show with business leaders, entrepreneurs, and creatives on tapping 
into innovation and problem-solving strategies through creativity. You learn how 
the show’s guests discover solutions by thinking outside the box. The Creative 
Solutions Podcast, shown in Figure 18-3, emphasizes the importance of creativity 
to solve the big problems we face in the world.

Gaming Podcasts
You might think that podcasts about games and gaming would be better suited for 
the slice-of-life, geek, or comedy category. But long-form and serialized produc￾tions of friends gathering to crawl through a dungeon, claim the title of King of 
Tokyo, or share strategies and the latest news from World of Warcraft or Minecraft
stand in a class all their own. Gaming podcasts are quite popular, and the skill 
levels featured on these podcasts range from the professionals appearing on the 
circuit to the novices purely in it for the fun:
» What’s Good Games (https://pfdbook.link/whatsgood): Join Andrea 
Rene, Brittney Brombacher, and Riana Manuel-Peña as they analyze the 
week’s video game news, give hands-on impressions of upcoming titles, 
discuss your questions, and have a little bit of off-topic conversation. With 
over 30 years of video game industry experience, the conversation is sure 
to be passionate but never takes itself too seriously.
» Esports Insider Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/esi): This podcast site has 
the world of esports covered with two different brands in one feed. Esports 
Today offers short digests from the world of esports news, and The Industry
podcast brings you longer conversations with industry notables and key 
stakeholders. If you’re curious about what sponsors look for in funding an 
esports event or what effect virtual reality will have on esports, you’ll enjoy 
Esports Insider Podcast.
» The Steamrollers Adventure Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/tsap):
Michael J. Rigg loves to write. Michael J. Rigg loves role-playing games. Michael 
J. Rigg loves steampunk. When Mike wrote his debut novel, Clockwork Looking 
Glass, he wanted to delve even deeper into his world but also invite his friends 
along for the ride. This inspired Mike to design a d20-style role-playing game 
around his steampunk universe. The Steamrollers Adventure Podcast (shown 
in Figure 18-4) is an immersive RPG experience for its audience because 
Mike provides music and sound effects associated with events in-game. 
“Storycrafter Mike” keeps the action going as he pulls his friends and 
special guests deeper into a wonderland of his own making, with a lot 
of inappropriate humor peppered throughout.318 PART 6 The Part of Tens
» Critical Role (https://pfdbook.link/critrole): This podcast began as 
many role-playing campaigns did, with a bunch of nerdy friends playing 
Dungeons & Dragons in each other’s living rooms. Critical Role is now the 
must-listen-to RPG podcast, attracting over a half million viewers every week. 
With dreamy Matthew Mercer as your dungeon master in their third cam￾paign storyline, popular voiceover actors and special guests take on impossi￾ble challenges while enjoying a few laughs along the way.
Podcasts of the Pen
Tee stepped into podcasting with an idea no author had set out to do: serialize a 
novel in podcasts. When the podcast of MOREVI ended for Tee in the summer of 
2005, he looked at the audio equipment he had and asked himself, “So . . . what do 
I do now?” By this time, a few writerly podcasts were out there talking about the 
craft of writing, but there were no active podcasts on the business of writing books. 
To fill this void, Tee launched The Survival Guide to Writing Fantasy in September 
2005, a podcast about the everyday operations of a writing career.
The last episode of The Survival Guide to Writing Fantasy aired in 2009, but many 
more podcasts have appeared that cover all aspects of writing from the business

» The Creative Penn Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/tcp): New York Times

into all aspects of writing. Business. Creative demands. Time management. If

creative project, The Creative Penn may be your first stop.

Hugo-winning podcast featuring seven professional authors and a huge 
collection of interviews, advice, and back-and-forth banter, Writing Excuses is a

minutes long because you’re in a hurry and we’re not that smart,” but if you

working on his next work-in-progress, Dan hosts The Everyday Novelist, a daily 
writing podcast about taking your writing passion to the next level. Within a 
few minutes, The Everyday Novelist tackles a new topic presented by Dan, a 
special guest, or his listeners. It’s a podcast that serves as your daily affirma￾tion for what you want to accomplish as a writer.
Geek Podcasts
If you’ve ever attended a Comic Con or a science-fiction-fantasy-horror conven￾tion, you may have witnessed firsthand passionate individuals who love to talk 
about their favorite movie, television show, or game. Or maybe you caught a live 
interview with an actress or writer that you love, and heard that Nathan Fillion is 
a comic book nerd or that Erin Grey is big into Tai Chi. Truly one of the joys of 
podcasting is capturing good laughs between friends, luminaries, and a room full 
of fellow geeks attending a con and riffing on the mics.
This is the joy you hear in these podcasts. A love and a passion for all things geek. 
(There it is again! The P word — passion.)320 PART 6 The Part of Tens
SOMETIMES, OPPORTUNITY PRESENTS 
ITSELF. TAKE IT!
The photo shows Tee Morris and Jack Mangan interviewing Battlestar Galactica’s Richard 
Hatch in February 2006 at Farpoint, a science fiction convention in Baltimore, Maryland. 
You’ll notice that Richard is wearing a coat. He wasn’t cold. He had a plane to catch, but 
he made time before leaving to sit down and talk on the mic about his experience 
between the BSG of the 1970s and of the 2000s. With Battlestar Galactica being a hot 
property once again, it was a real treat for Tee and Jack, two fans of both renditions of 
the epic space opera, for Richard to give them time, but how did they do it? How did two 
podcasters — at a time when podcasting was still a new media — land an interview with 
one of BSG’s regular players?
Simple: Tee asked.
We go into more detail on approaching people about interviews in Chapter 6, but some￾times opportunities present themselves in the moment. Nothing is wrong with asking 
celebrities, subject matter experts, and special guests relevant to your podcast if they 
have a few minutes to talk. The worst thing a potential guest could say to you is “No.” 
(And if they do, they’re not being jerks. They have somewhere to be or a pocket of time 
to get something to eat.) But if they say “yes,” be ready to go with solid, intelligent 
questions.
Then let the magic happen.CHAPTER 18 Ten Types of Podcasts to Check Out 321
» Geek Radio Daily (https://pfdbook.link/grd): Geek Radio Daily featuring 
the Wonderful Billy Flynn, Podcasting’s Rich Sigfrit, and the Flynstress provides 
a dose of geek every weekday. Monday highlights the weekend box office 
results, Tuesday covers what’s out on Blu-Ray, Wednesday is new comic book 
day, Thursday is all about new video game releases, and Friday offers up 
what’s opening at theatres everywhere. On alternate weekends, as a bonus, 
Carol the Cat hosts a review of geeky horror movies as part of her Scream 
Queens Survival Manual. Then there’s the GRD’s Weekly super-sized show — in 
many cases, recorded live at a convention — featuring special guests and 
in-depth discussions on how to get your geek on.
» We Were Told We Should Do a Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/
wwtwsdap): Sometimes it’s fun to get insights from other parts of the world. 
We Were Told We Should Do a Podcast comes from New Zealand and is 
available both in audio and video. Join Rowan, Alan, and Adam as they blend 
their nerdy obsessions with infectious banter. They speak to the realms of 
pop culture and gaming, with a sprinkle of personal revelations and whatever 
random topics enter their brains. Expect laughs, deep chats, and a reminder 
that it’s perfectly cool to embrace your inner geek.
» Wired Magazine’s Geek’s Guide to the Galaxy (https://pfdbook.link/
ggttg): Geek’s Guide to the Galaxy, hosted by author David Barr Kirtley and 
produced by Lightspeed Magazine editor John Joseph Adams, is an unabashed 
embracing of your inner geek. Geek’s Guide features in-depth conversations 
and interviews about books, movies, games, and comics, all coming from a 
place of science-fiction, fantasy, horror, and a love for the genre.
Podcasts about . . . Podcasting
It may sound redundant and feel a little odd to listen to a podcast about podcast￾ing. It would be like having an electrician come over to another electrician’s house 
to fix faulty electric wiring. But why not?
» The Feed (https://pfdbook.link/thefeed): When it comes to podcast tutors, 
you want to work with people who have trustworthy track records (and thank you 
for trusting us!). And when it comes to trustworthy track records, Elsie Escobar, 
Rob Walch, and LibSyn are responsible for many podcasts available today. The 
Feed (shown in Figure 18-5) is LibSyn’s official podcast, covering podcast strategy, 
tips, media hosting, and all things LibSyn. (See Chapter 3 for more.) The Feed is a 
community-driven show, so their topics go beyond getting a podcast off the 
ground. Rob and Elsie go deep into ways of increasing your audience, promotion 
that won’t come across spammy, and crafting a strategy for your podcast, 
whether it’s a casual endeavor or a professional venture.322 PART 6 The Part of Tens
» The Audacity to Podcast (https://pfdbook.link/tatp): Podcaster Daniel 
J. Lewis is your host for The Audacity to Podcast, an award-winning podcast all 
about podcasting using Audacity and WordPress. Along with those founda￾tions, Dan recommends audio gear, suggests good podcast habits, and 
reminds you of the reasons why you podcast in the first place.
» She Podcasts (https://pfdbook.link/shepodcasts): The vision for She 
Podcasts is to support and nurture as many female-lead podcasts as possible 
and to encourage knowledge sharing without excessive promotion. Through 
the podcast, group coaching, workshops, webinars, and other educational 
materials, Elsie Escobar (yes, Elsie from The Feed) and Jessica Kupferman hope 
to grow the number of successful woman-hosted podcasts, thereby giving 
light and voice to the millions of messages that need to be shared with 
the world.
» Better Podcasting (https://pfdbook.link/betterpodcasting): Like other 
podcasts about podcasting, Better Podcasting offers hobbyist podcasters great 
information about improving their show. Stephen Jondrew and SP Rupert 
originally hosted three different shows — Better Podcasting, Better Podcasting 
Live, and Better Podcasting Downloads — but restructured their lineup in early 
2024. After doing a careful evaluation of their current content, time available, 
and listener feedback, they combined two formats into one show and tempo￾rarily parked the third. As they changed, the listeners have gone along for the 
ride and learned from the experience. Well done gentlemen, well done!

Chapter 19
Ten Original Podcasters
Most readers are likely new to podcasting and missed out on the trials and 
tribulations that went along with first huge growth spurt between 2004 
and 2005. It was truly undiscovered country not only for Chuck and Tee 
but also for the people listed in this chapter, recognized as some of the original 
voices who have stood the test of time. While some of these OPs (original podcast￾ers) may not still have their original shows, they continue to produce content. (We 
regret that we couldn’t include in this chapter all the other talented OPs, and we 
want to acknowledge their hard work, persistence, and content, which continues 
to find subscriptions in podcast client apps everywhere.)
As you read these names, try to remember that these are regular people like the 
rest of us. True, some of them may seem larger than life, but all of them put their 
pants on one leg at a time — unless they’re wearing kilts.
A future podcast star would do well to listen to the wisdom, information, and 
rants put forth by this group. Often controversial and usually informative, each 
has a unique outlook on the world of podcasting. We’ve found them to be helpful 
guides in our own podcasting careers.
IN THIS CHAPTER
» Discovering the humble beginnings of 
some experienced podcasters
» Sharing tips for those just starting
» Gaining insights into why these 
original podcasters keep podcasting324 PART 6 The Part of Tens
Mignon Fogarty
Mignon, better known as “Grammar Girl” to her listeners, is a master. Her deliv￾ery and experience are why Chuck and Tee asked her to kick off the third edition 
of Podcasting For Dummies. She has taken the topic of English grammar, something 
most would find mundane — and many think they have mastered only to find 
they learn something new in every episode — and turned it in to a podcast empire.
Trivia time! What was Mignon Fogarty’s first podcast? If you said Grammar Girl,
you ’ve chosen poorly. She had a podcast in 2005 called Absolute Science co-hosted 
with Adam Lowe. That show ran for about eight months and taught her some val￾uable lessons.
Mignon’s breakout show, Grammar Girl’s Quick and Dirty Tips for Better Writing
(https://pfdbook.link/grammargirl), started in 2006 and received numerous 
acclaims and recognition, such as reaching #2 on iTunes’s most recommended 
podcasts list. As she describes it, “It will help you improve your writing and 
delight you with the quirks of language.” Her grammar show has since turned into 
a Quick and Dirty Tips network featuring topics such as productivity by the “Get-It￾Done Guy,” Steve Robbins; money and finance by the “Money Girl,” Laura Adams; 
and even parenting by the “Mighty Mommy,” Cheryl Butler. Mignon even got her 
old co-host, Adam Lowe from Absolute Science, back to be the original “Modern 
Manners Guy.” She’s been featured in the Wall Street Journal and on The Oprah Win￾frey Show and The Today Show. Twice.
Like many podcasters, Mignon’s audience is not contained to the United States — 
take that, terrestrial radio! She receives feedback from all over the globe, again not 
unusual but always appreciated. Many of the comments are from teachers using 
her episodes to teach English as a second, third, or fourth, language.
Her podcast gave birth to the book Grammar Girl’s Quick and Dirty Tips for Better 
Writing (Holt) in 2008, which reached number 9 on the New York Times Bestseller 
List. In 2020, her Grammar Girl Presents the Ultimate Writing Guide for Students was 
Macmillan’s No. 1 bestselling nonfiction book at Barnes & Noble, and in 2023 the 
Grammar Girl podcast celebrated 18 years of podcasting with close to 1,000 
episodes produced.
When asked why she continues to podcast, the unsurprising answer is, “I really 
love it. I get to learn new things along with the listeners, and it’s always great 
when I hear how I helped someone. That happens almost every week. What could 
be better than that?” Mignon Fogarty is one of the lucky few who get to earn a 
living by podcasting.CHAPTER 19 Ten Original Podcasters 325
Adam Curry
In 1987, your authors were finishing school and MTV (the icon of the generation 
that remembers when they played music videos) hired a new VJ named Adam 
Curry. Decades later, despite life experiences, diplomas, and other strange changes 
and artistic ventures, it’s still baffling to think that the same guy who was intro￾ducing the latest Madonna video is now referred to as “the Podfather.”
Adam Curry’s importance to the craft might be the single best example to illus￾trate the just-do-it nature of podcasting. It was Adam Curry, not some highly 
trained programmer, who hacked together the first podcatching client program to 
harness the power of the recently created enclosed-media files in RSS 2.0 feeds — 
and made them downloadable during the computer’s off hours.
Since that time, Adam has become the most recognized (and arguably the most 
popular) podcaster around. While his first podcast, Daily Source Code, has pod￾faded, Curry continues to produce No Agenda (https://pfdbook.link/noagenda), 
co-hosted with John C.  Dvorak. No Agenda listeners never know quite what to 
expect: a tour of Adam’s kitchen, the latest round of talks in a business venture, 
or a discussion with a content partner that could change the shape of podcasting. 
Everything and anything is possible.
Mur Lafferty
Mur Lafferty (https://pfdbook.link/murlafferty) or “The Grand Dame of 
Podcasting,” as we refer to her with great affection, began her podcasting career 
with Geek Fu Action Grip, a show that was an ongoing commentary about what was 
going on in her life, what games she was playing, and what bands she was listen￾ing to. Each episode of Geek Fu ended with an original essay regarding something 
that struck her funny bone. This podcast spawned I Should Be Writing, the 2007 
Parsec-winning podcast documenting her own tests and trials in getting her first 
novel published. As proof of her talent, she has been a finalist or a Parsec Award 
winner in 2008, 2010, and 2011. She was a finalist for a Hugo Award in 2012 and 
won a Hugo award in the category of John W. Campbell award for Best New Writer 
of 2013. In 2018 she was again nominated for a Hugo Award for Best Semiprozine 
as editor of Escape Pod.
Keeping many of her podcasts to the basics, Mur produces quality content from 
both sides of the brain: the analytical (I Should Be Writing) and the creative (The 
Heaven series, Playing for Keeps). Mur raises the bar for other creative podcasters, 
inspiring many to podcast their own fiction and even return to a writing passion 
they had stepped away from for many years.326 PART 6 The Part of Tens
It’s hard to listen to podcasts and not hear the name Mur Lafferty. It’s harder still 
to subscribe to any Mur Lafferty podcast and not be completely taken by her. Her 
personality shines in every episode, with every work, and with every show.
Steve Boyett
Like many featured in this chapter, Steve Boyett’s background was not in radio, 
broadcasting, audio engineering, or even computers. Instead, Steve’s early inter￾ests were in writing; he boasts several books, short stories, and novellas to his 
name. Go look him up at https://pfdbook.link/steveboyett! Several years 
ago — okay, maybe more than several — he started composing electronic music 
for fun and began DJing at parties, which he refers to as “straight-up crack.”
Almost like the proverbial accidental invention, Steve stumbled on to his fate. 
“Because I’m a writer, I was considering a fiction or interview podcast. Doing a 
music podcast hadn’t occurred to me.” On a whim, he asked his girlfriend, an 
aerobics instructor and runner, if she thought anyone would be interested in some 
of the mixes he was producing. Her response was something like this: “Are you 
kidding? Do you know how much I used to pay for this as an aerobics teacher? It’s 
perfect!” So he launched Podrunner (https://pfdbook.link/podrunner), a regu￾lar, hour-long dose of fixed-tempo music for walkers, joggers, and runners, from 
beginner to competitor. There were plenty of music podcasts at the time, but Steve 
was the first to do a music series specifically for workouts.
At first he thought he might attract 50 people or so. Talk about underestimating 
your audience! iTunes featured his podcast a few days after it was listed, and his 
website crashed within days of Podrunner’s debut. It became so popular that it shot 
up and stayed in the Top 10 for about a year. That’s when he gave some serious 
thought to legitimize it or kill it. Thankfully, he chose to invest and continue.
At its height, Podrunner was commanding over 600,000 downloads a month, 
receiving sponsorship from Timex and the United States Navy. The podcast still 
produces new and original mixes, but for Steve, the benefits do not stop at the 
success of his BPS-bending compositions. “I have met people that I wouldn’t have 
otherwise met. A listener reached out to me and asked me if I’d be interested in 
DJing for a group of fire dancers at the annual Burning Man Festival, where they 
had sound-activated flame cannons, a 30-foot stage, and on and on. I said, ‘You 
had me at sound-activated flame cannons.’”
But the biggest reward he receives is from hearing how he helped people. Almost 
every week he gets a note about someone who lost 100 pounds thanks to his music, 
or a chemotherapy patient who said, “This got me out of my house and on the way CHAPTER 19 Ten Original Podcasters 327
to recovery.” And since the first edition of Podcasting For Dummies, Tee still takes 
Podrunner with him on 5K runs, 10K runs, and the odd half-marathon. Steve con￾tinues to make the finish line appear that much sooner for Tee.
When asked why he continues to podcast after all this time, at first Steve jokingly 
said, “I’m in complete denial about its influence on my life,” but later admitted, 
“It’s what I do. The thought of giving it up really bothers me, and when I do think 
about it, I think of those listeners whom I’ve helped.”
Adam Christianson
All superheroes have their origin story. Adam Christianson, the Clark Kent of Pod￾casting, is really the Superman who brought us Maccast (https://pfdbook.link/
maccast) for over 18 years. His story starts with a degree in printing. (That’s right, 
kids, the paper-and-ink kind of printing that came before the internet.) Adam has 
always nurtured an interest in Apple computers, dating to their introduction, and 
it just so happens that’s what his printing company was using. He became the de 
facto — and eventually official — IT guy. In the mid-90s, he discovered HTML 
and found himself interested in web development while learning all he could 
about Macs.
In late 2004, he was listening to some of the early shows and thought, “I need to 
find a Mac podcast to help pass the time on my hour-long.” Sadly, he found out 
that there were no Mac podcasts at the time.
(Insert screaming sound effects here.)
Inspired by The Dawn & Drew Show (https://pfdbook.link/dawnanddrew), Adam 
recognized how easy podcasting could be when he discovered that his favorite 
podcast was powered by a Logitech USB microphone and GarageBand. Adam 
thought, “I’ve got GarageBand — I’m halfway there!” On December 13, 2004, the 
Maccast was revealed to the world. As he states at the beginning of each episode, 
this was “the show for Mac geeks, by Mac geeks.”
That last part, “by Mac geeks,” is important to note because his intent was to cre￾ate a five- to ten-minute daily news show about all things Mac. Remember, this 
was before the days of the iPhone and iPad. Macintosh computers and early iPods 
were about all Apple had going at the time! What amazed Adam was that after his 
first episode, a listener emailed him with a technical question. This may not seem 
too crazy today, but in those days he wondered, “How did this guy even find my 
show?!” Back then, you generally found new shows by word of mouth — other 
podcasters or listeners mentioning you. It was as if he sent a message in a bottle 
and got a response back from across the ocean.328 PART 6 The Part of Tens
Later, Adam expanded the stories he covered to include a wider range of Apple 
products, but he still kept the name and tag line the same — mostly by listener 
request. The audience had also grown considerably, allowing Adam to be his own 
boss, splitting time between podcasting and web development — with the bulk of 
revenue coming from Maccast.
After talking to Adam, one quickly gets a sense of his humility. He’s not concerned 
with being perceived as the authority on Macs or having millions of listeners. His 
main motivation for doing the show is the sense of community and the feedback 
it generates. He considers Maccast a global user group where he does just as much 
learning as educating.
In December 2023, Adam retired his one-man Mac show to focus on family, but he 
hasn’t disappeared completely. As he was planning to retire Maccast, he was asked 
to be a third co-host on the Mac Geek Gab podcast (https://pfdbook.link/
macgeekgab). Keep up the great work, Mac of Steel!
Dave Slusher
Dave Slusher is one of those lucky individuals who happened to be at the right 
place at the right time. As he describes it, “I started podcasting partly out of con￾venience and dumb luck.” In 2004, he had been following the latest advancements 
of transferring and listening to audio files on the internet, but it wasn’t until 
Adam Curry’s first podcatching client written in AppleScript that things started to 
heat up for him. “It was only a matter of time before someone put it all together. 
The pieces were all there and sooner or later it was going to happen,” says Slusher. 
Once that happened, Dave got out the gear from his former radio gig and started 
Evil Genius Chronicles (https://pfdbook.link/egc) in late August 2004 — one day 
after Adam Curry started his Daily Source Code — which is now one of the three 
longest continually running podcasts.
When asked to describe his show, Dave’s response was, “It’s about music, tech￾nology, and culture — but I know that doesn’t sound too compelling.” Dave may 
not be the best at marketing with a description like that, but if you give a listen, 
you’ll realize his personality and perspectives on topics are what make Evil Genius 
Chronicles work. He conveys situations that affect him, digs for the deeper mean￾ing, and articulates it in a way that matters to the listener.
Another interesting aspect about EGC is that you never know what you’re going to 
get in each episode. Dave seems to fly in the face of convention and make the most 
of it. Remember when we advised you (in another chapter) to not shake up your 
show format too much or you’ll confuse your listeners? Well, let’s just say Dave CHAPTER 19 Ten Original Podcasters 329
has a four-letter response to that, but it may not work for everyone. One episode 
may be a combination of music and monologue, the next could be an interview 
with a comic book artist, and another could be a panel discussion with friends. It’s 
how he rolls and how his podcast thrives.
When asked why he continues to podcast, he said, “I can do whatever I want 
unfettered. My show, my format, my content. I’ll keep doing it while it’s still fun. 
Besides, as long as I continue, I still have the status of one of the longest running 
podcasts. When I stop, I’ll just become a footnote to history  — and nobody 
wants that.”
Scott Sigler
When it comes to the early days of podcasting fiction, Tee has been described as 
epic classical music like Wagner. Mark Jeffrey could be compared to Dave 
Brubeck’s style of jazz music. Then you have Scott Sigler (https://pfdbook.
link/scottsigler). He’s Metallica. The Black Album.
“I podcast because it’s the perfect vehicle for serialized fiction,” Sigler says of his 
decision to podcast his debut novel, Earthcore. He seeks not only to hook listeners 
with the format but also to keep them in suspense until next week’s episode: “My 
novels are long, with cliffhangers at the end of each chapter. That’s designed to 
keep you turning the page. With podcasts, you just have to wait for the next 
episode.”
Scott’s nostalgia for radio-style serials is not the only motivation behind his pod￾casts. “The amazing reaction I get from the listeners is the motivation,” he adds, 
acknowledging his loyal fan base, also known as Junkies. The fans showed Scott 
(along with print publishers Dragon Moon Press and Crown Publishing) how 
hooked they were when the podcast of Ancestor premiered in print. The book shot 
up Amazon charts to hit #1 in both Horror and Science Fiction. That was enough 
for Crown to approach Scott with a contract for another one of his podcasts, Infec￾tion (which the publisher retitled as Infected).
Scott has also achieved a spot on the coveted New York Times Bestseller List. He 
continues to deliver high-octane fiction crossing many genres, one podcast at a 
time, as part of Empty Set Entertainment. “To know that my fiction has enter￾tained thousands of people, helped them escape from their day-to-day lives, and 
just plain have some fun means the world to me. Is it a power trip? Sure! But 
knowing that I’ve delivered a great story and entertained someone is a fantastic 
feeling.”330 PART 6 The Part of Tens
Michael Butler
Original — that’s the word that comes to mind when talking to Michael Butler, 
host of the Rock and Roll Geek Show (https://pfdbook.link/rnrgeek). Before 
meeting Michael, you might have visions of a hard rockin’, loud-mouthed, swag￾gering, crazy man, only to find he’s a humble down-to-earth guy. He states, “I 
don’t listen to any other music podcasts because I don’t want to be influenced by 
them. I’d rather just do my own thing.”
Michael earns his place in this chapter because he’s somewhere in the first ten or 
so podcasters who immediately followed Adam Curry. He started in early 
September 2004 and hasn’t stopped since. At the time, he was playing in a band 
called American Heartbreak and running a blog for them. He came across Adam 
Curry’s audio blog and was inspired by what Adam was doing. Like many 
podcasters just dipping their toes in, he started with modest technology, talking 
into the built-in microphone on his laptop. Shortly afterward, he upgraded to a 
USB microphone and eventually got a mixer and a bit better recording equipment.
Michael describes his show as “A guy talking about music, playing music, review￾ing albums, and doing interviews.” Michael is very well known in the podosphere 
not because of the popularity of his show but because of his humbleness and quick 
wit. In fact, contrary to his on-air personality, he’s a quiet and shy person, often 
stating when he’s at social events, “I’m the quiet guy in the corner.” Get to know 
Michael Butler and you discover his resume includes working full time for Adam 
Curry and collaborations with many other podcasters, including Dave Slusher.
Despite the popularity of the Rock and Roll Geek Show, Michael doesn’t care about 
the numbers. In fact, he states, “My fans are probably some of the most loyal I 
have ever encountered.” Hardly an episode goes by where he doesn’t do a segment 
called “Opening Butler’s Mail” to find anything from cash to special hunting 
arrows that Ted Nugent uses. That’s some serious fandom! He continues to pod￾cast largely because of the fans and “It’s just fun to do.”
Rock on, Mr. Michael Butler! Rock on!
Dr. Pamela Gay
Let’s just go on record here — we think science is cool. Like Mignon Fogarty, 
Dr. Gay brought academics to the podcasting world with the right blend of educa￾tion and entertainment. Also like Grammar Girl, Dr. Gay’s hugely successful show 
Astronomy Cast (https://pfdbook.link/astronomycast) was not her first dip in CHAPTER 19 Ten Original Podcasters 331
the podpool. The birth of Pamela’s podcast universe started when she was on the 
way to a meeting of the American Astronomical Society in early 2005 and her 
friend Aaron Price said he was reading about this new thing called podcasting. He 
told her about some evangelicals doing a show to convert people, to which she 
replied, “We can convert people to science!” Within a week they had the first epi￾sode of Slacker Astronomy online. The show ran for about 18 months until life 
intervened, taking the show’s hosts in different directions.
Not one to let her podcasting passion sit idle, she quickly connected with colleague 
Fraser Cain and started Astronomy Cast, “A weekly facts-based journey through the 
cosmos where we help you understand not only what we know, but how we know 
what we know.” Great tag line, by the way!
Astronomy Cast, and its cousin podcast, 365 Days of Astronomy, have been finalists or 
winners of Parsec Awards from 2007 to 2012, and now Dr. Gay can be seen on 
occasion in episodes of The Universe explaining such topics as how the universe 
ends. She has also been a speaker at science and sci-fi conventions. Both on her 
podcast and in real life, Dr. Gay does not shy away from being a fan as well. “I was 
talking to Richard Hatch, the actor who played Apollo on the 1970s series Battlestar 
Gallactica, about the meteor that struck the Earth’s atmosphere over Chelyabinsk, 
Russia in 2013,” she recalls. “I was happy to share with him his intersection 
between science and science fiction. The meteor’s origins were from an Apollo 
asteroid.”
And she’s still not finished trying new things. In 2024, she’s collaborating with 
Tee on Spectres & Science. Tee brings his paranormal pursuits to the same table 
where Pamela offers science, and they find an unexpected common ground. 
“Here’s where the fun begins,” as Han Solo puts it so eloquently.
The “Dr.” in her title is her PhD in Astronomy from the University of Texas, so 
yes, she knows what she’s talking about. What’s even better is that her passion 
makes physics fun and interesting to listen to.
Brian Ibbott
We put Brian Ibbott in this list for two reasons: He’s one of the original music 
podcasters, and you might be thinking of starting your own music podcast. (Hey, 
lots of us wanted to be DJs when we were kids.) “It’s weird hearing my name 
associated with the word ‘original,’” jokes Ibbott. More importantly, Brian is a 
music podcaster who is doing it right — the legal way.332 PART 6 The Part of Tens
As we mention in Chapter 5, podcasting licensed music legally is a challenge. With 
his podcast Coverville (https://pfdbook.link/coverville), Brian works directly 
with ASCAP, BMI, musicians, and other organizations that hold the rights to 
major-label music and songs. Although many music podcasters are just a process 
server away from a major lawsuit, Brian is sitting pretty.
Brian also serves as an example of how to create a niche podcast out of the music 
you love and fill a void not covered by traditional outlets. Brian has a passion for 
cover songs and has built a show completely around songs that fit that bill. “I 
wanted to create a radio show that I always wanted to listen to,” he states. He took 
this concept a step further by featuring groups of cover songs sharing a common 
thread, thereby producing themed episodes of his podcast. He’s even started his 
own record label! Simply brilliant — we don’t know of any radio stations or pro￾grams that provide this service.
Brian also has a charitable heart. Each year on the last Friday in November (known 
as Black Friday to many) he does a 24-hour live-streaming marathon of cover 
songs, known simply as Cover-thon, to raise money for Alzheimer’s research.
And Coverville is still producing episodes to this day. What a legend, this guy!
Before you rush out to make the next hot music podcast, consider the legal rami￾fications. And while you’re pondering that, also ask whether your future podcast 
sounds like the same stuff people can listen to over their radios. Follow Brian’s 
lead and give the world something different with your podcast.Index 333

zooming on screens, 297About the Authors
Tee Morris is a communications and AI strategist based out of the Washington, 
D.C./Virginia/Maryland metro area and an award-winning author of science￾fiction and fantasy. Tee’s 2002 historical epic fantasy, MOREVI: The Chronicles of 
Rafe & Askana, was the first novel to be podcast from cover to cover and led to his 
teaming up with podcaster Evo Terra on the original Podcasting For Dummies in 
2005. Since then, Tee has written other social media titles, including Twitch For 
Dummies, Discord For Dummies, and YouTube for Dummies Jr. Alongside his wife, Pip 
Ballantine, they worked together on The Ministry of Peculiar Occurrences, a steam￾punk series that won several Parsec awards as well as numerous literary awards, 
including RT Reviewer’s Choice for Best Steampunk of 2014. Tee can be found 
in the YouTube podcast directory with Phil Rossi in Old Spirits, a series featuring 
two dads living their best life while investigating the afterlife. Find out more about 
Tee Morris at https://pfdbook.link/tee and at https://pfdbook.link/mopo.
Chuck Tomasi is among the early podcast pioneers who started in 2004. He has 
produced over 1,000 episodes across multiple shows, the longest running being 
Technorama, a light-hearted geek show that he co-hosts with Kreg Steppe. In 2011, 
Technorama took home a Parsec award for best comedy/parody. He also co-hosts 
The Topic is Trek with Kreg and podcast veteran Clinton Alvord. Chuck will tell you 
he’s very fortunate to be able to take the skills, creativity, and passion of content 
creation and combine them with his technical acumen to deliver value to his 
employer. He’s currently a senior developer advocate (aka software influencer) for 
ServiceNow, a software company providing a cloud-based enterprise workflow 
platform. Since 2013, he has been the host and driving force behind TechNow, the 
most successful ServiceNow web series, and Break Point, a ServiceNow developer 
podcast. He has also hosted or produced hundreds of developer-based audio and 
video productions. Chuck has also emceed several developer events, including 
ServiceNow’s CreatorCon. When people talk about podcasting with passion, 
Chuck’s enthusiasm comes to mind. Currently residing in Phoenix, Arizona, he’s 
a devoted husband and proud father of two beautiful girls.
Learn more about Chuck at his website: https://pfdbook.link/chuck.
Authors’ Acknowledgments
Due to the complexity of the issue and the incredible growth in the community, it 
would be impossible to properly express our thanks to all the parties who were of 
great help with this book. So with that . . .To our wives, Donna and Pip: Thanks for not strangling us for our constant “Oh! 
We’ve got to add that to the book!” and “Come on, honey, we have to record this 
for the book!” moments. We deeply appreciate the averted gazes of death when we 
answered that no, unfortunately, we would not be coming to bed. No, we forgot to 
scoop the litter boxes because we had one more chapter to write. Yes, we did 
realize it was 3 o’clock in the morning.
To the podcasters who provided not only inspiration but also camaraderie and 
friendship along the way. Through listening to you all and talking to many, you 
served as a constant reminder of why we were pouring our hearts and souls into 
this text.
A huge thank you to Rob Walch, vice president of podcaster relations at LibSyn and 
host of the podcasts The Feed and Podcast 411. Not only did you keep us in the loop 
concerning recent changes and updates at LibSyn, but you continue to be a 
constant supporter of the podcasting community. You are a friend and an ally we 
can count on.
Finally, a special nod to Michael R. Mennenga for passing along an email back in 
2004 that opened a door to a world of time-shifting, kick-ass mystic ninjas, 
podiobooks, and science-fiction and fantasy geeks around the world interested 
in what we have to offer.Publisher’s Acknowledgments
